Refutation of the Boundary Between Light and Darkness
Maturidi refutes dualist boundary with logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi's logical dilemma refutes the dualist claim of a boundary between light and darkness.

Core Idea
If light and darkness meet, they must be finite or temporal.

Maturidi presents a powerful logical dilemma to dismantle the dualist concept of an eternal boundary between light and darkness. He forces the dualist into a contradiction: either light and darkness are completely separate (implying both are temporal) or they are touching (which requires mixing and thus finitude). This lesson walks through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist claim
The dualists claim that light and darkness meet at a boundary (حد) and that both are eternal. Maturidi begins by questioning the nature of their meeting.
Think about what 'meeting' implies: are they in contact or not?
2
Follow the first horn: separate in eternity
If they were not touching, then any coming-to-be of a part would imply the whole is temporal. This contradicts the dualists' claim of eternality.
The reasoning uses analogy from the witnessed to the absent.
3
Follow the second horn: touching in eternity
If they were touching, mixing requires one to penetrate the other. This leads to either an increase or a cutting, which implies finitude. If they were infinite, penetration would be impossible.
Consider the physical impossibility: a dense substance cannot penetrate a subtle one without contradiction.
4
Conclude the refutation
Maturidi concludes that the dualist model collapses: either the boundary implies temporality or finitude. The light would also acquire evil if mixing occurred. The argument shows the logical flaws in the dualist position.
This passage is a classic example of using logical dilemmas in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إثباتهم الحد من حيث الالتقاء، إما إن كانا متماسين في الأزل أو غير متماسين، فإن كانا متباينين قال: إن تماما حدثا، فحدث الجزء يوجب الكل بحق الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب، وإن كانا متماسين فلا بد من أن أحدهمها حتى يمتزج بالآخر أو يجيد من الآخر حتى يدخل في نفسه، وأيهما كان ففيه زيادة لم يكن أو قطع وإدخال في جوهر، فيبطل القول بأنه غير متناه؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن لأجزائه تناه لم يكن للآخر فيه تداخل ليمتزج به، ثبت أنه متناه إذا احتمل الامتزاج، مع البعد أن تبقى الظلمة كثافتها على النور مع رقته فيقتطع منها؛ إذ كل ممتلى بما يلطف من الأشياء لا يتمكن فيه ما يكثف ولو كان ذلك من النور فقد اكتسب الشر وألقى نفسه في الحبس مع ثبات الكيف جوهرا واحدا، وإنما بحد اللطف المنفذ فى الكثيف إذا كان من جواهر مختلفة يبقى بينها الفرج، وأما الذى سبيله ما ذكر فلا، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then their establishment of the limit from the point of meeting: either they were touching in pre-eternity or not touching. If they were distinct, [they] said: that both came to be, so the coming to be of the part necessitates the whole by the right of inference from the witnessed to the absent. And if they were touching, then it is necessary that one of them [acts] until it mixes with the other or it pulls from the other until it enters into itself. Whichever it is, there is in it an increase that was not there or a cutting and insertion into an essence. So nullifies the statement that it is infinite; because if its parts have no end, the other cannot penetrate it to mix with it. It is established that it is finite if it admits mixing. Moreover, it is far-fetched that darkness preserves its density over light while light is subtle, so [darkness] is cut off from it; for everything filled with subtle things cannot have something dense enter it. And if that were from light, then light would have acquired evil and thrown itself into imprisonment while the quality remains one essence. And it is only by the limit of subtlety penetrating the dense, if they are of different essences, there remains gaps between them. But that whose way is as mentioned is not [so]. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 759

Commentary
This passage refutes the dualists' claim of a boundary (حد) where light and darkness meet. Maturidi presents a dilemma: either they were touching from eternity or not. If they were distinct (not touching), then any coming-to-be of parts implies a temporal beginning, which they try to avoid. If they were touching, then mixing requires one to penetrate the other, which either increases the essence or cuts it, implying finitude. If the essences are infinite, penetration is impossible, so mixing implies finitude. He also argues that darkness cannot preserve its density when mixing with subtle light; if it does, light acquires evil. The argument shows that the dualist model leads to contradictions about infinity, finitude, and the nature of mixing.

التقاء
متماس
متباين
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light and darkness can coexist in an eternal boundary without contradiction.
Correction: Maturidi shows that either they are completely separate (both temporal) or touching (which implies finitude or mixing that leads to contradictions).

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the possibility of mixing between light and darkness imply?
A) Both are eternal
B) They must be finite
C) They are completely separate
D) Darkness is denser than light

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dilemma shows that the dualist concept of an eternal boundary between light and darkness is logically incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter evil or darkness in the world, do you ever view it as an independent force opposing God? This lesson shows that dualism is logically incoherent. How does affirming that only God is eternal and all else is contingent reshape your trust in His absolute sovereignty?
?
Maturidi argues that if light mixed with darkness, light would 'acquire evil' and 'throw itself into imprisonment.' Where in your own life have you allowed what is pure to be compromised by what is dark? What boundary must you re-establish to remain whole?
?
The lesson ends with 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with God). How does this logical refutation deepen your reliance on God's power alone? What is one area where you need to stop acting as if evil has independent power and instead submit fully to God?

Maturidi's Refutation of Temporal Beginning of Mixing
Refuting the dualist claim that mixing had a temporal beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that mixing between light and darkness began in time, using logical possibilities and the constancy of natures.

Core Idea
If mixing began in time, dualism collapses into absurdity.

Imam Maturidi engages with dualist cosmologies that posit two eternal principles, light and darkness. Their mixing is said to have a temporal beginning. Maturidi systematically examines the possible causes of this mixing, revealing internal contradictions. The passage also invokes the principle that natures are unchanging, so mixing, if natural, must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure and the list of possibilities introduced by 'either...or'.
The passage is dense; take your time to parse each conditional.
2
Identify the logical possibilities
List the different scenarios Maturidi considers for the cause of mixing: by one principle, by both, by a third, by themselves, or darkness remaining itself.
Each possibility leads to a problematic consequence for dualism.
3
Understand the nature argument
Maturidi argues that mixing is by nature, and natures do not change. Therefore, if mixing occurs naturally, it must be eternal. Also note the argument about two parts of the same nature not mixing.
This argument targets the core assumption that mixing began in time.
4
Reflect on the refutation's force
Consider how each possibility undermines dualism, especially the need for a third principle or the denial of distinctness.
This refutation shows the internal inconsistency of dualist cosmogony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سبقت بما حدث من الامتزاج بعد أن لم يكن فإما أن كان بأحدهما أو بهما، وفيه احتمال الحدوث، فمثله الكل، أو ليس بها، ففى ذلك تثبيت ثالث، أو لأنفسهما كان، فلزم نفى التباين، أو تبقى الظلمة بنفسها فلم يكن ذلك الوقت بأولى مما قبله، وإذا لم يحدث في الجزئين اللذين لم يمتزجا شيء، وقد وجد، لم لا كان كذلك فى الكل؟ مع ما لا يخلو من الافتراق؛ إذ الامتزاج أن يكون بالطبع، والطبائع لا تنقلب، فيجب أن يكون أبدا كذلك. وأطنب في نوع الطبائع
And if mixing has been preceded by its happening after it had not been, then either it was by one of them or by both. In that is the possibility of temporal origination, so the whole would be like it. Or not by them, then in that is the establishment of a third [principle]. Or it was by themselves, then the denial of distinctness is necessary. Or darkness remains by itself, then that time would not be more likely than before. And if nothing occurred in the two parts that did not mix, but it has occurred, why would it not be so in the whole? Moreover, it is inseparable from separation; for mixing is by nature, and natures do not change, so it must always be as such. And he elaborated on the type of natures.
Source pages p. 759

Commentary
This passage refutes the dualist idea that mixing between light and darkness began in time. Maturidi presents several possibilities for the cause of mixing: (1) by one of them, but then that principle would be subject to temporal origination like the whole; (2) by a third principle, which challenges dualism; (3) by themselves, which negates their distinction; (4) darkness remains itself, making the timing arbitrary. He also argues that if two parts of the same nature have not mixed, but mixing occurred elsewhere, why not the same for the whole? Finally, mixing is by nature, and natures are constant, so mixing should be eternal. This undermines the dualist claim of a temporal beginning of the cosmic mixture.

حدوث
طبع
تباين
ثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists claim that mixing between light and darkness began in time without internal contradiction.
Correction: Maturidi shows that every possible cause for the temporal beginning of mixing leads to a contradiction with dualism, such as requiring a third principle or denying distinctness.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence Maturidi draws if mixing began in time?
A) Mixing must be by a third principle, undermining dualism
B) Mixing is impossible
C) Darkness is eternal
D) Light is the only cause

Key Takeaway
Maturidi systematically refutes the dualist claim of a temporal beginning of mixing by showing that each possible cause leads to an absurdity, and that the nature of mixing itself implies eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists claimed mixing began without a sufficient reason. In what areas of your life do you act as if things 'just happen' without accountability to Allah? Will you identify one such area today and submit it to His control?
?
Maturidi refuted the idea that anything temporal can originate without a cause. How does this truth confront your own excuses for delaying repentance? Do you live as if your next breath is uncaused by Allah's will?
?
If mixing is by nature and natures are immutable, then the dualist position is self-contradictory. What 'nature' have you assumed is unchangeable in yourself (e.g., a bad habit, a weak faith) that you have not submitted to the transforming power of Allah? What step will you take to break that assumption?

The Natural State of Things: Conjunction Over Separation
Maturidi refutes the claim that things naturally separate by pointing to observed mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Manichaean claim that separation is the natural state of things.

Core Idea
Observed mixing proves conjunction, not separation, is the natural state.

Maturidi begins his refutation by challenging a fundamental Manichaean premise: that things naturally separate and return to their origins. He uses the simple observation of mixing in the world to argue that conjunction is the norm. This lesson unpacks that core argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi contrasts the Manichaean claim with observed reality.
2
Identify the Manichaean claim
The Manichaeans argue that the nature of things is separation (التفرق) and everything returns to its original substance. Find this in the passage.
They rely on an abstract principle, not direct observation.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi counters that the nature of things is conjunction (الاجتماع) because mixing is witnessed. Note how he uses the fact that mixing has occurred to assert it is perpetual.
He calls their claim obstinacy (تعنت).
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this refutation is effective: it uses observable evidence against an unproven principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الامتزاج والانفصال ؛ ليعلموا تعنتهم ، ويقال : لم يعاينوا شيئا ممتزج من خير وشر ، ولم يرد لكم خبرا يحتمل الصدق ، يحتمل الصدق ، فإن قال : علمنا بالأدلة أن شأن الأشياء التفرق ، وكل شيء يرجع إلى أصل جوهره . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال : بل شأنهم الاجتماع ، فمنتهى كل على أصل جوهره ، وإذا كان وقع هذا قد اجتمع ، فاجعل ذلك أبدا كذلك . ويقال إذ التفرق تبدد ، والاجتماع تأكد وقوعه " لم لا كان شأنهم الاجتماع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ولو جاز تثبيت ما لا شاهد له في الشاهد ، مع كونه من جوهره ، لجاز القول يفعل الحواس على المعروف ، أو الدرك / باضداد ما به الدرك . وعُورضوا : لا يكون من النور غير الخير ولا من الظلمة غير الشر، فإذا قتل رجل ثم أقر ، فإن كان المقر هو الذي قتل ، وهو صدق ، فقد عمل به الخير بعد الذي لم يقتل ، فهو كذب ، وهو شر ، قد كان بقولهم هو الشر ، وإن كان المقر منه الخير ، وهو ترك القتل وكذلك من قولهم : إن كل حاسة لا تدرك ما تدركه الأخرى ، ثم فيما قال : سمعت ، أو فما رأى قال : رأيت، وما قال به رأيت وسمعت غير الذي به سمع ورأى ، وذلك جواب بما لم يدرك . وسئل عن سواد الظلمة إذا زيد على سواد النور ، وهل زاد في السواد شيئا ؟ فإن قالوا : لا ، صيروا ما كثر هو الذى لم يكثر ، فإن قالوا : ازداد ، قيل : أهو النور أو الظلمة أو غيرهما ؟ فإن قال بالأولين فازداد النور أو الظلمة ، وذلك بعيد ؛ إذ يزداد كل واحد منهما بالجوهر الآخر، وإن قال : غيرهما ، للأمرين غيرا . ثم ما يدريهم أن ليس فى النور أو الظلمة زيادة على تلك الأجناس لا يعلمون يجميع أجزاء الجنسين بما لا نهاية لكل واحد ، فإن ادعى الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب أبطل قوله بالتفرق وارتفاع النهاية ؛ لأنه لم يشهد ذلك ، فإن قال : علمنا بالرسل ، قيل : إذ كان الرسل من أجزاء النور والظلمة أن يكون الظلمة منعت وسترت أغيارا فيها غير الخمس فلم يعنم : مانعة فيا يدريكم وإن زعم فى الأول أنه يدرك بكل حاسة ما يدرك بغيرها فبطل قولهم : خمس حواس وحصل على الواحد ، ثم موجود / العجز مع السمع ، وكذلك سائر ذلك ، فثبت به الاختلاف. ثم عورض بحواس الظلمة، إنها إذا أدركت ما أدرك حواس النور. وكل شيء على ما هو عليه ، كيف صار أحد الإدراكين خيرا والآخر شرا؟ ثم عارض بالعفو عن الذم إنه فعل من ؟ فإن قال : فعل النور ، فهو نفع عدوه . وذلك شر ، وإن كانت من الظلمة فقد عفا ، فهو خير . والأصل إذا نجد في الشاهد جاهلا يعلم ومخطئا يندم وقائلا يرجع عن قوله ، فأما إن كان الثاني هو الأول فيثبت الفعلان المتضادان عن واحد ومن غيره ، فثبت كذب الخير بالموجود. الثلاثة ، وبالله التوفيق
Mixing and separation: that they may know their obstinacy, and it is said: they have not witnessed anything mixed of good and evil, and no report has come to you that could be true, could be true. For if he says: we know by evidence that the nature of things is separation, and everything returns to the essence of its substance. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said: Rather their nature is conjunction, and the end of each is upon the origin of its substance, and since this has occurred as conjunction, then make it always so. And it is said: separation is dispersal, and conjunction is confirmed in its occurrence, "why would their nature not be conjunction, and there is no power except by Allah. And if it were permissible to establish what has no witness in the witnessed, while it is of its substance, then it would be permissible to say that the senses act according to what is known, or perception by opposites of what perception is by. And they were objected to: there is from light nothing but good and from darkness nothing but evil. So if a man killed and then confessed, if the confessor is the one who killed, that is truth, then good was done by him after the one who did not kill, so it is falsehood, and it is evil, and it was in their saying it is evil. And if from the confessor there is good, which is refraining from killing. And likewise from their saying: each sense does not perceive what the other perceives, then in what he said: I heard, or what he saw he said: I saw, and what he said by I saw and I heard is other than that by which he heard and saw, and that is an answer by what is not perceived. And he was asked about the blackness of darkness if it is added to the blackness of light, and did it add anything to the blackness? If they say: no, they make what is abundant to be what did not become abundant. If they say: it increased, it is said: is it light or darkness or something else? If he says the first two, then light or darkness increased, and that is unlikely because each one increases by the other substance. And if he says: something else, then for the two things they have changed. Then what makes them know that there is not in light or darkness an increase over those genera? They do not know all parts of the two kinds without limit for each one. For if he claims inferring the unseen by the witnessed, his statement is invalidated by separation and the absence of limit, because he has not witnessed that. For if he says: we know by the messengers, it is said: since the messengers are from the parts of light and darkness, that darkness prevented and concealed other things in them other than the five, so they did not know: preventing, so how do you know? And if he claims in the first that he perceives by each sense what is perceived by another, then their statement of five senses is invalidated, and he ends up with one. Then existence/inability with hearing, and likewise the rest of that, so difference is established. Then they were objected to by the senses of darkness, that if they perceive what the senses of light perceive, and everything is as it is, how does one perception become good and the other evil? Then he objected to forgiveness from blame: it is an act of whom? If he says: an act of light, then it is benefiting its enemy, and that is evil. And if it is from darkness, then it has forgiven, so it is good. And the principle, if we find in the witnessed an ignorant person who knows, and an erroneous person who regrets, and a speaker who recants his statement. Now if the second is the first, then two opposing acts are established from one and from other than him. So the falsehood of good is established by what exists. The three, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation of Manichaean arguments regarding the mixing of light and darkness, the nature of good and evil, and the reliability of sensory perception. Maturidi begins by critiquing the Manichaean claim that things naturally separate to their origins, arguing instead that conjunction is the natural state, using the fact that mixing is observed. He then challenges the principle of inferring the unseen from the witnessed when the two are not analogous. The text presents several specific objections: a case of a murderer's confession to show that good and evil are not simply from light and darkness; the argument that each sense perceives differently, leading to contradictions in Manichaean claims; a question about adding blackness to blackness to expose incoherence; and an argument about messengers and senses. The discussion concludes with a challenge about forgiveness: if forgiving an enemy is good, but light acting against its enemy is evil, then Manichaean categories collapse. Maturidi ends by stating that the falsity of their dualistic good is established through these three points, trusting in Allah's guidance.

Key Terms
الامتزاج
الانفصال
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Manichaeans believe things naturally separate to their origins, based on an abstract principle.
Correction: Maturidi argues that observed mixing shows conjunction is the natural state, not separation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's refutation, what is the natural state of things based on observed evidence?
A) Separation, because everything returns to its original substance.
B) Conjunction, because mixing is actually witnessed.
C) Both separation and conjunction are equally possible.
D) It cannot be known from observation.

Key Takeaway
Observed mixing disproves the claim that separation is the natural state; conjunction is the norm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you assumed a rigid separation between good and evil—perhaps between 'sacred' and 'secular' activities? Are you willing to let that assumption be challenged by the reality you actually see, where good and evil are mixed daily? What would change if you accepted that conjunction is the natural state?
?
Maturidi accuses the Manichaeans of obstinacy for ignoring clear evidence. Is there a belief you hold—about yourself, others, or the world—that you cling to despite clear evidence to the contrary? What would it cost you to admit you were wrong and adjust your view based on what you observe?

Inferring the Unseen: The Senses and Knowledge
Maturidi refutes the Manichaean inference from the witnessed to the unseen and their sensory theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Manichaean method of inferring the unseen from the witnessed and their understanding of sensory perception.

Core Idea
Inferring the unseen from the witnessed fails when the two realms are fundamentally different.

Maturidi continues his refutation by attacking the Manichaean epistemology. He challenges their inference from the witnessed to the unseen and their claims about the senses, showing internal contradictions. This lesson covers these epistemological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the inference rule
The Manichaeans infer that what applies in the witnessed world must apply in the unseen. Maturidi points out the fallacy: if the unseen is of a different nature, the inference fails.
He uses the example: if something has no witness in the witnessed, it cannot be established in the unseen unless their substances are alike.
2
Understand the objection about senses
Maturidi then critizes their theory of senses: they claim each sense perceives only what is proper to it, yet they contradict themselves in testimony.
Note the example of someone saying 'I heard' when they saw, and vice versa.
3
See how the argument concludes
Maturidi concludes that their claim of five senses is invalid, and the existence of inability with hearing shows difference among senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الامتزاج والانفصال ؛ ليعلموا تعنتهم ، ويقال : لم يعاينوا شيئا ممتزج من خير وشر ، ولم يرد لكم خبرا يحتمل الصدق ، يحتمل الصدق ، فإن قال : علمنا بالأدلة أن شأن الأشياء التفرق ، وكل شيء يرجع إلى أصل جوهره . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال : بل شأنهم الاجتماع ، فمنتهى كل على أصل جوهره ، وإذا كان وقع هذا قد اجتمع ، فاجعل ذلك أبدا كذلك . ويقال إذ التفرق تبدد ، والاجتماع تأكد وقوعه " لم لا كان شأنهم الاجتماع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ولو جاز تثبيت ما لا شاهد له في الشاهد ، مع كونه من جوهره ، لجاز القول يفعل الحواس على المعروف ، أو الدرك / باضداد ما به الدرك . وعُورضوا : لا يكون من النور غير الخير ولا من الظلمة غير الشر، فإذا قتل رجل ثم أقر ، فإن كان المقر هو الذي قتل ، وهو صدق ، فقد عمل به الخير بعد الذي لم يقتل ، فهو كذب ، وهو شر ، قد كان بقولهم هو الشر ، وإن كان المقر منه الخير ، وهو ترك القتل وكذلك من قولهم : إن كل حاسة لا تدرك ما تدركه الأخرى ، ثم فيما قال : سمعت ، أو فما رأى قال : رأيت، وما قال به رأيت وسمعت غير الذي به سمع ورأى ، وذلك جواب بما لم يدرك . وسئل عن سواد الظلمة إذا زيد على سواد النور ، وهل زاد في السواد شيئا ؟ فإن قالوا : لا ، صيروا ما كثر هو الذى لم يكثر ، فإن قالوا : ازداد ، قيل : أهو النور أو الظلمة أو غيرهما ؟ فإن قال بالأولين فازداد النور أو الظلمة ، وذلك بعيد ؛ إذ يزداد كل واحد منهما بالجوهر الآخر، وإن قال : غيرهما ، للأمرين غيرا . ثم ما يدريهم أن ليس فى النور أو الظلمة زيادة على تلك الأجناس لا يعلمون يجميع أجزاء الجنسين بما لا نهاية لكل واحد ، فإن ادعى الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب أبطل قوله بالتفرق وارتفاع النهاية ؛ لأنه لم يشهد ذلك ، فإن قال : علمنا بالرسل ، قيل : إذ كان الرسل من أجزاء النور والظلمة أن يكون الظلمة منعت وسترت أغيارا فيها غير الخمس فلم يعنم : مانعة فيا يدريكم وإن زعم فى الأول أنه يدرك بكل حاسة ما يدرك بغيرها فبطل قولهم : خمس حواس وحصل على الواحد ، ثم موجود / العجز مع السمع ، وكذلك سائر ذلك ، فثبت به الاختلاف. ثم عورض بحواس الظلمة، إنها إذا أدركت ما أدرك حواس النور. وكل شيء على ما هو عليه ، كيف صار أحد الإدراكين خيرا والآخر شرا؟ ثم عارض بالعفو عن الذم إنه فعل من ؟ فإن قال : فعل النور ، فهو نفع عدوه . وذلك شر ، وإن كانت من الظلمة فقد عفا ، فهو خير . والأصل إذا نجد في الشاهد جاهلا يعلم ومخطئا يندم وقائلا يرجع عن قوله ، فأما إن كان الثاني هو الأول فيثبت الفعلان المتضادان عن واحد ومن غيره ، فثبت كذب الخير بالموجود. الثلاثة ، وبالله التوفيق
Mixing and separation: that they may know their obstinacy, and it is said: they have not witnessed anything mixed of good and evil, and no report has come to you that could be true, could be true. For if he says: we know by evidence that the nature of things is separation, and everything returns to the essence of its substance. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said: Rather their nature is conjunction, and the end of each is upon the origin of its substance, and since this has occurred as conjunction, then make it always so. And it is said: separation is dispersal, and conjunction is confirmed in its occurrence, "why would their nature not be conjunction, and there is no power except by Allah. And if it were permissible to establish what has no witness in the witnessed, while it is of its substance, then it would be permissible to say that the senses act according to what is known, or perception by opposites of what perception is by. And they were objected to: there is from light nothing but good and from darkness nothing but evil. So if a man killed and then confessed, if the confessor is the one who killed, that is truth, then good was done by him after the one who did not kill, so it is falsehood, and it is evil, and it was in their saying it is evil. And if from the confessor there is good, which is refraining from killing. And likewise from their saying: each sense does not perceive what the other perceives, then in what he said: I heard, or what he saw he said: I saw, and what he said by I saw and I heard is other than that by which he heard and saw, and that is an answer by what is not perceived. And he was asked about the blackness of darkness if it is added to the blackness of light, and did it add anything to the blackness? If they say: no, they make what is abundant to be what did not become abundant. If they say: it increased, it is said: is it light or darkness or something else? If he says the first two, then light or darkness increased, and that is unlikely because each one increases by the other substance. And if he says: something else, then for the two things they have changed. Then what makes them know that there is not in light or darkness an increase over those genera? They do not know all parts of the two kinds without limit for each one. For if he claims inferring the unseen by the witnessed, his statement is invalidated by separation and the absence of limit, because he has not witnessed that. For if he says: we know by the messengers, it is said: since the messengers are from the parts of light and darkness, that darkness prevented and concealed other things in them other than the five, so they did not know: preventing, so how do you know? And if he claims in the first that he perceives by each sense what is perceived by another, then their statement of five senses is invalidated, and he ends up with one. Then existence/inability with hearing, and likewise the rest of that, so difference is established. Then they were objected to by the senses of darkness, that if they perceive what the senses of light perceive, and everything is as it is, how does one perception become good and the other evil? Then he objected to forgiveness from blame: it is an act of whom? If he says: an act of light, then it is benefiting its enemy, and that is evil. And if it is from darkness, then it has forgiven, so it is good. And the principle, if we find in the witnessed an ignorant person who knows, and an erroneous person who regrets, and a speaker who recants his statement. Now if the second is the first, then two opposing acts are established from one and from other than him. So the falsehood of good is established by what exists. The three, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation of Manichaean arguments regarding the mixing of light and darkness, the nature of good and evil, and the reliability of sensory perception. Maturidi begins by critiquing the Manichaean claim that things naturally separate to their origins, arguing instead that conjunction is the natural state, using the fact that mixing is observed. He then challenges the principle of inferring the unseen from the witnessed when the two are not analogous. The text presents several specific objections: a case of a murderer's confession to show that good and evil are not simply from light and darkness; the argument that each sense perceives differently, leading to contradictions in Manichaean claims; a question about adding blackness to blackness to expose incoherence; and an argument about messengers and senses. The discussion concludes with a challenge about forgiveness: if forgiving an enemy is good, but light acting against its enemy is evil, then Manichaean categories collapse. Maturidi ends by stating that the falsity of their dualistic good is established through these three points, trusting in Allah's guidance.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
الحواس
الاختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What flaw does Maturidi expose in the Manichaean claim that each sense perceives only what is proper to it?
A) Senses can perceive everything equally.
B) People sometimes say they heard what they saw, mixing senses.
C) The five senses are not enough.
D) Senses cannot perceive opposites.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that Manichaean epistemology is flawed: their inference from the witnessed to the unseen fails, and their sensory theory is internally inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that inference from the physical world to the divine realm can fail if the realms are fundamentally different. Where in your own reasoning about Allah do you unknowingly project worldly rules onto Him? Will you today examine one belief you hold about God to see if it is based on a false analogy?
?
When you read the example of the killer and confessor, did you feel the weight of your own moral contradictions? Do you ever excuse your sins by blaming your environment or your 'nature'? What would it mean to fully accept that good and evil come from your choices, not from outside forces? Make one decision today that reflects that ownership.
?
The passage ends with a warning about mixing up the senses: saying 'I heard' when you saw, or 'I saw' when you heard. When it comes to your faith, how often do you claim to 'see' what you have only been told, or claim to 'hear' what you have only imagined? What is one area where you need to be honest about the source of your knowledge?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
4 min
The Nature of Increase: Blackness of Darkness and Light
Maturidi uses the additivity of blackness to expose inconsistencies in Manichaean substance dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument against the Manichaean conception of substance increase through the example of adding blackness to blackness.

Core Idea
If darkness can add to light, the substances must interpenetrate, contradicting their separation.

Maturidi presents a clever dilemma about the addition of blackness: if the blackness of darkness is added to the blackness of light, does it increase the blackness? This exposes the incoherence of their substance theory. This lesson covers that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the question
The Manichaeans are asked: if the blackness of darkness is added to the blackness of light, does it add anything?
This challenges their idea that light and darkness are distinct substances.
2
Understand the two possible answers
If they say no, then what is abundant (the combined blackness) is the same as what was not abundant (the original). If they say yes, then ask: has light increased or darkness increased?
Either answer leads to a problem.
3
See the consequence of increase
If they say light or darkness increased, that means one substance increases by the other, implying interpenetration. If they say something else, then the two have changed, which contradicts their fixed natures.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
This argument undermines the rigid dualism by showing that increase is impossible without mixing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Are they truly aligned with the Qur'an and Sunnah, or have they concealed the truth? What step will you take to verify your sources?
?

Final Refutations: Senses of Darkness, Forgiveness, and Conclusion
Maturidi delivers final criticisms on sensory parity, forgiveness, and concludes the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final arguments against Manichaean dualism, including the challenge of sensory parity, forgiveness, and the establishment of falsehood in their system.

Core Idea
If darkness's senses perceive the same as light's, then good/evil distinction collapses; forgiveness shows the system's inconsistency.

Maturidi concludes his refutation with several powerful objections: if the senses of darkness perceive the same as those of light, how can one perception be good and the other evil? Also, the act of forgiveness—if light forgives its enemy, that is good, but light acting for its enemy's benefit is evil. These contradictions show the falsehood of their dualism. This lesson covers these final points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find the objection about senses of darkness
Maturidi objects: if the senses of darkness perceive the same as the senses of light, how can one perception be good and the other evil?
If both perceive the same, the moral valuation cannot be based on the sense itself.
2
Understand the forgiveness argument
Forgiveness from blame: if it is an act of light, then light is benefiting its enemy, which they consider evil. If it is an act of darkness, then darkness has forgiven, which is good. So both cannot be pure.
This undermines the fixed nature of good being only from light and evil only from darkness.
3
See the conclusion
Maturidi ends by stating that from these three points (the three arguments), the falsehood of their concept of good is established.
"فثبت كذب الخير بالموجود. الثلاثة"

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قولهم : إن كل حاسة لا تدرك ما تدركه الأخرى ، ثم فيما قال : سمعت ، أو فما رأى قال : رأيت، وما قال به رأيت وسمعت غير الذي به سمع ورأى ، وذلك جواب بما لم يدرك . وسئل عن سواد الظلمة إذا زيد على سواد النور ، وهل زاد في السواد شيئا ؟ فإن قالوا : لا ، صيروا ما كثر هو الذى لم يكثر ، فإن قالوا : ازداد ، قيل : أهو النور أو الظلمة أو غيرهما ؟ فإن قال بالأولين فازداد النور أو الظلمة ، وذلك بعيد ؛ إذ يزداد كل واحد منهما بالجوهر الآخر، وإن قال : غيرهما ، للأمرين غيرا . ثم ما يدريهم أن ليس فى النور أو الظلمة زيادة على تلك الأجناس لا يعلمون يجميع أجزاء الجنسين بما لا نهاية لكل واحد ، فإن ادعى الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب أبطل قوله بالتفرق وارتفاع النهاية ؛ لأنه لم يشهد ذلك ، فإن قال : علمنا بالرسل ، قيل : إذ كان الرسل من أجزاء النور والظلمة أن يكون الظلمة منعت وسترت أغيارا فيها غير الخمس فلم يعنم : مانعة فيا يدريكم وإن زعم فى الأول أنه يدرك بكل حاسة ما يدرك بغيرها فبطل قولهم : خمس حواس وحصل على الواحد ، ثم موجود / العجز مع السمع ، وكذلك سائر ذلك ، فثبت به الاختلاف. ثم عورض بحواس الظلمة، إنها إذا أدركت ما أدرك حواس النور. وكل شيء على ما هو عليه ، كيف صار أحد الإدراكين خيرا والآخر شرا؟ ثم عارض بالعفو عن الذم إنه فعل من ؟ فإن قال : فعل النور ، فهو نفع عدوه . وذلك شر ، وإن كانت من الظلمة فقد عفا ، فهو خير . والأصل إذا نجد في الشاهد جاهلا يعلم ومخطئا يندم وقائلا يرجع عن قوله ، فأما إن كان الثاني هو الأول فيثبت الفعلان المتضادان عن واحد ومن غيره ، فثبت كذب الخير بالموجود. الثلاثة ، وبالله التوفيق
Mixing and separation: that they may know their obstinacy, and it is said: they have not witnessed anything mixed of good and evil, and no report has come to you that could be true, could be true. For if he says: we know by evidence that the nature of things is separation, and everything returns to the essence of its substance. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said: Rather their nature is conjunction, and the end of each is upon the origin of its substance, and since this has occurred as conjunction, then make it always so. And it is said: separation is dispersal, and conjunction is confirmed in its occurrence, "why would their nature not be conjunction, and there is no power except by Allah. And if it were permissible to establish what has no witness in the witnessed, while it is of its substance, then it would be permissible to say that the senses act according to what is known, or perception by opposites of what perception is by. And they were objected to: there is from light nothing but good and from darkness nothing but evil. So if a man killed and then confessed, if the confessor is the one who killed, that is truth, then good was done by him after the one who did not kill, so it is falsehood, and it is evil, and it was in their saying it is evil. And if from the confessor there is good, which is refraining from killing. And likewise from their saying: each sense does not perceive what the other perceives, then in what he said: I heard, or what he saw he said: I saw, and what he said by I saw and I heard is other than that by which he heard and saw, and that is an answer by what is not perceived. And he was asked about the blackness of darkness if it is added to the blackness of light, and did it add anything to the blackness? If they say: no, they make what is abundant to be what did not become abundant. If they say: it increased, it is said: is it light or darkness or something else? If he says the first two, then light or darkness increased, and that is unlikely because each one increases by the other substance. And if he says: something else, then for the two things they have changed. Then what makes them know that there is not in light or darkness an increase over those genera? They do not know all parts of the two kinds without limit for each one. For if he claims inferring the unseen by the witnessed, his statement is invalidated by separation and the absence of limit, because he has not witnessed that. For if he says: we know by the messengers, it is said: since the messengers are from the parts of light and darkness, that darkness prevented and concealed other things in them other than the five, so they did not know: preventing, so how do you know? And if he claims in the first that he perceives by each sense what is perceived by another, then their statement of five senses is invalidated, and he ends up with one. Then existence/inability with hearing, and likewise the rest of that, so difference is established. Then they were objected to by the senses of darkness, that if they perceive what the senses of light perceive, and everything is as it is, how does one perception become good and the other evil? Then he objected to forgiveness from blame: it is an act of whom? If he says: an act of light, then it is benefiting its enemy, and that is evil. And if it is from darkness, then it has forgiven, so it is good. And the principle, if we find in the witnessed an ignorant person who knows, and an erroneous person who regrets, and a speaker who recants his statement. Now if the second is the first, then two opposing acts are established from one and from other than him. So the falsehood of good is established by what exists. The three, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation of Manichaean arguments regarding the mixing of light and darkness, the nature of good and evil, and the reliability of sensory perception. Maturidi begins by critiquing the Manichaean claim that things naturally separate to their origins, arguing instead that conjunction is the natural state, using the fact that mixing is observed. He then challenges the principle of inferring the unseen from the witnessed when the two are not analogous. The text presents several specific objections: a case of a murderer's confession to show that good and evil are not simply from light and darkness; the argument that each sense perceives differently, leading to contradictions in Manichaean claims; a question about adding blackness to blackness to expose incoherence; and an argument about messengers and senses. The discussion concludes with a challenge about forgiveness: if forgiving an enemy is good, but light acting against its enemy is evil, then Manichaean categories collapse. Maturidi ends by stating that the falsity of their dualistic good is established through these three points, trusting in Allah's guidance.

Key Terms
الحواس
الظلمة
النور
العفو
الخير
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi's argument about forgiveness show?
A) Only light can forgive.
B) Forgiveness can be good or evil regardless of the substance.
C) Darkness cannot forgive because it is evil.
D) Forgiveness is not possible in the Manichaean system.

Key Takeaway
Through the objections of sensory parity, forgiveness, and personal change, Maturidi conclusively shows the falsehood of Manichaean dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something evil, do you tend to blame it on an external force (e.g., environment, upbringing) or accept it as your own choice? How does Maturidi's argument that opposing acts come from one person challenge your tendency to shift responsibility?
?
Think of someone you consider an enemy. Would you ever do a good deed for them? The objection shows that if light forgives its enemy, it is considered evil by Manichaeans. Does your heart hold back from forgiving an enemy because it feels like a betrayal? What does this reveal about your attachment to your ego?
?
The principle of change shows that a person can go from ignorant to knowledgeable, from error to regret. Which area of your life are you most resistant to change? What would it mean for you to truly believe that you are not fixed in your moral state but can recant past statements and actions?

The Daysanite Doctrine of Light and Darkness
Maturidi presents the Daysanite claim about light and darkness and begins his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Daysanite dualist conception of light and darkness and how Maturidi relates it to Manichaeism.

Core Idea
The Daysanites reduce good and evil to light and darkness, but Maturidi sees them as akin to Manichaeans.

This lesson introduces the Daysanite sect, a dualist group that posits light and darkness as two fundamental principles. Imam Maturidi presents their view and immediately draws a parallel to the more well-known Manichaeans, noting their common origin. Though Maturidi's full refutation is not included here, this passage establishes the initial framing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Daysanite claim
Read the passage where the Daysanites state that light is all whiteness and living, while darkness is all blackness and dead. Notice how they describe the mixing process.
The analogy of filing iron illustrates their view of softening roughness.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the terms: light, darkness, whiteness, blackness, living, dead, mixing, management. Understand that light is active and darkness passive.
3
Observe Maturidi's response
Read Maturidi's brief comment: he says the Daysanite saying is like that of the Manichaeans in origin, but then the text cuts off. Consider why he might make this comparison.
This sets up a refutation that likely follows on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : أقاويل الديصانية وبيان فسادها قال الشيخ قالوا : النور بياض كله ، والظلمة سواد كلها ، والنور حي ، هو الذي مازج الظلمة وهي ميتة ، لما وجد من خشونتها في الجهة التي تلقاه ، فأراد الممازجة ليدبر تدبيرا يلين ، وقد يخشن اللين كما يخشن الحديد عن المنشار إذا نقل بعض عن بعض بالمبرد ، فإذا ذهب الشق واستوت أجزاؤه لان . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وقول الديحانية مثل قول المنانية في الأصل ، لكنهم
Secondly: The sayings of the Daysanites and the explanation of their corruption. The Shaykh said: They said: Light is all whiteness, and darkness is all blackness. Light is living, it is that which mixed with darkness while it is dead, because it found roughness in it in the direction that meets it. It wanted mixing in order to manage a management that softens. And the soft may become rough just as iron becomes rough from the file when one part is transferred from another by the file. And when the split goes away and its parts become even, it softens. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The saying of the Daysanites is like the saying of the Manichaeans in origin, except that they...
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the Daysanite doctrine along with the beginning of Maturidi's response. The Daysanites, a dualist sect, claim that light is entirely white and living, while darkness is entirely black and dead. They assert that light mixed with darkness because it found roughness in it, aiming to soften it through a process analogous to filing iron. Maturidi points out that the Daysanites' belief is similar in origin to the Manichaeans, but notes that they differ in some details. The text ends abruptly with the word 'لكنهم' (except that they...), likely indicating the start of an explanation of the difference, which is not fully captured in this window. The editor's footnote indicates that the Daysanites are named after their founder, and their doctrine resembles Manichaeism.

Key Terms

النور (light)
الظلمة (darkness)
البياض (whiteness)
السواد (blackness)
الممازجة (mixing)
تدبير (management/arrangement)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Daysanites are entirely distinct from the Manichaeans.
Correction: Maturidi states that their saying is like that of the Manichaeans in origin, indicating a close relationship, though there may be differences.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between the Daysanite and Manichaean doctrines?
A) They are completely different in origin.
B) The Daysanites are a branch of the Manichaeans.
C) They are similar in origin but differ in details.
D) The Daysanites rejected the Manichaean teaching.

Key Takeaway
The Daysanites, like the Manichaeans, are dualists who explain the world through light and darkness, but Maturidi begins to show their connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Daysanites divided the world into pure light (good) and pure darkness (evil). Do you ever find yourself categorizing people or events as entirely good or entirely bad, forgetting that Allah alone knows the full truth? How can you train yourself to see nuance instead of dualism?
?
Maturidi immediately identifies the Daysanites' error by comparing them to Manichaeans. When you hear a new idea that contradicts Islamic teachings, do you quickly compare it to known false beliefs, or do you sometimes get swayed by its novelty? What step will you take to guard your aqeedah against subtle deviations?

Refutation of Dualist Theory of Perception
Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that perception is divided between light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist theory of perception by exposing internal contradictions in their claims about the senses, good/evil, and the source of actions.

Core Idea
Dualism collapses when its own logic is applied to the senses and morality.

This passage is a dense refutation of a dualist sect that claimed perception is divided between light (good) and darkness (evil). Imam Maturidi systematically dismantles their argument by showing that their own premises lead to absurdities—such as one sense being indistinguishable from another, and the arbitrary assignment of good and evil to perceptions. He also analyzes the concept of forgiveness to further undermine their framework. Follow along as he moves through each contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage has several conditional refutations; note the structure 'If he says... it is said...'
2
Identify the four key objections
Look for each objection introduced by 'ثم' (then) or 'فإن قال' (if he says). There are at least four distinct arguments: collapse of five senses, comparison of perceptions, analysis of pardon, and examples from the witnessed world.
The objections are numbered in the commentary.
3
Trace how each objection exposes contradiction
For each objection, ask: what dualist premise is being used against them? Notice how Maturidi turns their own logic back on them.
4
Summarize the conclusion
The final sentence 'فثبت كذب الخير بالموجود' (the falsity of good by the existent is established) is the punchline. Consider how the whole argument leads to this conclusion.
The dualist cannot maintain that good and evil come from separate sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : علمنا بالرسل ، قيل : إذ كان الرسل من أجزاء النور والظلمة أن يكون الظلمة منعت وسترت أغيارا فيها غير الخمس فلم يعنم : مانعة فيا يدريكم وإن زعم فى الأول أنه يدرك بكل حاسة ما يدرك بغيرها فبطل قولهم : خمس حواس وحصل على الواحد ، ثم موجود / العجز مع السمع ، وكذلك سائر ذلك ، فثبت به الاختلاف. ثم عورض بحواس الظلمة، إنها إذا أدركت ما أدرك حواس النور. وكل شيء على ما هو عليه ، كيف صار أحد الإدراكين خيرا والآخر شرا؟ ثم عارض بالعفو عن الذم إنه فعل من ؟ فإن قال : فعل النور ، فهو نفع عدوه . وذلك شر ، وإن كانت من الظلمة فقد عفا ، فهو خير . والأصل إذا نجد في الشاهد جاهلا يعلم ومخطئا يندم وقائلا يرجع عن قوله ، فأما إن كان الثاني هو الأول فيثبت الفعلان المتضادان عن واحد ومن غيره ، فثبت كذب الخير بالموجود. الثلاثة ، وبالله التوفيق
If he says: 'We know by the messengers,' it is said: Since the messengers are from the parts of light and darkness, that darkness prevented and concealed changes other than the five, so it is not known: a preventer? In what informs you? And if he claims in the first that he perceives by every sense what is perceived by other than it, then their statement of five senses is invalid, and he arrives at one. Then there exists incapacity with hearing, and likewise the rest of that, so difference is established. Then it is objected by the senses of darkness: that they perceived what the senses of light perceived. And everything is as it is, how does one of the two perceptions become good and the other evil? Then he objected by pardon from blame: whose act is it? If he says: 'act of light,' then it is benefit to his enemy, and that is evil. And if it is from darkness, then he pardoned, so it is good. And the principle: we find in the witnessed one who is ignorant yet knows, one who errs and regrets, and one who speaks and retracts. As for if the second is the first, then two opposing acts are established from one and from other than him, so the falsity of good by the existent is established. The three, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the dualist argument about the senses. The author first challenges the dualist claim that knowledge comes through messengers composed of light and darkness. He argues that if darkness were separate and prevented perception, then the claim of five senses collapses. He then points out that if one sense could perceive what another does, the classification is invalid, leading to a single sense. The objection continues: if darkness's senses perceive what light's senses perceive, then both perceptions are alike—so why is one deemed good and the other evil? Furthermore, the act of pardon: if light pardons, that benefits its enemy (evil); if darkness pardons, that is good, contradicting their assignment of good to light. The author concludes by noting that in the observed world, we find contradictory traits in one person (ignorance and knowledge, error and regret), so it is possible for opposing acts to come from one source, disproving the dualist claim that good and evil must have separate origins.

Key Terms
حواس
نور
ظلمة
خير
شر
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the refutation relies on circular reasoning.
Correction: The refutation exposes internal contradictions in the dualist position by using their own premises against them, not by assuming opposing premises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one consequence if the senses of darkness perceive what the senses of light perceive?
A) The senses become five.
B) The two perceptions are indistinguishable, so calling one good and the other evil becomes arbitrary.
C) Light becomes evil.
D) Darkness becomes good.

Key Takeaway
The dualist claim of separate sources for good and evil is refuted by showing that their own logic leads to contradictions in perception, morality, and the unity of opposing attributes in a single subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a mistake or do something you regret, do you ever attribute it to a 'dark side' or external force, rather than taking full ownership? How does Imam Maturidi's argument that contradictory attributes can coexist in one being (ignorant yet knowing, errant yet regretting) challenge you to see your own capacity for both good and bad as part of your unified self? What would change if you stopped excusing your faults as coming from 'outside' you?
?
The dualist assigns 'good' to light and 'evil' to darkness. In your daily judgments of people or events, do you tend to categorize them as all good or all bad, missing the complexity? How does the refutation's point that labeling one perception 'good' and another 'evil' is arbitrary (since they perceive the same things) apply to your own tendency to oversimplify? Will you commit to looking for the goodness in what you call 'dark' and the flaw in what you call 'light'?
?
Imam Maturidi asks: 'Whose act is pardon from blame?' If light pardons its enemy (darkness), that is considered evil; if darkness pardons, that is good. This shows that moral labels depend on context and intention. In your life, have you ever assumed an action was good or bad based only on its apparent source (e.g., 'this is a religious act, so it's good') without examining the intention? How does this argument push you to be more honest about your motives?

The Dayṣāniyya Doctrine of Two Origins
Imam Maturidi quotes the Dayṣāniyya creed on light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Dayṣāniyya's belief in two eternal principles—light and darkness—and their account of how light mixed with darkness to soften its roughness.

Core Idea
The Dayṣāniyya believed light and darkness are eternal opposites that mixed by accident.

Imam Maturidi begins by recording the views of the Dayṣāniyya sect, a dualist group similar to the Manichaeans. They held that light is pure goodness and darkness pure evil, and that light attempted to subdue darkness through an ill-fated mixing. Understanding their doctrine is essential to seeing the contradictions in their logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing their view
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the Dayṣāniyya define light and darkness and the story of their mixing.
2
Identify the attributes of light and darkness
Light is described as living, white, and active; darkness as dead, black, and passive. Notice the metaphor of iron being filed.
The simile of iron from the saw illustrates how roughness is smoothed.
3
Consider the internal inconsistency
Ask yourself: If light is all-good and all-powerful, why does it need to mix with darkness? Why does it fail to escape? This sets up the refutation.
The author will later point out that this story makes light ignorant and weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : أقاويل الديصانية وبيان فسادها قال الشيخ قالوا : النور بياض كله ، والظلمة سواد كلها ، والنور حي ، هو الذي مازج الظلمة وهي ميتة ، لما وجد من خشونتها في الجهة التي تلقاه ، فأراد الممازجة ليدبر تدبيرا يلين ، وقد يخشن اللين كما يخشن الحديد عن المنشار إذا نقل بعض عن بعض بالمبرد ، فإذا ذهب الشق واستوت أجزاؤه لان . رحمه الله : وقول الديحانية مثل قول المنانية في الأصل ، لكنهم قال بعضهم : لا ، بل تأذى بها ، فدفعها عن نفسه ، فمازجها كمن يبلى بالوحل ، إنه إذا تكلف الخروج يزداد فيه ولوجاً ، والحركة تكون من النور ، والسكون من ضده ؛ إذ هما متضادان ، فأوجبوا أصلين : نورا وظلمة ، وفرعين : حركة النور وحسه ، وسكون الظلمة وعدم الحس، من غير أن يبينوا شيئا سوى النور والظلمة قال الفقيه رحمه الله : ذكرنا أقاويلهم لتعلموا مقت الله ممن آثر عداوته ، ولم يتكر فى خلقه بفكر خاضع له مستغيث به ليوفقه لدينه وعدل عن طاعته ، لكن مال إلى الدنيا ركونا إليها ورغبة في شهوات نفسه فوكل إلى نفسه ، ولم يعصمه من عدوه ؛ إذ لم يتضرع إليه ، ولا رغب في غير الذى مال إليه ، وبالله نستعين والأصل أن الله عز وجل يجعل هلاك عبده بالذى به يدعى جحوده ، ويعدل عن طاعته خوفا عن أمر يلزمه بأن يهلكه بلزومه فيما طمع الخلوص عنه . فهؤلاء أن الذى يكون منه الخير لا يحتمل كون الشر منه صاروا إلى القول باثنين ، ويجعل أصل كل غير الذي هو أصل الآخر ، ثم صيروا الذي أصل الخير عندهم هو النهاية في الشر ، والذي هو أصل الشر عندهم في الخير ؛ لأن هؤلاء صيروا النور جاهلا بعواقب ما إليه يصير ، حتى كان على أحد القولين أراد دفع أذاه فبقى فيه ، لا يعلم أنه لا يقدر عليه ، ولا أنه يبقى فى غاية ما رام دفعه ، ولا قدر على التخلص ، إذ بلى به ، والأول صار إليه ليلين خشونته ويدفع أذاه جهلا منه أنه لا يقدر عليه ، وعجزاً أن يتخلص عنه . وكذلك على قول المانى إن الظلمة هى التى بغت على النور وألقته في حبسها وأوثقته بوثاقها حتى جهل مأتاه وعجز عن النجاة ، وبدوء كل خير ، ونهاية العلم ، والإحاطة بكل خير ، والبلوغ إليه إنما هو بالقدرة عليه ، فأزالوا الأمرين جميعا عن النور ، وحققوا للظلمة ، فصاروا إلى نقض جميع ما بنوا : إن الخير
Secondly: Sayings of the Dayṣāniyya and exposition of their corruption. The Shaykh said: They said: Light is all whiteness, and darkness is all blackness. Light is living; it is what mixed with darkness while it was dead, because it found roughness in it in the direction it encountered, so it wanted mixing to manage a management to soften it. And softness may become rough, as iron becomes rough from the saw when some is transferred by the file. So when the split goes and its parts become even, it becomes soft. May God have mercy on him: The saying of the Dayṣāniyya is like the saying of the Manichaeans in origin, but they... Some of them said: No, rather it was harmed by it, so it pushed it away from itself, so it mixed with it like one who gets stuck in mud: when he strives to get out, he sinks further in. Motion is from light, and stillness from its opposite, since they are opposites. So they posited two origins: light and darkness, and two branches: motion of light and its sense, and stillness of darkness and absence of sense, without explaining anything other than light and darkness. The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: We mentioned their sayings so that you may learn God's wrath upon those who preferred His enmity and did not apply themselves in His creation with a humble thought, seeking His help to guide them to His religion, and turned away from obedience, but inclined to the world, leaning towards it and desiring their own passions, so He left them to themselves and did not protect them from their enemy, since they did not supplicate to Him nor desire other than what they inclined to. And with God we seek help. The principle is that God Almighty causes the destruction of His servant by that by which he claims his denial, and he turns away from His obedience out of fear of a matter that obligates him, that He destroys him by his adherence to what he hoped to escape from. These people, because they thought that from which good comes cannot be the source of evil, resorted to the statement of two principles, and made the origin of each other than the origin of the other. Then they made that which is the origin of good in their view the ultimate in evil, and that which is the origin of evil in their view the ultimate in good. Because these people made light ignorant of the consequences of what it would become, so that on one of the two sayings it intended to repel its harm and remained in it, not knowing that it was unable to do so, nor that it would remain in the utmost of what it sought to repel, nor able to extricate itself, since it was afflicted by it. And the first (saying) had it that it came to soften its roughness and repel its harm out of ignorance that it was unable to do so, and incapacity to extricate itself. Likewise, according to the saying of Mani, darkness is what attacked light and threw it into its prison and bound it with its bonds until it became ignorant of its way and incapable of salvation. And the beginning of every good, the end of knowledge, and encompassment of every good, and attaining it, is only through power over it. So they removed both matters from light and affirmed them for darkness, leading to the nullification of all they had built: that good is...
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage is both a record and a refutation of the Dayṣāniyya sect. It begins by quoting their doctrine: light is pure white, darkness pure black; light is living and mixed with dead darkness to soften its roughness. A simile compares this to iron softened by filing. Some said light was harmed and pushed darkness away, like a person stuck in mud. They posited two origins (light, darkness) and two branches (motion/sense from light, stillness/absence of sense from darkness). The author then comments: he relates these sayings so that the reader may see God's wrath on those who reject Him, relying on their own reason rather than supplication. He explains that God may destroy a servant through the very thing he claims (i.e., the dualist argument leads to self-contradiction). The refutation is that the dualists, unable to conceive of good and evil from one source, posited two origins. But then they end up attributing ignorance and incapacity to light—the supposed source of all good—because in their stories, light either fails to escape darkness or acts ignorantly. Thus, they make light the ultimate evil and darkness the ultimate good, completely inverting their own principles and nullifying their entire system. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: the dualist position collapses because it attributes to light the very defects it ascribes to darkness.

Misconception: That the Dayṣāniyya are identical to the Manichaeans in every detail.
Correction: The author states their saying is like that of the Manichaeans in origin but then mentions a variant view, indicating they are a related but distinct sect.

Checkpoint

B) Dead and white

Key Takeaway
The Dayṣāniyya doctrine of two eternal opposites (light and darkness) with a narrative of mixing is internally inconsistent, as the refutation will show.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself subconsciously believing in a 'second force' — attributing your failures to external circumstances or the 'darkness' around you? In what area of your life have you been acting like light and darkness are independent powers, rather than both under Allah's control?
?
The Dayṣāniyya sought to 'explain' the world by inventing a story of mixing and softening. Where are you tempted to rationalize away the clear truth of tawhid — perhaps by making excuses for sins or explaining away divine commands?
?
Imagine you are like the light that 'mixed with darkness' — in what relationship, habit, or belief have you compromised your pure fitrah? What would it mean for you to stop 'softening' that darkness and instead cut it off entirely?

Refutation of Dualism: The Self-Destruction of the Two-Principle Theory
Imam Maturidi refutes the Dayṣāniyya dualism by showing its internal contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Dayṣāniyya by exposing that their own account attributes ignorance, weakness, and failure to light, thereby inverting their core claim and nullifying their entire system.

Core Idea
The dualists' own story makes light the ultimate source of evil.

Having presented the Dayṣāniyya's doctrine, Imam Maturidi now exposes its fatal flaw: if light is the source of all good, it cannot be ignorant, weak, or trapped by darkness. Yet their narratives force just such conclusions, leading to a complete self-contradiction that destroys their entire framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that the author begins by commenting on why he records these sayings and then launches into the logical critique.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فصاروا إلى نقض جميع ما بنوا'—they end up nullifying all they built.
2
Identify the key flaw: light's ignorance and impotence
The author points out that in both versions of the Dayṣāniyya story, light is either ignorant of the consequences or unable to escape darkness. This contradicts the claim that light is the source of all good.
He says: 'They made light ignorant of consequences... unable to extricate itself.'
3
Understand the inversion of good and evil
Because light is shown to be ignorant and powerless, it becomes evil. Darkness, which is the origin of evil, becomes the ultimate good. Thus the dualist position collapses.
The author says: 'They removed both matters from light and affirmed them for darkness.'

ثانياً : أقاويل الديصانية وبيان فسادها قال الشيخ قالوا : النور بياض كله ، والظلمة سواد كلها ، والنور حي ، هو الذي مازج الظلمة وهي ميتة ، لما وجد من خشونتها في الجهة التي تلقاه ، فأراد الممازجة ليدبر تدبيرا يلين ، وقد يخشن اللين كما يخشن الحديد عن المنشار إذا نقل بعض عن بعض بالمبرد ، فإذا ذهب الشق واستوت أجزاؤه لان . رحمه الله : وقول الديحانية مثل قول المنانية في الأصل ، لكنهم قال بعضهم : لا ، بل تأذى بها ، فدفعها عن نفسه ، فمازجها كمن يبلى بالوحل ، إنه إذا تكلف الخروج يزداد فيه ولوجاً ، والحركة تكون من النور ، والسكون من ضده ؛ إذ هما متضادان ، فأوجبوا أصلين : نورا وظلمة ، وفرعين : حركة النور وحسه ، وسكون الظلمة وعدم الحس، من غير أن يبينوا شيئا سوى النور والظلمة قال الفقيه رحمه الله : ذكرنا أقاويلهم لتعلموا مقت الله ممن آثر عداوته ، ولم يتكر فى خلقه بفكر خاضع له مستغيث به ليوفقه لدينه وعدل عن طاعته ، لكن مال إلى الدنيا ركونا إليها ورغبة في شهوات نفسه فوكل إلى نفسه ، ولم يعصمه من عدوه ؛ إذ لم يتضرع إليه ، ولا رغب في غير الذى مال إليه ، وبالله نستعين والأصل أن الله عز وجل يجعل هلاك عبده بالذى به يدعى جحوده ، ويعدل عن طاعته خوفا عن أمر يلزمه بأن يهلكه بلزومه فيما طمع الخلوص عنه . فهؤلاء أن الذى يكون منه الخير لا يحتمل كون الشر منه صاروا إلى القول باثنين ، ويجعل أصل كل غير الذي هو أصل الآخر ، ثم صيروا الذي أصل الخير عندهم هو النهاية في الشر ، والذي هو أصل الشر عندهم في الخير ؛ لأن هؤلاء صيروا النور جاهلا بعواقب ما إليه يصير ، حتى كان على أحد القولين أراد دفع أذاه فبقى فيه ، لا يعلم أنه لا يقدر عليه ، ولا أنه يبقى فى غاية ما رام دفعه ، ولا قدر على التخلص ، إذ بلى به ، والأول صار إليه ليلين خشونته ويدفع أذاه جهلا منه أنه لا يقدر عليه ، وعجزاً أن يتخلص عنه . وكذلك على قول المانى إن الظلمة هى التى بغت على النور وألقته في حبسها وأوثقته بوثاقها حتى جهل مأتاه وعجز عن النجاة ، وبدوء كل خير ، ونهاية العلم ، والإحاطة بكل خير ، والبلوغ إليه إنما هو بالقدرة عليه ، فأزالوا الأمرين جميعا عن النور ، وحققوا للظلمة ، فصاروا إلى نقض جميع ما بنوا : إن الخير

Commentary
This passage is both a record and a refutation of the Dayṣāniyya sect. The Dualist Doctrine of Light and Darkness and Maturidi's Moral Reflection
Maturidi reports the dualist view of two origins and adds a moral reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist (Manichaean) doctrine of two origins (light and darkness) and Maturidi's moral warning against relying on one's own reasoning without supplication to Allah.

Core Idea
Dualism mistakes creation's oppositions for eternal principles.

Maturidi reports the dualist belief that light and darkness are two eternal principles, with motion and sensation from light, stillness and insensibility from darkness. After presenting their view, he adds a personal reflection: he cites their doctrine so that we may recognize God's wrath against those who turn away from Him, trusting their own intellect. This lesson unpacks both the doctrine and the moral lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: first a report of the dualist view, then Maturidi's commentary.
The passage is in two parts; you will analyze each separately.
2
Identify the two origins and two branches
The dualists affirm two origins (aṣlān): light and darkness. From these come two branches (farʿān): motion and sensation from light; stillness and lack of sensation from darkness. Find these in the text.
These are the core of their metaphysical system.
3
Reflect on the mud metaphor
The dualists use a metaphor: light was harmed by darkness and tried to push it away, but became entangled like someone stuck in mud—the more he tries to escape, the more he sinks. This explains the mixture of good and evil in the world.
Consider what this metaphor implies about the nature of light and darkness.
4
Understand Maturidi's moral commentary
Maturidi says he mentions these views so that you may learn God's wrath against those who prefer enmity over submission, who rely on their own intellect rather than humbly seeking Allah's guidance. Such people are left to their devices.
This is the key lesson: turning away from Allah leads to being abandoned to one's own desires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : لا ، بل تأذى بها ، فدفعها عن نفسه ، فمازجها كمن يبلى بالوحل ، إنه إذا تكلف الخروج يزداد فيه ولوجاً ، والحركة تكون من النور ، والسكون من ضده ؛ إذ هما متضادان ، فأوجبوا أصلين : نورا وظلمة ، وفرعين : حركة النور وحسه ، وسكون الظلمة وعدم الحس، من غير أن يبينوا شيئا سوى النور والظلمة. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : ذكرنا أقاويلهم لتعلموا مقت الله ممن آثر عداوته ، ولم يتكر في خلقه بفكر خاضع له مستغيث به ليوفقه لدينه، وعدل عن طاعته، ويفتح عليه باب الحق، لكن مال إلى الدنيا ركونا إليها ورغبة في شهوات نفسه فوكل إلى نفسه ، ولم يعصمه من عدوه ; إذ لم يتضرع إليه ، ولا رغب في غير الذي مال إليه ، وبالله نستعين.
And some of them said: No, rather he was harmed by it, so he pushed it away from himself, and thus mixed with it like one who is afflicted with mud; when he attempts to get out, he sinks further in. Motion is from light, and stillness from its opposite, since they are opposites. So they affirmed two origins: light and darkness, and two branches: motion and sensation from light, and stillness and lack of sensation from darkness, without explaining anything beyond light and darkness. The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: We have mentioned their sayings so that you may learn God's wrath against one who prefers His enmity, and does not reflect on His creation with a thought submissive to Him, seeking His help that He may guide him to His religion, and turns away from His obedience, and (hoping that) He opens for him the door of truth; rather he inclined to the world, leaning towards it and desiring the pleasures of his soul, so He left him to himself, and did not protect him from his enemy, because he did not supplicate to Him, nor did he desire other than what he inclined to. And with God we seek help.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage is a report and a moral commentary. Maturidi first recounts the dualist (Manichaean) doctrine: they posit two eternal principles, light (nūr) and darkness (ẓulma), with their respective attributes—motion and sensation from light, stillness and insensibility from darkness. The metaphor of one stuck in mud illustrates how light became entangled with darkness. The author then adds his own editorial remark: he cites these views so that readers may recognize God's wrath against those who reject Him and rely on their own reasoning, turning away from supplication. The lesson is that such people are left to their own devices and cannot attain truth.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi presents the dualist view without criticism, so he might agree with it.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states he mentions it to teach about God's wrath and to warn against relying on one's own reason without Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The dualists tried to escape darkness but only sank deeper. Where in your life have you struggled against a temptation or trial by your own effort, only to entangle yourself further? Will you now pause and beg Allah to open the door of truth for you?
