The Impossibility of a Finite Middle Between Infinite Extremes
Maturidi refutes the finitude of the middle principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Marcionite claim that the middle principle is finite while the extremes are infinite, and why this leads to the middle being subsumed under the infinite.

Core Idea
A finite middle between two infinites cannot be a distinct principle.

Maturidi continues his refutation of the Marcionites by attacking their claim that the middle being is finite while the two extremes are infinite. He shows that a finite entity cannot stand as a separate principle when it is contained within the infinite—like a short person compared to a tall one. This argument undermines their dualistic framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi first reports the Marcionite claim before refuting it.
2
Identify the Marcionite claim
The Marcionites claimed that the middle (the creator) is finite while the two extremes (good and evil principles) are infinite. Find this statement in the passage.
The passage begins with 'Then they said: They made the middle finite and the other two infinite.'
3
Analyze the analogy
Maturidi uses an analogy: the finite is to the infinite as the short is to the tall. Consider how this shows that the finite middle is subsumed under the infinite extremes, making it irrelevant as a separate principle.
This analogy is crucial for understanding the logical flaw.
4
Draw the conclusion
Reflect on why this argument invalidates the Marcionite position. The middle cannot be a distinct, independent being if it is finite and surrounded by infinite realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا: جعلوا الواسط متناهيا والآخرين غير متناهيين، والمتناهي تحت غير المتناهى؛ لأنه كالمقصر عن تمام ما ليس بمتناه كالقصير من الطويل. والإنسان إن كانت الحياة التي في البدن فهي محسه للبدن مستعملة له، فيجب أن يكون الواسط هو الذي له تدبير الغالي والسافل، وهو المستعمل لهما، فيصير الإله في الحقيقة واحدا أو يبطل الامتزاج وما ذكر من الخيال خطأ؛ إذ لا إنسان يعرف تدبير... ثم إشارته إلى الامتزاج ابتدائه، ولا أصلح ما فسد منه، ولا دفع ما حل به، ثبت أن المدبر واحد، وهو غير الذي ذكر، وأن الذي ذكر تحت تدبير الواحد. ثم لا فرق بين أن يحدث مزاجا لم يكن لا عن أصل هو امتزاج وبين أن يحدث ..... لم يكن لا عن أصل البينونة، ثم لا فرق بين إمكان تغير قد تم إلى احتمال الحوادث بعد أن لم تكن كذلك بقدرة قادر وبين أن يكون الحوادث به، لا يقلب القديم إلى معنى الحديث؛ إذ هما جميعا في البعد عن البصر في الوهم واحد. وبالله المعونة والنجاة.
Then they said: They made the middle finite and the other two infinite, and the finite is under the infinite, because it is like something falling short of the completeness of what is not finite, like the short compared to the tall. And if the human being is the life that is in the body, then it is the sense of the body using it. Then it must be that the middle is the one that has the planning of the high and the low, and it is the user of them. Then the god would in reality be one, or the mixing is invalidated. And what was mentioned of imagination is wrong, because no human being knows the planning of... then its indication to the mixing of its beginning, nor did it set right what was corrupted from it, nor did it repel what befell it. So it is established that the planner is one, and He is other than what was mentioned, and that what was mentioned is under the planning of the One. Then there is no difference between bringing about a mixing that was not from an origin that is mixing and between bringing about ..... that was not from an origin of separation. Then there is no difference between the possibility of a change that has been completed to the capacity to receive events after they were not like that by the power of a powerful being, and between that events are from Him. He does not turn the eternal into the meaning of the temporal; because both are equal in being far from sight in the estimation. And with Allah is the help and salvation.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage contains two related refutations. First, Maturidi reports a Marcionite claim that they made the middle finite and the extremes infinite, and then argues that the finite is subsumed under the infinite, making the middle irrelevant. Second, he examines the Marcionite identification of the human with life in the body. If life is the sense of the body, then the middle should be the planner of both high and low, leading to a single god or the collapse of mixing. Since no human is observed to originate mixing, repair corruption, or repel harm, the true planner is one. Maturidi then draws analogies to show that bringing about existence from nothing does not change the eternal into temporal, and both possibilities are equally beyond sense perception.

Key Terms
المتناهي
غير المتناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The middle principle can be finite while the extremes are infinite as separate independent realities.
Correction: A finite entity is subsumed under the infinite and cannot be a distinct principle, as shown by the analogy of short and tall.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use to illustrate the relationship between the finite and the infinite?
A) A short person and a tall person
B) Light and darkness
C) A seed and a tree
D) Body and soul

Key Takeaway
A finite entity cannot be an independent principle if it is encompassed by the infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every finite thing is under the infinite, why do you still act as if your plans and efforts are independent of Allah’s will? In what area of your life today will you consciously acknowledge that you are 'under the infinite'?
?
The Marcionites thought a finite being could mediate between infinities. Where in your own thinking do you place something finite—like your status, wealth, or even your own piety—as a mediator between you and Allah? What would it mean to utterly erase that mediator and stand directly under the Infinite?
?
Reflect on the analogy of the short compared to the tall: your entire existence falls short of the completeness of Allah. How does this recognition change the way you approach your next act of worship? Will you pray today with a palpable sense of being encompassed?

The Planner Must Be One: Refuting the Life-in-Body Argument
Maturidi refutes the life-body identification and proves a single planner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if the human is identified with life in the body, then the planner of both high and low must be one, and that no human can originate or repair mixing, proving the existence of a single divine planner.

Core Idea
No human can plan the initial mixing; therefore, the true planner is one.

Maturidi turns to the Marcionite identification of the human with the life in the body. He shows that if this life is the sense of the body and uses it, then the middle should be the planner of both high and low, leading to either a single god or the collapse of their mixing theory. Moreover, since no human is observed to originate mixing, repair corruption, or repel harm, it is established that the planner is one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage contains a chain of reasoning; follow the logical steps.
2
Identify the Marcionite assumption
The Marcionites assumed that the human is the life in the body. Find this assumption in the passage.
The passage says 'if the human being is the life that is in the body'.
3
Follow the implication
If life is the sense of the body using it, then the middle (the human) should plan both high and low. That forces a choice: either there is one true god or their mixing theory collapses.
This leads to the conclusion that 'the god would in reality be one'.
4
Consider the observational evidence
No human is observed to know the planning of mixing, set right corruption, or repel harm. This proves that the true planner is one, not the human.
Maturidi uses empirical observation to reject the Marcionite view.
5
Reflect on the analogies
Maturidi draws two analogies: bringing about mixing from nothing is like bringing about separation from nothing; and a change in potential to receive events does not turn the eternal into temporal. Both show that divine action does not change God's essence.
These analogies reinforce that creation from nothing is within God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Magian Doctrine on the Origin of Iblis
Maturidi reports and refutes the Magian story of God's creation of Satan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Magian account of the origin of Satan and why Maturidi considers it more problematic than dualism.

Core Idea
The Magian myth makes God the direct source of evil, contradicting divine transcendence.

Imam Maturidi reports a fascinating yet problematic Magian account of how Satan came into being. According to this story, God's own thought or fear gave rise to Iblis, who then becomes the source of all evil. Maturidi then refutes this by showing it is worse than dualism, because it makes God directly responsible for evil. This passage illustrates the importance of safeguarding Allah's attributes of power and goodness from any imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Magian story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Focus on the sequence of events as described by the Magians.
There are two versions reported; note the differences.
2
Identify the two versions
The first version says God admired His creation, feared opposition, and then thought, producing Iblis. The second says God was struck by an evil eye, turned, and saw Iblis, then made a truce.
Both versions share the idea that God is somehow involved in Iblis's origin.
3
Note the attribution of good and evil
The Magians attribute all evil to Iblis and all good to Allah. This is a dualistic tendency, but with a twist: God is the indirect cause of Iblis.
4
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi states that if this is their real belief, they are worse than dualists because dualists posit two eternal principles, whereas the Magians make God the direct cause of evil through His thought or fear.
This refutation highlights the importance of God's transcendence.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why Maturidi would include this refutation in a work on tawhid. What does it teach about Allah's attributes?
The passage guards against anthropomorphism and preserves God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: قالت المجوس: أعجب الله حسن خلقه فتخوف ما يضاده فيه، فتفكر في ذلك فكرة فحدث منها إبليس. وقال بعضهم: أصابته بعينة فالتفت وراءه فرأى إبليس، فصالحه على أن يمهله إلى مدة، ووادعه على ذلك، حتى إذا مضت المدة أهلكه الله، فكان من إبليس كل شر، ومن الله كل خير. وهذا الذي حكوا إن كان هو قولهم في الحقيقة فهم شر من جميع الثنوية؛ لأن الثنوية قالت باثنين لما رأوا خلق الشيء لا عن شيء غير متصور في الوهم.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The Magians said: Allah admired the beauty of His creation, then He feared what would oppose Him in it, so He thought a thought, and from it Iblis came into being. And some of them said: He was struck by an evil eye, so He turned behind Him and saw Iblis, so He made a truce with Him to give Him respite for a period, and He made peace with Him on that, so that when the period passed, Allah would destroy him. Thus from Iblis is every evil, and from Allah is every good. And this that they narrated, if it is truly their saying, then they are worse than all the dualists, because the dualists said there are two when they saw that creating a thing from nothing is not conceivable in the imagination.
Source pages p. 771

Commentary
This passage reports a Magian myth about the origin of Satan. According to one version, God admired His creation, then feared opposition, thought, and from that thought Iblis was born. Another version says God was struck by an evil eye, turned, and saw Iblis, then made a truce until the end of time when God would destroy him. The Magians then attribute all evil to Iblis and all good to God. Maturidi refutes this by stating that if this is their real belief, they are worse than dualists because dualists at least posit two eternal principles, whereas the Magians make God the direct cause of evil through His thought or fear, contradicting His goodness and power.

Key Terms
المجوس
إبليس
الثنوية
الخير
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Satan came into being from a thought or fear in God.
Correction: Allah is free from any imperfection; He does not fear or think in a human way. The Magian story anthropomorphizes God and contradicts His transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the Magian story, how did Iblis come into being?
A) From a thought of God
B) From a battle between light and darkness
C) From a fallen angel
D) From a mixture of elements

Key Takeaway
The Magian story of Satan's origin is refuted by Maturidi as worse than dualism because it attributes the cause of evil to God Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even silently, blamed God for a difficulty or evil in your life, similar to how the Magians attributed the origin of Iblis to God's thought? What would it take for you to fully entrust both good and evil to His perfect wisdom?
?
The Magians claimed to honor God by saying all good is from Him, yet they slandered Him by making Him the source of evil. Where in your own speech or thoughts do you risk 'defending' God in a way that actually diminishes His transcendence?
?
Maturidi shows that even polytheists can be more correct than those who attribute evil to God. What does this teach you about the seriousness of guarding your understanding of God's attributes? Will you commit to studying divine transcendence until your heart is free from any trace of this error?

The Majus Claim and Maturidi's First Refutation
Maturidi recounts the Majus myth and argues they are worse than dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Majus claim by showing that their own myth makes God the origin of evil, making them worse than dualists.

Core Idea
The Majus' attempt to separate good from evil actually makes God the source of evil.

The Majus (Zoroastrians) believed that good and evil come from two separate principles, with God being only good. Maturidi begins by recounting their myth and then delivers a devastating blow: if their myth is true, they are worse than the dualists because they make God directly responsible for the evil thought that produced Iblis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Majus myth
Read the Arabic passage describing the Majus belief about God, Iblis, and the truce. Notice the details that Maturidi will critique.
The myth involves God fearing opposition, thinking, and Iblis being created from that thought.
2
Understand the dualist position
Maturidi explains why dualists (al-thanawiyya) posited two eternal principles: they couldn't conceive creation from nothing, and they thought attributing both good and evil to one being would make that being both praised and blamed.
3
See the contradiction in the Majus' view
Maturidi argues that the Majus accept creation from nothing, yet their own myth makes evil originate from God's thought. Thus they are worse than dualists, because they make God the source of evil while trying to separate it.
The key point: the evil thought is evil, and Iblis from it is evil, so both good and evil come from God in their story.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : قالت المجوس : أعجب الله حسن خلقه فتخوف ما يضاده فيه، فتفكر فى ذلك فكرة، فحدث منها إبليس . وقال وقال بعضهم : أصابته بعينة فالتفت وراءه فرأى ابليس، فصالحه، على أن يمهله إلى مدة، و وادعه على ذلك، حتى إذا مضت المدة أهلكه الله، فكان من إبليس كل شر، ومن الله كل خير وهذا الذي حكوا إن كان هو قولهم في الحقيقة فهم شر من جميع الثنوية؛ لأن الثنوية قالت باثنين لما رأوا خلق الشيء لا عن شيء غير متصور في الوهم. عظم عليهم القول بحدث العالم لا عن شيء ، ثم رأوا العالم مشتملا على خير وشر موصوفا كل من فعله الخير والعدل بالصفات المحمودة، ومن فعله الشر والجور بالصفات المذمومة، استعظموا نسبتهما إلى الواحد، فيكون واحدا محمودا مذموما بما عليه العرف، فقالوا باثنين قديمين. والمجوس قد استجازوا حدث العالم لا عن شيء وأصل، وإنما عظم عندهم وصف من منه الخيرات بفعل الشر، لم ألزموه فعل شر الشر صيروه أمه؛ إذ الفكرة الردية شر، وما حدث وهو إبليس شر وكان منه الأمران جميعا، وهو السبب الذي دعاهم إلى القول باثنين فتناقض قولهم. مع ما لا يؤمن منه حدوث الفكر رقنا بعد وقت، فيكون جميع الشر بذلك، وإن أريد إحالة ذا دل وجوده مرة على دفع الإحالة إلا أن يقول بالخير، فلعل بداه عن الفكرة التي هي شر. على أنه إذا وادعه على الترك إلى تلك المدة فأما أن لم يعلم أنه يعمل ما يعمل من الشر والجهل شر فهو شر آخر، أو علم فتركه على ما علم من الفساد به، فذلك منه شر، ومثله إما أن يكون علم من قبل ما يعمل فكره، ففكر على العلم بما يكون منه وهو شر، [وإما] لم يعلم، والجهل شر. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون [قادرا] على منع ابليس وقهره أو لا، فإن قدر ثم أمهله ليفسد الخلق فهو شر عندهم وإن لم يقدر فلا يكون العاجز رب العالمين. مع ما يقال : ثم علم أن إبليس عند المدة يفى له بالذى وعد ، وفاء الوعد خير وحق ، فإذا يكون من الشر خير . مع ما كان هذا لازما له ، إنه إذا كان ممن هو أصل الخير يحى الشر : فنعكس عليهم ونجعل كل خير من إبليس ، وكل شر من غيره . وبعد ، فكيف يأمن بالقدرة / عليه في الوقت الذى لم يكن لإبليس غير نفسه عون والذى به ذلك
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The Majus said: 'God admired His beautiful creation, then feared what opposed Him in it, so He thought a thought, and from that Iblis was created.' And some of them said: 'An evil eye struck Him, so He turned behind Him and saw Iblis, then He made peace with him by giving him a respite until a time, and He made a truce with him on that, so that when the time passed, God would destroy him. So from Iblis came all evil, and from God all good.' If this is their true statement, then they are worse than all the dualists; because the dualists said there are two [principles] when they thought that creation from nothing was inconceivable. The statement that the world originated from nothing was great to them, then they saw the world including good and evil, each one who does good and justice described with praiseworthy attributes, and the one who does evil and injustice described with blameworthy attributes. It seemed great to them to attribute both to the One, so He would be one praised and blamed according to custom. So they said there are two eternals. But the Majus permit the origination of the world from nothing and an origin. What seems great to them is the description of the one from whom good comes as doing evil. They did not force upon Him the evil of evil? they made Him its mother? For the evil thought is evil, and what occurred, Iblis, is evil, and from Him are both matters. That is the reason that led them to say two, so their statement is contradictory. With what is not safe from the occurrence of thought anew after a time, so all evil would be by that. If intended to preclude this, its existence once indicates the denial of preclusion unless He says by good, perhaps its beginning is from the thought which is evil. If He made a truce with him until that time, then either He did not know that he would do what he does of evil and ignorance – evil – so it is another evil, or He knew and left him on what He knew of corruption through him, so that is evil from Him. Likewise, either He knew beforehand what he would do, so He thought? So He thought based on knowledge of what would happen from him – which is evil – or He did not know, and ignorance is evil. Then it is not free from either being able to prevent Iblis and overpower him or not. If He was able then gave him respite to corrupt creation, that is evil according to them. If He was not able, then the incapable is not the Lord of the worlds. Additionally, it is said: then He knew that Iblis would fulfill what he promised at the time – fulfillment of promise is good and right – so from evil good would come. This is necessary for him: if He is the one who is the origin of good and gives life to evil? Then we invert against them and make every good from Iblis and every evil from other than him. Then how can He be safe with power over him at a time when Iblis has no support except himself? And that by which that...
Source pages p. 771

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Majus (Zoroastrians) who claimed that good and evil come from two separate eternal principles, with God being the source of good only. Maturidi first relates their myth: that God admired creation, feared opposition, thought, and from that thought Iblis (Satan) was created, leading to a truce. Maturidi then argues that if this is their belief, they are worse than the dualists (al-thanawiyya). The dualists at least posited two principles because they could not conceive creation from nothing, whereas the Majus accept creation from nothing but then attribute evil to a thought from God, implying evil originates from God. Maturidi points out their contradiction: they allow the world to come from nothing, but then balk at attributing evil and good to one being. They tried to avoid that by two principles, but their own story makes God the origin of evil (the thought that produced Iblis). Maturidi then raises multiple objections: (1) If God did not know Iblis' future actions, that is ignorance (evil); if He knew and allowed it, that is evil. (2) If God has power to prevent Iblis and did not, that is evil; if He cannot, He is incapable. (3) The truce implies God knows Iblis will fulfill his promise (good), so from evil comes good, which contradicts the absolute separation. (4) Maturidi then turns the tables: if God is the origin of evil, then by the same logic, one could say all good is from Iblis. The passage concludes with a question about God's power over Iblis.

الثنوية
إبليس
الفكرة الردية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Zoroastrians believe in two co-eternal principles without any connection.
Correction: The Majus myth actually makes God the origin of evil through a thought, so they don't keep the principles truly separate.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction Maturidi identifies in the Majus' belief as presented in this passage?
A) They deny creation from nothing yet posit two eternals.
B) They accept creation from nothing but then attribute the origin of evil to God's thought.
C) They claim Iblis is eternally evil while God is eternally good.
D) They believe in a truce between God and Iblis.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Majus myth, intended to separate good and evil, actually makes God the source of evil through His thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or see evil in the world, does your heart ever subtly separate God from His sovereignty over that evil, like the Majus? What would it mean for you to fully affirm that all that occurs is by God's will without attributing evil to Him in a flawed way? Will you today repent of any thought that limits God's authority over good and evil?
?
The Majus myth tried to protect God's goodness but ended up making Him the source of evil. Are there any beliefs you hold—perhaps without realizing—that you think protect God's honor but actually diminish His perfection? Will you examine your heart and ask God to purify your understanding of His attributes?
?
Maturidi shows that the dualists' reasoning failed, but the Majus' reasoning was even worse. Do you ever rely on human reasoning to solve theological puzzles instead of submitting to revelation? Will you commit to humbly accepting what Allah has revealed about Himself, even when your mind struggles with it?

Maturidi's Detailed Logical Objections to the Majus
Maturidi presents a series of logical dilemmas based on the Majus' own narrative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the series of logical dilemmas Maturidi raises against the Majus belief, involving knowledge, power, and the truce with Iblis.

Core Idea
If God knew and allowed evil, or didn't know and couldn't stop it, the Majus' position collapses.

Maturidi continues his refutation by examining the implications of the Majus myth more closely. He poses dilemmas about God's knowledge, power, and the truce. If God knew Iblis would do evil and allowed it, that is evil; if He didn't know, that is ignorance. If He could prevent it and didn't, that is evil; if He couldn't, He is not omnipotent. He also points out that the truce implies promise-keeping, which is good, so evil produces good—contradicting their separation.

Guided Path
1
Explore the knowledge dilemma
Maturidi argues: either God knew Iblis would do evil and allowed it (evil), or He did not know (ignorance, also evil). Consider both possibilities.
This dilemma is key to showing that the Majus cannot escape attributing evil to God.
2
Explore the power dilemma
Either God could prevent Iblis and did not (evil), or He could not (then He is not all-powerful). Both options are problematic for the Majus.
The Majus' concept of God is incompatible with the truce narrative.
3
Analyze the truce argument
The truce implies God knows Iblis will keep his promise. Promise-keeping is good, so from evil (the truce) comes good. This undermines absolute separation.
Maturidi also suggests inverting the roles: if God is origin of evil, then all good could be from Iblis.
4
Reflect on the closing question
The passage ends with a rhetorical question about God's security in power. Maturidi challenges the Majus to explain how God could be safe when Iblis, with no support, opposes Him.
This emphasizes the weakness of the Majus' position.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Majus (Zoroastrians) who claimed that good and evil come from two separate eternal principles, with God being the source of good only. According to Maturidi's dilemma about power, what does the Majus' truce imply about God?
A) God is all-powerful and uses the truce to test humans.
B) Either God could prevent Iblis and didn't (evil), or He couldn't (not all-powerful).
C) God has no control over Iblis and is therefore not divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something painful or unjust happens to you, do you find yourself instinctively blaming a source other than God? How does Maturidi's argument call you to examine that tendency and trust that nothing is outside of His will, even if you cannot understand it?
?
Are there areas of your life where you try to 'compartmentalize' good and evil, attributing your successes to God and your failures to other factors? How would your faith change if you truly believed that everything—both what you call good and evil—comes from Him?
?
Maturidi demonstrates that separating God from evil leads to contradictions about His knowledge and power. Do you ever secretly doubt God's power or goodness when you face tragedy? What would it mean for you to fully accept that He is both all-powerful and all-wise, even in the darkest moments?

The Origin of Dualism: Good and Evil Cannot Be from One
Maturidi introduces the dualist dilemma and their initial solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dualists felt compelled to posit two eternal principles and the initial contradiction in their reasoning.

Core Idea
Dualism is born from the difficulty of attributing both good and evil to one God.

The dualists began by accepting that the world originated from nothing. Yet they observed good and evil in the world and could not accept that a single deity could be both praised for good and blamed for evil. This led them to propose two eternal principles. In this lesson, we explore their starting point and the first flaw in their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the dualist dilemma
Read the passage describing how the dualists were troubled by attributing both good and evil to one God. Note their initial acceptance of creation from nothing.
2
Identify the two attributes
The passage says that the one who does good is described with praiseworthy attributes, and the one who does evil with blameworthy ones. They could not combine these in one being.
This is the core of their reasoning.
3
See Maturidi's critique of the Magians
Maturidi points out that the Magians still allow the origination of the world from nothing, but they are especially troubled by the good principle doing evil. Then he exposes their contradiction: evil thought and Iblis are evil, and if both matters come from one source, their dualism collapses.
Notice the key phrase 'so their saying is contradictory'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عظم عليهم القول بحدث العالم لا عن شيء، ثم رأوا العالم مشتملا على خير وشر، موصوفا كل من فعله الخير والعدل بالصفات المحمودة، ومن فعله الشر والجور بالصفات المذمومة، استعظموا نسبتهما إلى الواحد، فيكون واحدا محمودا مذموما بما عليه العرف، فقالوا باثنين قديمين. والمجوس قد استجازوا حدث العالم لا عن شيء وأصل، وإنما عظم عندهم وصف من منه الخيرات بفعل الشر، لم ألزموه فعل شر الشر صيروه أمه؛ إذ الفكرة الردية شر، وما حدث وهو إبليس شر وكان منه الأمران جميعا، وهو السبب الذي دعاهم إلى القول باثنين فتناقض قولهم. مع ما لا يؤمن منه حدوث الفكر رقنا بعد وقت، فيكون جميع الشر بذلك. وإن أريد إحالة ذا دل وجوده مرة على دفع الإحالة إلا أن يقول بالخير، فلعل بداه عن الفكرة التي هي شر. على أنه إذا وادعه على الترك إلى تلك المدة فأما أن لم يعلم أنه يعمل ما يعمل من الشر والجهل شر فهو شر آخر، أو علم فتركه على ما علم من الفساد به، فذلك منه شر، ومثله إما أن يكون علم من قبل ما يعمل فكره، ففكر على العلم بما يكون منه وهو شر، [وإما] لم يعلم، والجهل شر. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون منه وهو شر، [وإما] لم يعلم، والجهل شر. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون [قادرا] على منع إبليس وقهره أو لا، فإن قدر ثم أمهله ليفسد الخلق فهو شر عندهم وإن لم يقدر فلا يكون العاجز رب العالمين. مع ما يقال: ثم علم أن إبليس عند المدة يفى له بالذى وعد، وفاء الوعد خير وحق، فإذا يكون من الشر خير. مع ما كان هذا لازما له، إنه إذا كان ممن هو أصل الخير يحى الشر: فنعكس عليهم ونجعل كل خير من إبليس، وكل شر من غيره. وبعد، فكيف يأمن بالقدرة عليه في الوقت الذي لم يكن لإبليس غير نفسه عون والذي به كان كل الأشياء أعوان، ثم اختلط خلقه الذين هم أعوانه بالذين هم أعوان الله عن المعونة عليه، جل الله عما وصفه الملحدون في منعهم.
They found it difficult to say that the world originated from nothing, then they saw the world containing good and evil, each one who does good and justice described with praiseworthy attributes, and he who does evil and injustice described with blameworthy attributes. They considered it great to attribute both to the One, so that He would be one praised and blamed according to custom. So they said there are two eternal (principles). The Magians have allowed the origination of the world from nothing and an origin, but what is great to them is describing the one from whom good things come with doing evil. They did not commit him to doing evil of evil, so they made him its mother? For evil thought is evil, and what originated, which is Iblis, is evil, and from him were both matters. That is the reason that called them to say two eternal (principles), so their saying is contradictory. Moreover, there is no security from the occurrence of thought after time, so all evil would be by that. If it is intended to make this impossible, its existence once indicates the prevention of impossibility, except that he says by good, perhaps its beginning is from the thought that is evil. Regarding when he made a truce with him to abandon until that period: either he did not know that he would do what he does of evil, and ignorance is evil, so it is another evil; or he knew and left him upon what he knew of corruption by it, so that is from him evil. Likewise, either he knew beforehand what his thought would do, so he thought upon knowledge of what will be from him, and that is evil, or he did not know, and ignorance is evil. Then it is not free that he [؟] from him is evil, or he did not know, and ignorance is evil. Then it is not free that he is [able] to prevent Iblis and overpower him or not. If he is able then gives him respite to corrupt creation, that is evil according to them; if he is not able, then the incapable is not the Lord of the worlds. With what is said: then he knew that Iblis at the term would fulfill what he promised, and fulfilling a promise is good and right, so then from evil comes good. This was incumbent upon him: if he is the one who is the origin of good, he does evil. So we reverse upon them and we make every good from Iblis and every evil from other than him. Then how is he secure with power over him at the time when Iblis had no helper other than himself, and with which everything was helpers, then his creation who are his helpers mixed with those who are the helpers of God, due to the help against him – exalted be God above what the atheists describe, in refuting them.
Source pages p. 772

Commentary
This passage refutes the dualist position by exposing its internal contradictions. Maturidi begins by stating the dualists' initial difficulty: they accept creation from nothing but see the world containing both good and evil, and they cannot attribute both to a single deity without that deity being both praised and blamed. Thus they posit two eternal principles. Then he criticizes the Magians who allow creation from nothing but still object to attributing evil to the good principle. Maturidi points out that their reasoning is inconsistent because evil originates from evil thought and Iblis, and if both good and evil come from the same source, their position collapses. He then examines the scenario of a truce between the good deity and Iblis: if the good deity does not know Iblis's future evil, that is ignorance (evil); if he knows and permits it, that is also evil. Thus the good deity cannot be good if he allows evil. Moreover, if he is able to prevent Iblis and does not, that is evil; if he is unable, he cannot be God. The argument also reverses: one could claim all good comes from Iblis and evil from the good deity, demolishing the dualist distinction. The passage concludes that the dualists' god is not truly God, as he depends on others for help, and God is exalted above their descriptions.

Key Terms
قديمان
خير
شر
مجوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the dualists reject a single creator according to the passage?
A) They found it difficult to believe the world originated from nothing.
B) They saw the world containing both good and evil and could not attribute both to one being.
C) They believed two gods were necessary for creation.
D) They wanted to avoid describing God with blameworthy attributes.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' solution of two eternal principles is contradictory because they allow both good and evil to originate from a single source through evil thought and Iblis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience suffering or evil, do you ever find yourself questioning God's goodness or silently positing a 'second source' of evil in your heart? How does Maturidi's argument expose that inner dualism as a contradiction to true Tawhid?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but do you live as though some areas of your life (like your anger, envy, or pride) are outside His control? What would it mean for you to repent of that subtle dualism and submit every part of your existence to His oneness?
?
The Magians allowed evil thought to come from an evil source. Have you ever excused your own evil thoughts by blaming an external force or circumstance? How can you take full ownership of your inner states in light of the absolute unity of God's sovereignty?

The Truce with Iblis and the Problem of Divine Knowledge and Power
Maturidi examines the truce between the good deity and Iblis to show the problem of knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the scenario of a truce between the good deity and Iblis to expose further contradictions in dualism.

Core Idea
If the good deity knows or permits evil, He is not good; if He cannot prevent it, He is not God.

Maturidi takes the dualists' own assumptions and examines what happens when the good deity makes a truce with Iblis. He shows that whether the deity knows about Iblis's evil actions or not, the deity is implicated in evil. Moreover, if the deity is able to prevent Iblis but refrains, that is evil; if unable, He is not omnipotent. This lesson explores these logical traps.

Guided Path
1
Read the truce scenario
Read the passage that describes the truce between the good deity and Iblis. Note that all evil originates from Iblis, and the good deity is not directly doing evil.
2
Analyze the knowledge alternatives
The passage considers two possibilities: the deity did not know what Iblis would do (ignorance is evil) or He knew and allowed it (that is also evil). In both cases, the deity is implicated in evil.
Ignorance is a defect, and permitting known evil is also evil.
3
Analyze the power alternatives
Next, consider whether the deity is able to prevent Iblis or not. If able but does not, that is evil; if unable, He cannot be God.
This trap leaves no escape for the dualist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a truce shows the good deity is merciful or wise, but Maturidi shows it leads to moral or logical failure.
Correction: The truce scenario actually forces the conclusion that the good deity is either evil or not divine.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the good deity is able to prevent Iblis but gives him respite, what does that imply?
A) The deity is merciful.
B) The deity is evil.
C) The deity is powerful.
D) The deity is ignorant.

Key Takeaway
The truce scenario exposes that the dualists' good deity cannot escape being implicated in evil, whether through ignorance, permission, or inability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evil or suffering in your own life, do you ever find yourself doubting God's knowledge or power? How does this passage's logic confront those doubts? What specific thought will you hold onto next time you struggle with this?
?
If you were forced to defend God's goodness in the face of evil, would you be tempted to use excuses like 'it's a test' or 'free will'? How does Maturidi's argument show that those excuses can still implicate God in evil? What will you say instead to someone who is suffering?
?
This passage shows that ignorance or permission of evil makes a being not good. Examine your own life: where have you known about an evil and done nothing, or remained ignorant of suffering you could have prevented? What will you do today to change that?

The Reversal and the True God
Maturidi presents the reversal argument and the problem of divine self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final contradiction Maturidi exposes: the dualists' god is not self-sufficient and the argument can be reversed.

Core Idea
If even good can come from evil, the dualist distinction collapses entirely.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by pointing out that from evil (Iblis) good can arise, and one could just as well claim all good comes from Iblis and all evil from the good deity. Moreover, the good deity depends on helpers against Iblis, which contradicts His divinity. This lesson presents the decisive closing arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the part about the promise
Read the passage where Maturidi points out that Iblis fulfilling a promise is good, so good can come from the evil source.
2
Understand the reversal
If good can come from Iblis, one can reverse the attribution: all good from Iblis, all evil from the other. This destroys the dualist distinction.
This is a logical counter-attack.
3
Note the problem of helpers
The good deity needs helpers against Iblis, and his creation is mixed with Iblis's helpers. This shows He is not self-sufficient, unlike the true God.
The passage ends with exalting God above such descriptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the dualists' god needing helpers?
A) It shows He is merciful.
B) It shows He is not self-sufficient.
C) It shows He created Iblis.
D) It shows He is powerful.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' good deity is not truly God because He is not self-sufficient and the argument can be reversed, showing the bankruptcy of dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that even from evil—like Iblis fulfilling a promise—good can come. Reflect on a time in your life when you saw good emerge from a situation you considered purely evil. Did you acknowledge that as from Allah, or did you credit 'luck' or your own planning? How often do you attribute good to your own effort and evil to external circumstances, just as the dualists did? When will you start attributing every good to Allah alone and seek His help in all matters?
?
The good deity in the argument is not self-sufficient; He needs helpers. Do you sometimes act as if you are 'good' or self-reliant, depending on your own resources, wealth, or relationships, forgetting that true self-sufficiency belongs only to Allah? Examine your trust: are your 'helpers' (money, status, friends) security for you? What does that reveal about your concept of divine unity? Will you repent and place your trust solely in the Self-Sufficient One?
?
Maturidi reverses the dualist claim: maybe all good comes from Iblis and all evil from the 'good' deity. This exposes the fragility of their thinking. Have you ever held a belief that, if reversed, would sound equally absurd? Are you willing to examine your own deeply held assumptions about good and evil in your daily life—like who you blame and who you thank—or do you cling to them out of habit? What would it cost you to truly submit every judgment to Allah's revelation?

Refuting the Truce Objection: Evil's Purpose Proves One Creator
Maturidi refutes the truce objection and argues from harmful creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the dualist claim that the good deity made a truce with Iblis for beneficial purposes, and how this actually supports the existence of a single all-powerful Creator.

Core Idea
A truce implies weakness; harmful things serve a purpose under one wise Creator.

The dualists argued that the good deity made a truce with Iblis for some benefit, thus explaining evil. Maturidi dismantles this by showing that such a truce would imply fear and ignorance in the deity, contradicting His divinity. He then compares harmful pests that nonetheless benefit creation, proving a single wise manager behind all things.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the truce
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi responds to the objection that the truce had some beneficial interest.
The key point is that fear of an opponent would make the deity ignorant and weak.
2
Understand the analogy with harmful pests
Maturidi compares the truce to harmful pests: they exist for some benefit under a wise manager. Consider how this analogy undermines the need for a separate evil principle.
Every harmful thing ultimately benefits some creation, so it cannot be attributed to an independent evil force.
3
Trace the conclusion
See how Maturidi concludes that claiming an independent originator of evil is far from the truth. The truce argument actually proves monotheism.
The passage ends with 'ثبت أن القول بانفراد منشئ الشر بعيد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : الموادعة كانت لبعض المصالح ، فمثله الهوام الضارة والأشياء المؤذية . وبعد ، فإن تخوفه من يضاده يوجب الجهل بأنه رب كل شيء ، وكذلك إصابة العين ، فإذا ضر به العين ، ومن تقهره العين ، وتزيل قدرته ، وتدفع علمه رب بغيره لا بنفسه ، خالق بغيره ، فيلزم القول في معبودهم إنه عبد لا معبود لا شيء من تلك الجواهر المؤذية إلا وهى تنفع خلقا ، فلا تضر ولا تؤذى لأنفسها ، ولكن بمدير حكيم عليم جعلها بحيث تؤذي أحدا وتنفع ؛ ثبت أن القول بانفراد منشئ الشر بعيد .. الخلق الطبع ثم إن لم يكن فى خلق الشيء من غير شيء إلا خروجه من وسع وارتفاعه عن التصور فلا أحد امتنع عن القول بتحقيق مثله ؛ لأن إنشاء الجسم وكونه في الأرحام بالطبائع وحدوثه بحركات النجوم أو خروج العالم عن هذا الطر و امتزاج النور والظلمة ثم التباين خارج عن الوجه الذى [ذكر] . على أن حقيقة كل شيء من تأمله كذلك نجده ؛ لأنه ليس في النطفة ولا في جميع الأغذية ولا في الأرحام شيء من معانى البشر : ثم مما له من العقل والسمع والنظر ، فإنما ذلك خارج عن ذلك بتقدير عليم حكيم . وكذلك جميع الطبائع المختلفة أو جواهر الخير والشر لو خلى بينها وبين عملها ما ظهر بها جوهر ولا يمكن بها خلق ، بالكون بمثله أبعد عن التصور فى العقل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . فالقول وقد بينا وجه الحكمة فى خلق الجواهر المختلفة ، وأن فعل الله لا يوصف بذلك وإن أنشأها على ما عليه من قبح القبيح وحسن الحسن ، هو معنى الحكمة
And if they say: The truce was for some interests, then similar are the harmful pests and painful things. And moreover, his fear of one who opposes him necessitates ignorance that he is Lord of everything, and likewise the affliction of the eye: if he is harmed by the eye, and the one whom the eye overpowers, and removes his power, and repels his knowledge, a lord by another not by himself, a creator by another – then it follows that their worshipped one is a slave, not a worshipped. None of those harmful substances exist except that they benefit a creation, so they do not harm or hurt for themselves, but by a wise, knowing manager who made them so that they hurt one and benefit. It is established that the statement of the singularity of the originator of evil is far (from truth). Creation and nature: then if there is nothing in creating a thing from nothing except its going beyond capacity and its being above conceptualization, then no one refrained from saying the realization of its like. For the origination of a body and its being in the wombs by natures, and its occurrence by movements of the stars, or the world's coming out of this mode, and the mixing of light and darkness and then separation, is outside the way that [was mentioned]. However, the reality of everything – one who contemplates it finds it likewise: for there is nothing in the sperm nor in all foods nor in the wombs of the meanings of a human; then what he has of intellect, hearing, and sight – that comes from outside that by the decree of a wise, knowing (God). And likewise all the different natures or substances of good and evil – if left between them and their action, no substance would appear by them nor would creation be possible by them. The existence of something like that is further from conceptualization in the intellect, and there is no power except by God. So the statement – and we have explained the aspect of wisdom in the creation of different substances, and that God's action is not described by that, though He created them as they are of the ugliness of the ugly and the beauty of the beautiful – that is the meaning of wisdom.
Source pages p. 773

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by addressing the dualists' potential objection that the truce between the good deity and Iblis had some beneficial purpose. Maturidi compares this to harmful pests which nonetheless serve a purpose under a wise manager. He then argues that if the good deity fears an opponent, that implies ignorance and weakness, contradicting his divinity. Every harmful thing ultimately benefits some creation, so it cannot be attributed to an independent evil principle. The second part of the passage argues from the nature of creation: things originate from nothing, and their complexity (e.g., a human from sperm) cannot be explained by mere natures or chance; it requires a wise Creator. The different natures and substances of good and evil, if left to themselves, could not produce anything. Thus the claim of dualism is untenable. The passage concludes by stating that God's wisdom lies in creating things with their qualities of good and evil as they are.

إصابة العين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the good deity made a truce with Iblis for some beneficial purpose, implying a separate evil principle.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such a truce would imply fear and ignorance in the deity, contradicting His divinity. Instead, all harmful things serve a purpose under a single wise Creator.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be the consequence if the good deity feared an opponent?
A) It would show His mercy.
B) It would necessitate ignorance that He is Lord of everything.
C) It would prove the truce was necessary.
D) It would make Iblis the creator of evil.

Key Takeaway
The dualist truce argument actually supports monotheism because it implies a single wise manager of all things, including evil.

?

?
If you truly believed that everything—including the most painful events—is from the same single Creator who is all-wise, how would your immediate reaction to calamity differ from your typical response? What would you say to yourself in that moment?

Proof from Creation: Complexity Requires a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues from the nature of creation for a single wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the complexity and order in creation—from sperm to human, from natures to the world—cannot be explained by mere chance or natural forces but require a wise, knowing Creator.

Core Idea
Creation's complexity shows it must come from a wise Creator, not nature or chance.

Maturidi turns to the argument from creation. He points out that things come into existence from nothing, which is beyond human capacity. Moreover, the development of a human from sperm with intellect, hearing, and sight cannot be explained by natures or stars. Even the different natures and substances, if left to themselves, would produce nothing. Thus, a wise Creator is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the creation argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Maturidi begins by discussing the impossibility of creating from nothing by humans, but affirms it for God.
The phrase 'ليس في النطفة ... شيء من معاني البشر' is key.
2
Follow the examples of complexity
Maturidi gives examples: a human from sperm, the world from light/darkness, different natures. See how each points to a wise designer.
Each example shows that the outcome is not inherent in the raw materials.
3
Understand the conclusion on wisdom
The passage ends by explaining that God's wisdom lies in creating things with their qualities of good and evil as they are, not in any deficiency.
God's action is not described by ugliness or beauty; He simply creates.

Refutation of Dualism: No Helpers for God
Maturidi refutes dualism by arguing harmful things are managed by God alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of dualism by demonstrating that all harmful things are under God's control and serve a wise purpose.

Core Idea
Evil does not prove a second creator; all harm serves a wise purpose.

Maturidi confronts the dualist claim that evil must have a separate source. He argues that harmful creatures, the evil eye, and all injurious things are part of God's creation, managed by a wise director. This lesson unpacks his reasoning, showing how the existence of harm actually confirms God's sole lordship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the key terms like 'a'wan (helpers) and 'munshi' al-sharr' (originator of evil).
2
Identify the dualist claims being refuted
Maturidi refutes several dualist assertions: that there are helpers assisting God, that harmful creatures are the result of a truce, and that God fears an opponent. Find each of these in the passage.
These claims are central to the Majus/dualist worldview.
3
Understand the argument about the evil eye
Maturidi argues that if the evil eye could overpower God, it would itself be a lord by another. See how he uses this to show that nothing can diminish God's power or knowledge.
4
Conclude: no independent originator of evil
The passage concludes that all harmful things benefit creation and are directed by a wise manager. Therefore, the claim of a separate originator of evil is far from the truth.
This is Maturidi's key refutation of dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان كل الأشياء أعوان، ثم اختلط خلقه الذين هم أعوانه بالذين هم أعوان الله عن المعونة عليه، جل الله عما وصفه الملحدون في منعهم. وإن قالوا: الموادعة كانت لبعض المصالح، فمثله الهوام الضارة والأشياء المؤذية. وبعد، فإن تخوفه من يضاده يوجب الجهل بأنه رب كل شيء، وكذلك إصابة العين، فإذا ضر به العين، ومن تقهره العين، وتزيل قدرته، وتدفع علمه رب بغيره لا بنفسه، خالق بغيره، فيلزم القول في معبودهم إنه عبد لا معبود لا شيء من تلك الجواهر المؤذية إلا وهي تنفع خلقا، فلا تضر ولا تؤذى لأنفسها، ولكن بمدير حكيم عليم جعلها بحيث تؤذي أحدا وتنفع؛ ثبت أن القول بانفراد منشئ الشر بعيد.
All things were helpers, then his creatures who are his helpers mixed with those who are helpers of God regarding assistance against Him, exalted is God above what the atheists describe in their prevention. And if they said: the truce was for some interests, like harmful vermin and injurious things. And moreover, his fear of one who opposes him necessitates ignorance that he is Lord of everything, and likewise the evil eye, so if the eye harms him, and one whom the eye overpowers, and removes his power, and pushes his knowledge, a lord by another not by himself, a creator by another, then it follows that their object of worship is a slave not a worshipped. Nothing from those harmful substances except that they benefit creatures, so they do not harm nor injure for themselves, but by a wise, knowing manager who made them such that they harm one and benefit; so it is established that the statement of the singularity of the originator of evil is far.
Source pages p. 773

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist claims (likely Majus) who posit a separate source of evil. Maturidi argues that harmful things are not independent agents but are created by God with a purpose. He begins by dismissing the idea that there are helpers (a'wan) assisting God, as atheists claim. Then he addresses the objection that harmful creatures were created for some benefit (mawada'a), comparing them to harmful vermin. He then argues that if God feared an opponent, that would imply ignorance of being Lord of all—contradicting divinity. Similarly, the evil eye (al-'ayn) cannot overpower God. If something could remove His power or knowledge, that thing would be lord by another, not by itself. Thus, the objects of worship of the dualists are actually slaves, not gods. Nothing harmful exists purely for harm; they benefit creation. A wise manager (mudabbir) directs them. Therefore, the claim of an independent originator of evil (munshi' al-sharr) is far from the truth.

Key Terms
أعوان
الملحدون
الموادعة
الهوام الضارة
إصابة العين
منشئ الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil proves there must be a separate creator of evil (dualism).
Correction: Maturidi shows that harmful things are part of God's creation, serving a purpose under His wise management, so no second creator is needed.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the claim that God fears an opponent imply?
A) That God is all-powerful
B) That God is ignorant of being Lord of all
C) That God created evil independently
D) That harmful things are self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that all harmful things are under God's wise control, so there is no independent source of evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The evil eye is a test of faith. If you ever feel overwhelmed by envy or harm from others, does your reaction reveal a belief that some force can overpower God? What specific ayah or hadith will you memorize to reinforce that God alone controls all harm?

Refutation of the Ahad Report Objection
Maturidi refutes the ahad claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the claim that the Quran is transmitted via ahad (single) reports and affirms its mutawatir (mass) transmission.

Core Idea
The Quran was transmitted by the whole community, not isolated individuals.

An objector argued that Quranic reports are ahad (single-chain) and thus unreliable. Maturidi directly refutes this as false, asserting that the entire community (kaffa) transmitted the Quran, making it mutawatir – the highest level of authenticity. This lesson examines his decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objector's claim that the Quran's reports are ahad. Notice that this is presented as an attack on the reliability of the Quran.
The Arabic text reads: 'وطعن في أخبار القرآن، إنه خبر الآحاد'.
2
Identify Maturidi's refutation
Look for Maturidi's immediate response: 'وذلك كذب، بل رواه كافة كافة'. He calls the claim a lie and states that the entire community transmitted it.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation is powerful: if the whole community transmitted the Quran (tawatur), then it cannot be ahad. This establishes the Quran's authenticity beyond doubt.
This connects to broader Islamic methodology of transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطعن في أخبار القرآن، إنه خبر الآحاد، وذلك كذب، بل رواه كافة كافة، مع وطعن التواتر بما لا تخلو الجماعة عن البعد من السمع فيحتمل الحيلة، القرب فلا يحتمل مباشرة مثله إلا اليسير. قال ابن الروندي هذه الجهلة بالمحافل وإلا الأمر في ذلك ينتشر ما كان من ... قبل حتى لا يكاد شيء منه يخفى على الأبعدين فضلا عن الأقربين
And he objected to the reports of the Qur'an, that they are ahad (single) reports, and that is false; rather, the whole community transmitted it. And he also objected to tawatur (mass transmission) in that a group is not free from distance in hearing, so trickery is possible; but nearness does not allow direct like it except rarely. Ibn al-Rawandi said: 'These ignorant ones [claim] in gatherings, but the matter spreads in that... before until hardly anything of it is hidden from the farthest, let alone the nearest.'
Source pages p. 795

Commentary
This passage presents an objection about the transmission of the Qur'an. The objector claims that Quranic reports are ahad (single) and thus unreliable. Maturidi refutes this as a lie, stating that the entire community (kaffa) transmitted them, making them mutawatir. The objector further argues against tawatur by saying that large groups far apart could be tricked, but close proximity makes direct collusion rare. Ibn al-Rawandi is quoted mocking the believers, claiming that such matters become widely known anyway, so the argument fails. Maturidi's refutation emphasizes the widespread, continuous transmission of the Qur'an that precludes fabrication.

Key Terms
خبر الآحاد
الكافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran was transmitted through a few isolated chains (ahad).
Correction: Maturidi states the whole community transmitted it, making it mutawatir, not ahad.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how is the Quran transmitted?
A) By ahad reports.
B) By the entire community (kaffa).
C) By individual scholars.
D) By astrologers.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's transmission is mutawatir, not ahad, rendering the objection baseless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you pause to consider that the Quran you recite today has been transmitted by the entire community from generation to generation? Does that certainty change the way you approach its verses? Are you living as if the Quran is definitively from Allah, or do you treat it like a mere book of stories?
?
Maturidi calls the claim that the Quran is ahad a lie. Are there any hidden doubts in your heart about the authenticity of the Quran? What would it mean for your faith if you truly internalized that the Quran is mutawatir?
?
If the Quran's transmission is beyond doubt, why do you still hesitate to submit fully to its commands? What is holding you back from acting on its teachings with the same certainty you have in its authenticity?

Refutation of the Tawatur Impossibility Objection
Maturidi refutes the tawatur objection with a quote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that tawatur is impossible due to distance and trickery, and Maturidi's counter via Ibn al-Rawandi's quote.

Core Idea
Widespread news cannot be hidden, even from the farthest people.

The objector further argued that mass transmission (tawatur) is impossible because large groups far from an event can be tricked, while those near cannot directly witness except rarely. Maturidi counters by quoting Ibn al-Rawandi, who sarcastically remarks that such matters become widely known and cannot be hidden. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection to tawatur
Read the part where the objector says that a group far away might be tricked, and nearness only allows rare direct witness. Understand the logic: distance allows deception, proximity limits witnesses.
The Arabic says: 'بما لا تخلو الجماعة عن البعد من السمع فيحتمل الحيلة، القرب فلا يحتمل مباشرة مثله إلا اليسير'.
2
Examine Ibn al-Rawandi's quote
Read Ibn al-Rawandi's response: he calls the opponents ignorant and claims that such matters spread and are not hidden from the farthest or nearest. This is actually a concession that tawatur works.
Ibn al-Rawandi is an opponent, so his words support Maturidi's point.
3
Understand the refutation
Realize that the quote undermines the objection: if news spreads and is not hidden, then tawatur is possible. Maturidi uses the objector's own associate to refute him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطعن في أخبار القرآن، إنه خبر الآحاد، وذلك كذب، بل رواه كافة كافة، مع وطعن التواتر بما لا تخلو الجماعة عن البعد من السمع فيحتمل الحيلة، القرب فلا يحتمل مباشرة مثله إلا اليسير. قال ابن الروندي هذه الجهلة بالمحافل وإلا الأمر في ذلك ينتشر ما كان من ... قبل حتى لا يكاد شيء منه يخفى على الأبعدين فضلا عن الأقربين
And he objected to the reports of the Qur'an, that they are ahad (single) reports, and that is false; rather, the whole community transmitted it. And he also objected to tawatur (mass transmission) in that a group is not free from distance in hearing, so trickery is possible; but nearness does not allow direct like it except rarely. Ibn al-Rawandi said: 'These ignorant ones [claim] in gatherings, but the matter spreads in that... before until hardly anything of it is hidden from the farthest, let alone the nearest.'
Source pages p. 795

Commentary
This passage presents an objection about the transmission of the Qur'an. The objector claims that Quranic reports are ahad (single) and thus unreliable. Maturidi refutes this as a lie, stating that the entire community (kaffa) transmitted them, making them mutawatir. The objector further argues against tawatur by saying that large groups far apart could be tricked, but close proximity makes direct collusion rare. Ibn al-Rawandi is quoted mocking the believers, claiming that such matters become widely known anyway, so the argument fails. Maturidi's refutation emphasizes the widespread, continuous transmission of the Qur'an that precludes fabrication.

Key Terms
التواتر
ابن الروندي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawatur is impossible because distant people can be tricked and near people are few.
Correction: The quote shows that news spreads widely and cannot be hidden, making tawatur possible.

Checkpoint
What point does Ibn al-Rawandi's statement support?
A) That tawatur is impossible.
B) That news spreads and is not hidden.
C) That only the nearest know the truth.
D) That the objectors are wise.

Key Takeaway
The objection that tawatur is impossible fails because widespread news cannot be hidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you personally rely on tawatur for the core beliefs of your faith? Or do you unknowingly base some of your Islamic knowledge on weak or isolated reports? Take a moment to list one or two beliefs you hold and ask yourself: how did I come to accept them?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi, a skeptic, admitted that truth cannot be hidden. Yet today, many Muslims doubt the authenticity of certain hadiths or historical events due to chains of narration. Have you ever dismissed a teaching because of weak narrators without considering whether it is mass-transmitted? How can you strengthen your trust in the tawatur of Islamic sources?
?
The objection argued that tawatur is impossible because of distance and limited witnesses. In your own life, do you tend to distrust information that comes from far away or from a small number of sources? How does this lesson challenge that tendency, especially regarding the spread of Islamic knowledge?

Ibn ar-Rawandi on the Wiraq's Inconsistency
Ibn ar-Rawandi's critique of the Wiraq's hypocrisy regarding prophetic reports and Manichaean myths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn ar-Rawandi exposes the logical inconsistency of the Wiraq who rejects prophetic proofs yet accepts irrational Manichaean myths.

Core Idea
Rejecting truth while embracing absurdity reveals hypocrisy.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents Ibn ar-Rawandi's scathing critique of the Wiraq, a heretic who denies the clear proofs of prophethood but uncritically accepts the bizarre cosmogony of the Manichaeans. It highlights the importance of consistency in evaluating truth claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall accusation.
Notice the tone of astonishment in 'العجب'.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
The Wiraq rejects prophetic reports despite proofs but accepts Manichaean sayings. Contrast these two stances.
Manichaeans are a dualist sect.
3
Examine the specific Manichaean beliefs
Ibn ar-Rawandi lists absurdities: heavens from devils' skins, earth disturbed by snakes/scorpions, and acceptance of reports about light and darkness.
These beliefs are irrational and lack evidence.
4
Reflect on the logical inconsistency
The Wiraq rejects what his own intellect recognizes as good while embracing nonsense. Consider how this exposes hypocrisy.
This lesson underscores the need for consistent criteria in evaluating claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن الروندى : العجب من الوراق حيث جحد أخبار الرسل مع البراهين ودعاء إلى قبول قول المنانية، وألزم القوم حماقاتهم من بسط السماوات من جلود الشياطين، واضطراب الأرض باضطراب الحيات والعقارب فيها، وقبول أخبارهم بعمل النور والظلمة، ودفع ما هو في عقلهم حسنة، وبالله التوفيق.
Ibn al-Rawandi said: It is surprising from the Wiraq that he denies the reports of the messengers despite the proofs, and calls for accepting the sayings of the Manichaeans. And he imposes upon the people their follies: the spreading out of the heavens from the skins of devils, and the disturbance of the earth by the disturbance of snakes and scorpions in it, and accepting their reports about the action of light and darkness, and rejecting what in their intellect is good. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
In this passage, Ibn ar-Rawandi criticizes the Wiraq for his inconsistency. The Wiraq rejects the true reports of the prophets despite clear proofs, yet he accepts the absurd teachings of the Manichaeans (المنانية). Ibn ar-Rawandi mentions specific Manichaean beliefs: that the heavens are made from the skins of devils, the earth is disturbed by snakes and scorpions, and acceptance of reports about the actions of light and darkness. Despite their irrationality, the Wiraq accepts these and rejects what his own intellect recognizes as good. This highlights the hypocrisy of the heretic.

Key Terms
المنانية
جلود الشياطين
النور والظلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rejecting prophetic proofs while accepting heretical myths can be logically consistent.
Correction: Ibn ar-Rawandi shows it is hypocritical because the Wiraq rejects clear proofs but accepts absurd myths without evidence.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn ar-Rawandi accuse the Wiraq of doing?
A) Rejecting all reports
B) Accepting Manichaean myths while rejecting prophetic proofs
C) Being a true believer
D) Following the prophets correctly

Key Takeaway
Ibn ar-Rawandi exposes the logical inconsistency of rejecting prophetic proofs while embracing irrational myths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there truths from Allah that you have rejected because they demand sacrifice, while you embrace comfortable falsehoods from culture or desire? What would it cost you to submit fully to the prophetic message?
?
When you encounter a belief you don't like, do you apply the same critical scrutiny to it as you do to Islamic teachings? Or do you hold Allah's proofs to a higher standard than the myths of man?
?
Is there any area of your life where your actions betray that you 'reject what your intellect knows is good'? Will you repent and align your mind with the truth?
