Classification of Good and Evil in Acts
Maturidi classifies actions into two types and demonstrates slaughter is not inherently evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of actions into inherently good/evil and context-dependent, and how slaughter falls into the latter category.

Core Idea
Slaughter is not inherently evil; its moral value depends on context and divine permission.

In this passage, Maturidi lays out a foundational principle of Islamic moral epistemology: actions are of two types. Some are good or evil in themselves, while others derive their moral status from outcomes and divine sanction. Slaughter, he argues, belongs to the second type, and thus cannot be condemned as intrinsically evil. This sets the stage for refuting dualist objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of two types of actions
Maturidi states that things are of two types: those good/evil in themselves, and those dependent on need and outcomes. Read this classification carefully.
Note the Arabic terms: 'يحسن لنفسه' and 'على حسب الحاجة'.
2
Apply the classification to slaughter
Maturidi argues that slaughter cannot be evil in itself because it can be good in retaliation or when preventing harm. Notice the reasoning about outcomes.
3
Understand the natural vs. rational distinction
Aversion to slaughter is natural (due to pain), but rational judgment can override it. Maturidi uses habituation as evidence.
The prohibition is natural, not rational, and thus subject to change.
4
Reflect on the animal analogy
Just as wild animals can be tamed, aversion to slaughter can be overcome by habit. This reinforces the point that the prohibition is not inherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيد الوراق الذي بينا أن فى العقل ذم الإساءة إلى من لم يؤذ ، وإن لم يحب لغيره ما يحب المرء لنفسه ، والذبائح خارجة من ذلك ؛ فهذا لأنه توهمه مفردا من العامل ، فاذا تأمل حسن العواقب والسلامة مع عقيب المنافع وكذلك فضل راحة وفى الذبائح ذلك ونحن نقول وبالله التوفيق : إن الأشياء نوعان : أحدهما مما يحسن لنفسه ويقبح ضده وكل خلافاته ، والثانى ما يحسن الشيء وخلافاته على حسب الحاجة وقيام الدلالة من حمد العواقب وذمها ، فلزم القول فى هذا بمن يعرف أحوال الحمد والذم ، فيخرج الأمر عليه على أنه لا بد لمن يكون يعتمد على عقله والاختلاف المتناقض ذلك سببه ، أو إلى مخصوص من العقل ، وفى ذلك القول بالرسول . ذا ثم أمر الذبائح لا يحتمل أن يكون قبحها لنفسها لما يحل فى موضع الانتقام ويحسن في العقول إذا تفكر فى ذلك دفع الأذى والمكروه ، أو تقع العواقب فبطل قبح ذلك لنفسه ، فلزم جواز المحنة فيه بالترك والإذن ، وفى ذلك إباحة . وأيضا أن كل شيء حسنه العقل فهو لا يقبح بحال ، وكذلك القبيح من الحسن ، وكل شيء قبح لنفار الطبع بما يتوهم حلوله في جوهر المتوهم فينفر طبعه لألمه ، ثم هذا قد يجوز أن يذهب ذلك بالاعتياد نحو القصابين والذين اعتادوا القتال ، فثبت أن النهى عنه طبيعي لا عقلى ، فتغير ذلك من العادة يزول ، وذلك نحو جواهر من الحيوان طبعه التوحش ، وعلى ذلك طبع الجميع عن الأحمال الثقيلة ، ثم تصير الرياضة وتعويد غيره كأنها على ذلك طبعت ، فعلى ذلك أمر الحيوان . وأيضا أن كل حي إذ هو يموت ، ثم لم يلحق أحد به لاممه ، فمثله إذا جاء الإذن ممن هو له وأحق من يقول الثنويه لأوجه : أحدها استجازتهم تباين النور والظلمة ثم الامتزاج ثم التباين ، وفى ذلك تفرق بين كل مقترنين وتميز بين كل ممتزجين ، وذلك معنى الذبح . والثانى أن الألم إما أن يحل بجوهر النور فيصير محتملا للاذى، وهو شر ، ولولا ذلك لم ينه عن الذبح ؛ إذ الذبح هو ذلك ، ثم هو لا يخلو من يحل بجوهر النور ، فقد عمل الشر ، أو بجوهر الظلمة ، فالنهى والإنكار مما لا معنى [له] ؛ لأنه ينكر على من لا يحتمل طبعه القبول في ذلك ، كمن يأمر من ليس له ما يطير [به] بالطيران ، أو أن يكون الألم يحل بجوهر الظلمة ، وذلك هو الحق عندهم ، ثم إما أن دخل عليه ذلك بجوهر النور فهو يصنع ما يُدم عليه ، أو بجوهر الظلمة فقد أحسن حيث آلم الظلمة ؛ إذ ذلك عدل ، والله الموفق . وإيضا إن فى الذبح إخراج الروح الصافى من الظلمة الكدرة ، وذلك الحق ، وهو عاقبة كل شيء .
And the papermaker's argument that we explained that in the intellect there is blame for harming one who did not harm, and not loving for others what one loves for oneself, and slaughter is excluded from that; this is because he imagined it isolated from the agent. When one reflects on good outcomes and safety along with benefits and the virtue of comfort, and in slaughter that is the same. And we say, and with Allah is success: Indeed, things are of two types: one of them is that which is good in itself and its opposite is evil, and all its opposites; the second is that which is good and its opposites depending on need and the establishment of evidence from praise and blame of outcomes. So it is necessary to speak about this with one who knows the states of praise and blame, and the matter emerges for him that it is necessary for one who relies on his intellect and contradictory difference that is the cause, or to a specific [part] of the intellect, and in that is the statement with the Messenger. Then the matter of slaughter cannot be that its evil is for itself because what happens in the place of retaliation is good in the intellect when one reflects on repelling harm and disliked things, or outcomes occur, so its evil for itself is invalidated. So it is permissible to test it by abandoning and permitting, and in that is permission. Also, everything that the intellect deems good cannot be evil at all, and likewise the evil from the good, and everything that is evil due to the aversion of nature because of imagining its occurrence in the substance of the imaginer, so his nature shuns it due to pain. Then this may disappear by habituation, like butchers and those accustomed to fighting. So it is established that the prohibition is natural, not rational, and that disappears by habit. This is like animals with a nature of wildness, and similarly all are naturally averse to heavy loads, then training and accustoming becomes as if they were created for it. So likewise is the matter of animals. Also, every living thing dies, then no one has experienced its pain, so similarly when permission comes from the one who has authority over it. And most worthy of saying [the refutation of] the dualists are aspects: one of them is their permitting the separation of light and darkness, then mixing, then separation, and in that is separation between every two joined and distinction between every two mixed, and that is the meaning of slaughter. The second is that pain either occurs in the substance of light, making it susceptible to harm, and that is evil, and were it not for that, slaughter would not be forbidden; for slaughter is that. Then it is inevitable that either it occurs in the substance of light – then he has committed evil – or in the substance of darkness – then prohibition and denial have no meaning, because he denies one whose nature does not accept that, like one who commands a person who has no means of flying to fly. Or that pain occurs in the substance of darkness, and that is the truth according to them. Then either that enters upon him through the substance of light – so he does what is blamed upon him – or through the substance of darkness – then he has done good by causing pain to darkness, for that is justice. And Allah is the grantor of success. Also, in slaughter there is extraction of the pure spirit from the turbid darkness, and that is the truth, and it is the outcome of everything.
Source pages p. 800

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist arguments against the permissibility of slaughter. Maturidi begins by recalling a previously established rational principle: the intellect inherently condemns harming the innocent and commands treating others as one loves for oneself, but slaughter is an exception because it is imagined without consideration of its agent. Then he presents a classification of actions into two types: (1) those good or evil in themselves, and (2) those whose moral value depends on context and outcomes. Slaughter falls into the second type, as its apparent harm is outweighed by benefits and permission. He argues that if slaughter were inherently evil, it would not be allowed even for retaliation, but since it can be good when intended for justice or benefit, its evil is not intrinsic. He further argues that aversion to slaughter is natural, not rational, as shown by habituation (butchers become desensitized). After establishing this, he refutes the dualist position using their own principles. The dualists (likely Manichaeans) believe in two eternal principles, light (good) and darkness (evil). They object that slaughter causes pain, which is evil. Maturidi presents two aspects: (1) The dualists themselves permit the mixing and separation of light and darkness, which is analogous to slaughter (separating soul from body). (2) Pain either affects the substance of light or darkness. If it affects light, the one inflicting pain commits evil, but if it affects darkness, it is praiseworthy because it harms the evil principle. Thus their objection is self-contradictory: if the victim is of darkness, causing pain is just; if of light, the prohibition would apply only if they accept that light can be harmed, which undermines their dualism. Finally, he adds that slaughter releases the pure soul from impure darkness, which is ultimately true and good. This refutation shows that the dualist objection fails on its own terms.

Key Terms
الذبائح
الحسن والقبح العقلي
الطبع والعادة
النهى الطبيعي والعقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slaughter is inherently evil because it causes pain.
Correction: Maturidi argues that goodness/evil are not intrinsic to many actions; they depend on context, and the apparent evil of slaughter is outweighed by benefits and divine permission.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is slaughter not inherently evil?
A) Because it does not cause pain to any sentient being.
B) Because its moral value depends on context and divine permission, not on the act itself.
C) Because it is a natural instinct that cannot be judged rationally.
D) Because the dualists' own principles prove it is always good.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's classification shows that slaughter is not inherently evil; its moral status depends on outcomes and divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you instinctively avoid—perhaps due to upbringing or discomfort. Is your avoidance based on rational evaluation, or is it simply habit? How would you test its moral status using Maturidi's criteria of outcomes and divine command? What would you need to change in your life to align your instincts with truth?
?
Maturidi says that butchers overcome natural aversion through habit. Is there an act of obedience—like giving charity, praying at night, or seeking forgiveness—that you find difficult because your nature resists it? What habit can you begin today to make it easier and turn your aversion into ease?
?
If slaughter can be good in the context of retaliation, how does that challenge your view of actions you consider obviously wrong? Are there any 'obvious evils' in your life that might actually be permissible or even good when placed in their proper context under divine command? What would it cost you to re-examine them honestly?

Refuting the Dualist Objection Using Their Own Principles
Maturidi refutes dualist arguments using their own concepts of light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes dualist arguments against slaughter by showing contradictions in their own system.

Core Idea
The dualist objection self-destructs: if pain affects darkness, it is good; if it affects light, it cannot be evil without undermining dualism.

After establishing that slaughter is not inherently evil, Maturidi directly engages the dualists' theology. They claim that causing pain is evil, but Maturidi shows that according to their own beliefs about light and darkness, either the pain is justified or their objection is meaningless. This is a powerful example of internal critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument: analogy of separation
Dualists permit the separation of light and darkness. Maturidi points out that slaughter is a form of separation (soul from body), so it should be permissible by their own logic.
2
Examine the dilemma of pain location
Pain must occur either in the substance of light (good) or darkness (evil). If in light, the dualist's own principle of light being invulnerable is compromised. If in darkness, causing pain is actually good because it harms the evil principle.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Understand the conclusion on extraction of spirit
Slaughter extracts the pure spirit from turbid darkness, which is ultimately true and good. This reinforces the permissibility of slaughter.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

العواقب وذمها ، فلزم القول فى هذا بمن يعرف أحوال الحمد والذم ، فيخرج الأمر عليه على أنه لا بد لمن يكون يعتمد على عقله والاختلاف المتناقض ذلك سببه ، أو إلى مخصوص من العقل ، وفى ذلك القول بالرسول . ذا القصابين والذين اعتادوا القتال ، فثبت أن النهى عنه طبيعي لا عقلى ، فتغير ذلك من العادة يزول ، وذلك نحو جواهر من الحيوان طبعه التوحش ، وعلى ذلك طبع الجميع عن الأحمال الثقيلة ، ثم تصير الرياضة وتعويد غيره كأنها على ذلك طبعت ، فعلى ذلك أمر الحيوان . وأيضا أن كل حي إذ هو يموت ، ثم لم يلحق أحد به لاممه ، فمثله إذا جاء الإذن ممن هو له وأحق من يقول الثنويه لأوجه : أحدها استجازتهم تباين النور والظلمة ثم الامتزاج ثم التباين ، وفى ذلك تفرق بين كل مقترنين وتميز بين كل ممتزجين ، وذلك معنى الذبح . والثانى أن الألم إما أن يحل بجوهر النور فيصير محتملا للاذى، وهو شر ، ولولا ذلك لم ينه عن الذبح ؛ إذ الذبح هو ذلك ، ثم هو لا يخلو من يحل بجوهر النور ، فقد عمل الشر ، أو بجوهر الظلمة ، فالنهى والإنكار مما لا معنى [له] ؛ لأنه ينكر على من لا يحتمل طبعه القبول في ذلك ، كمن يأمر من ليس له ما يطير [به] بالطيران ، أو أن يكون الألم يحل بجوهر الظلمة ، وذلك هو الحق عندهم ، ثم إما أن دخل عليه ذلك بجوهر النور فهو يصنع ما يُدم عليه ، أو بجوهر الظلمة فقد أحسن حيث آلم الظلمة ؛ إذ ذلك عدل ، والله الموفق . وإيضا إن فى الذبح إخراج الروح الصافى من الظلمة الكدرة ، وذلك الحق ، وهو عاقبة كل شيء .
And the papermaker's argument that we explained that in the intellect there is blame for harming one who did not harm, and not loving for others what one loves for oneself, and slaughter is excluded from that; this is because he imagined it isolated from the agent. When one reflects on good outcomes and safety along with benefits and the virtue of comfort, and in slaughter that is the same. And we say, and with Allah is success: Indeed, things are of two types: one of them is that which is good in itself and its opposite is evil, and all its opposites; the second is that which is good and its opposites depending on need and the establishment of evidence from praise and blame of outcomes. So it is necessary to speak about this with one who knows the states of praise and blame, and the matter emerges for him that it is necessary for one who relies on his intellect and contradictory difference that is the cause, or to a specific [part] of the intellect, and in that is the statement with the Messenger. Then the matter of slaughter cannot be that its evil is for itself because what happens in the place of retaliation is good in the intellect when one reflects on repelling harm and disliked things, or outcomes occur, so its evil for itself is invalidated. So it is permissible to test it by abandoning and permitting, and in that is permission. Also, everything that the intellect deems good cannot be evil at all, and likewise the evil from the good, and everything that is evil due to the aversion of nature because of imagining its occurrence in the substance of the imaginer, so his nature shuns it due to pain. Then this may disappear by habituation, like butchers and those accustomed to fighting. So it is established that the prohibition is natural, not rational, and that disappears by habit. This is like animals with a nature of wildness, and similarly all are naturally averse to heavy loads, then training and accustoming becomes as if they were created for it. So likewise is the matter of animals. Also, every living thing dies, then no one has experienced its pain, so similarly when permission comes from the one who has authority over it. And most worthy of saying [the refutation of] the dualists are aspects: one of them is their permitting the separation of light and darkness, then mixing, then separation, and in that is separation between every two joined and distinction between every two mixed, and that is the meaning of slaughter. The second is that pain either occurs in the substance of light, making it susceptible to harm, and that is evil, and were it not for that, slaughter would not be forbidden; for slaughter is that. Then it is inevitable that either it occurs in the substance of light – then he has committed evil – or in the substance of darkness – then prohibition and denial have no meaning, because he denies one whose nature does not accept that, like one who commands a person who has no means of flying to fly. Or that pain occurs in the substance of darkness, and that is the truth according to them. Then either that enters upon him through the substance of light – so he does what is blamed upon him – or through the substance of darkness – then he has done good by causing pain to darkness, for that is justice. And Allah is the grantor of success. Also, in slaughter there is extraction of the pure spirit from the turbid darkness, and that is the truth, and it is the outcome of everything.
Source pages p. 800

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist arguments against the permissibility of slaughter. Maturidi begins by recalling a previously established rational principle: the intellect inherently condemns harming the innocent and commands treating others as one loves for oneself, but slaughter is an exception because it is imagined without consideration of its agent. Then he presents a classification of actions into two types: (1) those good or evil in themselves, and (2) those whose moral value depends on context and outcomes. Slaughter falls into the second type, as its apparent harm is outweighed by benefits and permission. He argues that if slaughter were inherently evil, it would not be allowed even for retaliation, but since it can be good when intended for justice or benefit, its evil is not intrinsic. He further argues that aversion to slaughter is natural, not rational, as shown by habituation (butchers become desensitized). After establishing this, he refutes the dualist position using their own principles. The dualists (likely Manichaeans) believe in two eternal principles, light (good) and darkness (evil). They object that slaughter causes pain, which is evil. Maturidi presents two aspects: (1) The dualists themselves permit the mixing and separation of light and darkness, which is analogous to slaughter (separating soul from body). (2) Pain either affects the substance of light or darkness. If it affects light, the one inflicting pain commits evil, but if it affects darkness, it is praiseworthy because it harms the evil principle. Thus their objection is self-contradictory: if the victim is of darkness, causing pain is just; if of light, the prohibition would apply only if they accept that light can be harmed, which undermines their dualism. Finally, he adds that slaughter releases the pure soul from impure darkness, which is ultimately true and good. This refutation shows that the dualist objection fails on its own terms.

Key Terms
الثنوية
النور والظلمة
جوهر النور
جوهر الظلمة
الروح الصافى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualist objection to slaughter is logically sound.
Correction: Maturidi shows that by their own beliefs, the objection either justifies slaughter or is incoherent.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Maturidi's second aspect in refuting the dualists?
A) Pain always occurs in the substance of light, proving slaughter is evil.
B) Pain either occurs in light (evil) or darkness (good), so the objection contradicts itself.
C) Pain never occurs in either substance, so the objecton is invalid.
D) Pain occurs only in darkness, so slaughter is justified.

Key Takeaway
The dualist objection fails because it is inconsistent with their own metaphysical commitments about light and darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an objection to your faith from someone with a different worldview, do you engage with their assumptions like Maturidi, or do you simply dismiss them? What holds you back from entering their framework for the sake of truth?
?
The dualists saw pain as evil. How do you view the pains and trials in your own life—are they signs of divine neglect or opportunities for purification? Will you embrace the belief that whatever Allah does is just, even when it hurts?
?
Maturidi used the opponent's logic to expose their inconsistency. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs with the same rigorous honesty, even if it means finding contradictions you'd rather ignore?

Refuting Dualism: Prohibition Proves Contingency
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that prohibition of evil proves its contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fact that actions are prohibited demonstrates that they are not necessary in themselves, refuting dualistic claims about evil.

Core Idea
Prohibition proves possibility; if evil were essential, prohibition would be meaningless.

Maturidi engages with dualistic doctrines that posit two eternal principles—light and good, darkness and evil. The dualists claim that evil actions like slaughter originate from the dark essence. Maturidi argues that if evil were an inherent property of a substance, then prohibiting it would be absurd, just as commanding a bird to fly without wings. Thus, the very existence of prohibition shows that evil is contingent and not essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the structure: an argument from prohibition, two conditional scenarios, and an alternative explanation.
The passage contains OCR uncertainties; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the dualistic claim
Recognize that the dualists (e.g., Manichaeans) attribute evil to a separate eternal essence of darkness. Maturidi is rebutting this view.
The passage mentions 'essence of light' and 'essence of darkness' as the two principles.
3
Understand the prohibition argument
Maturidi says: if slaughter were inherently evil (belonging to darkness), then prohibition would be pointless, because you cannot forbid a nature. This proves evil is not necessary.
He compares it to commanding a wingless creature to fly.
4
Analyze the two scenarios
The passage then considers two possibilities: if the evil action originates from light, it is blameworthy; if from darkness, causing pain to darkness is seen as justice by dualists. Both undermine their position.
This shows internal inconsistency in dualism.
5
Reflect on the alternative view
Maturidi offers an alternative: slaughter extracts the pure spirit from turbid darkness, which is the ultimate truth. This reframes the action positively.
This is a subtle theological move to show that even 'evil' actions have a divine purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن وهو شر ، ولولا ذلك لم ينه عن الذبح ؛ إذ الذبح ؛ إذ هو ذلك ، ذلك ، ثم هو لا يخلو من يحل بجوهر النور ، فقد عمل الشر ، أو بجوهر الظلمة ، فالنهى والإنكار مما لا معنى [له؟] ؛ لأنه ينكر على من لا يحتمل طبعه القبول في ذلك ، كمن يأمر من ليس له ما يطير [به؟] بالطيران ، أو أن يكون الألم يحل بجوهر الظلمة ، وذلك هو الحق عندهم ، ثم إما أن دخل عليه ذلك بجوهر / النور فهو يصنع ما يُدم عليه ، أو بجوهر الظلمة فقد أحسن حيث آلم الظلمة ؛ إذ ذلك عدل ، والله الموفق . وإيضا إن فى الذبح إخراج الروح الصافى من الظلمة الكدرة ، وذلك الحق ، وهو عاقبة كل شيء .
That it is evil, and were it not for that, he would not have prohibited slaughter; since slaughter, since it is that, then it is not free from either residing in the essence of light, for he does evil, or in the essence of darkness, so prohibition and denial have no meaning; because he denies one whose nature does not accept that, like one who commands one who has no means to fly to fly, or that pain resides in the essence of darkness, and that is the truth according to them. Then either that enters upon him from the essence of light, then he does what is blameworthy [؟], or from the essence of darkness, then he has done well by causing pain to darkness; for that is justice. And God is the grantor of success. Also, in slaughter is the extraction of the pure spirit from the turbid darkness, and that is the truth, and it is the end of everything.
Source pages p. 801

Commentary
This passage refutes dualistic doctrines (such as Manichaeism) that posit two eternal principles, light and darkness, as sources of good and evil. Maturidi argues that prohibition demonstrates contingency: if evil were an inherent property of an essence (like darkness), then prohibiting it would be pointless, just as commanding a wingless being to fly is futile. He then examines two scenarios: if an action originates from the essence of light, it could be blameworthy; if from darkness, causing pain to darkness might be seen as justice by the dualists. Finally, Maturidi presents an alternative view: sacrifice extracts the pure soul from turbid darkness, which is the true end of all things.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the passage affirms dualism because it mentions light and darkness as principles.
Correction: The passage is refuting dualism; the mention of light and darkness is to show the internal inconsistencies of that view, not to endorse it.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument in this passage against the dualistic view of evil?
A) Evil is created by God directly.
B) Prohibition of an act proves that it is not necessary by nature.
C) Sacrifice is always evil.
D) Darkness is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
Prohibition of an act demonstrates that it is not an essential property of a substance, refuting dualistic claims that evil is inherent in darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by telling yourself 'that's just the way I am'? Maturidi's argument shows that if your nature made you do it, Allah's prohibition would be meaningless. What does this argument demand you do the next time you feel tempted to blame your 'nature'?
?
When you see evil in the world—whether from others or in your own heart—do you truly believe it is not a second eternal force, but part of a divinely ordered test? How does Maturidi's refutation of dualism change the way you should respond to personal suffering or injustice?
?
If slaughter (a seemingly evil act) can have a higher purpose of extracting pure spirit, what does that imply about the trials you currently face? Are you willing to ask Allah to show you the 'extraction of pure spirit' in your own hardships, and how might that shift your patience and gratitude?

Christian Doctrines on Christ
Maturidi enumerates Christian beliefs about Christ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different Christian beliefs about Christ's nature as recorded by Imam al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Christians hold multiple, sometimes contradictory, views on Christ's divinity and humanity.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by cataloguing the various Christian sects and their beliefs about Jesus Christ. He notes that some Christians attribute two spirits to Christ—one created human spirit and one eternal divine spirit that is a part of God. Others identify the spirit in Christ as God Himself, disagreeing on whether God is contained in the body or merely manages it. Still others claim that only a part of God entered Christ. Finally, a convert reports that some Christians interpret 'son' metaphorically as adoption. This taxonomy sets the stage for Maturidi's forthcoming refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the range of Christian views.
Pay attention to the different groups.
2
Identify the three main views
List the three distinct views: (1) two spirits (created and divine), (2) spirit is God with two sub-views on mode, (3) a part of God reached Christ.
The adoption view is a fourth, separate interpretation.
3
Note the adoptionist view
Ibn Shabib reports that some Christians say 'son' means adopted, not biological, similar to the Prophet's wives being called 'mothers'.
This is a metaphorical understanding.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Why does Maturidi list these views before refuting them? This shows the diversity and prepares the ground for a systematic refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وتفرقت النصارى في المسيح ، فمنهم من جعل له روحين : أحدهما محدثا وهو روح الناسوتية ، يشبه أرواح الناس ، وروح لاهوتي قديمة ، جزء من الله ، صار في البدن ذلك . وقالوا : ليس إلا أب وابن وروح القدس . ومنهم آخرون جعلوا الروح الذي في المسيح الله ، لا الجزء ، لكن فريقا يجعل في البدن على كون الشيء في الشيء ، وفريقا التدبير لا على إحاطة البدن به . وفيهم من يقول : ليصل إليه جزء من الله تعالى ، ويصل جزء آخر قال ابن شبيب : سمعت من مولديهم أنه كان ابن التبني لا ابن الولادة ، كما سمعت أزواج محمد عليه السلام أمهات ، وكما يقول الرجل لآخر : يا بني . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فيقال لهم : إذ كانت الروح التي فيه قديمة ، وهي بعض ، كيف صار ابنا ولم يصل غيره من الأبعاض ؟ فإن قيل : لأنه أقل
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: And the Christians divided concerning the Christ. Among them are those who attributed to him two spirits: one created, which is the human spirit, resembling the spirits of people, and an ancient divine spirit, a part of God, which entered that body. And they said: There is only Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. And others among them made the spirit that was in the Christ to be God, not the part, but one group holds that it is in the body as a thing in a thing, and another group holds that it is by way of management, not by the body encompassing it. And among them are those who say: A part of God, exalted, reached him, and another part reached? Ibn Shabib said: I heard from some of their converts that he was a son by adoption, not by birth, just as I heard that the wives of Muhammad, peace be upon him, are mothers, and as a man says to another: O my son. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: So it is said to them: Since the spirit that was in him is ancient, and it is a part, how did it become a son while no other parts reached him? Then if it is said: Because it is lesser...
Source pages p. 809

Commentary
This passage is a summary of various Christian doctrines about Christ and the beginning of a refutation. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, first lays out the differing views among Christians: some say Christ has two spirits—a created human spirit and an uncreated divine spirit that is a part of God—and they speak of Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. Others say the spirit in Christ is God Himself, not a part, but disagree on how the divinity relates to the body: some hold that it is contained in the body as one thing in another, while others hold that it is a management relationship without physical containment. Still others say only a part of God entered Christ, implying other parts elsewhere. Then a quote from Ibn Shabib mentions that some Christians interpret sonship metaphorically as adoption, similar to the Prophet's wives being called 'Mothers of the Believers'. Finally, the author begins his refutation: if the spirit in Christ is ancient and a part of God, how can it be a son when other parts did not become sons? The argument is cut off with the likely Christian response 'because it is lesser', but the full response is not provided in this window.

روح ناسوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Christians believe Jesus is the literal Son of God in a biological sense.
Correction: The passage shows diverse views, including metaphorical adoption and differing understandings of divinity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do some Christians believe about the relationship of the divine spirit to Christ's body?
A) It is fully contained in the body as one thing in another.
B) It is only a management relationship without containment.
C) Both views are mentioned as different opinions.
D) They believe the body itself is divine.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi presents a range of Christian views on Christ, from two-spirit theories to metaphorical adoption, illustrating the diversity before launching his refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that some Christians believe Jesus had two spirits—one human and one divine—do you feel a stronger conviction that Islamic monotheism is the only consistent truth? How does this knowledge push you to deepen your own understanding of Tawhid?
?
The adoptionist view among Christians resembles the Islamic honorific of 'Mother of the Believers' for the Prophet's wives. How does this similarity affect your attitude when discussing faith with Christians? Does it humble you or make you more confident?
?
This lesson reveals that even Christians disagree among themselves about Jesus. Have you ever assumed all non-Muslims hold uniform beliefs? How might this realization change the way you approach dawah or interfaith conversations?

Initial Refutation of Christian Doctrines
Maturidi begins refuting Christian views with a logical question about sonship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beginning of Imam al-Maturidi's refutation of Christian belief in the divinity of Christ.

Core Idea
If the spirit in Christ is an ancient part of God, why is it uniquely called son while other parts are not?

After enumerating Christian views, Imam al-Maturidi begins his refutation by posing a critical question: If the spirit in Christ is an ancient part of God, how can it be called 'son' when no other parts of God receive that title? He anticipates a possible Christian response that it is because of some 'lessening' but leaves the argument unfinished here.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the opening refutation.
Notice it begins with 'So it is said to them' indicating a rebuttal.
2
Understand the logical question
Maturidi asks: Since the spirit is ancient and a part, why is it a son while other parts are not? This challenges the consistency of the Christian claim.
The question targets the concept of sonship.
3
Consider the anticipated response
The passage mentions a possible answer: 'because it is lesser'. This seems to refer to the spirit being a lesser part? The text is cut off.
Reflect on how this response might be countered.

?

What step will you take today to ensure your devotion remains centered on Allah alone, not on isolated 'parts' of His creation?
?
When you encounter a belief that seems inconsistent (like the Christian claim here), do you honestly examine its foundations, or do you accept it because it is comfortable or familiar? Maturidi's raw question exposes a flaw in a widely held doctrine. Are you willing to let such questions unsettle your own assumptions? What comfortable belief about your own practice might need the same kind of rigorous logical examination today?

Christian Views on Christ Classified by al-Maturidi
Al-Maturidi enumerates Christian sects' beliefs about Jesus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major Christian sects' beliefs about Jesus as classified by Imam al-Maturidi, including the concepts of two natures, divine indwelling, and adoption.

Core Idea
Christians disagreed radically on Christ's nature, from two spirits to adoption.

Imam al-Maturidi begins his refutation of Christian doctrines by first cataloguing the diverse views among Christians themselves about Jesus. This classification shows that Christian beliefs are not monolithic, and many of their claims are internally contradictory. The passage introduces several sects and their specific positions regarding the divinity and humanity of Christ.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic slowly, noting the different groups mentioned. Try to identify the key terms: ناسوتية, لاهوتية, روح القدس, ابن التبني.
Focus on the flow: the author lists views in sequence.
2
Identify the three main groups
The passage groups Christians into those who believe Jesus has two spirits, those who believe the spirit is God Himself (with two subgroups), and those who say a part of God reaches him. Find each.
3
Understand the 'son of adoption' view
The final part reports a view that Jesus is the son of adoption, not biological. Relate this to the Islamic understanding of Jesus as a prophet.
4
Reflect on why classification matters
Al-Maturidi lists these views to prepare for refutation. Ask yourself: how does knowing these internal contradictions help in arguing against Christian theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وتفرقت النصارى في المسيح، فمنهم من جعل له روحين: أحدهما محدثا وهو روح الناسوتية، يشبه أرواح الناس، وروح لاهوتية قديمة، جزء من الله، صار في البدن ذلك. وقالوا: ليس إلا أب وابن وروح القدس. منهم وآخرون جعلوا الروح الذي في المسيح الله، لا الجزء، لكن فريقا يجعل في البدن على كون الشيء في الشيء، وفريقا التدبير لا على إحاطة البدن به. وفيهم من يقول: ليصل إليه جزء من الله تعالى، ويصل جزء آخر. قال ابن شبيب: سمعت من مولديهم أنه كان ابن التبنى لا ابن الولاد، كما سمعت أزواج محمد عليه السلام أمهات، وكما يقول الرجل لآخر: يا بني.
The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: The Christians divided concerning the Messiah. Among them are those who made for him two spirits: one created, which is the spirit of humanity, resembling the spirits of people, and an ancient divine spirit, a part of God, which came to be in that body. And they said: There is only Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. Among them are others who made the spirit that is in the Messiah to be God, not a part, but one group makes it in the body according to the mode of a thing being in a thing, and another group makes it a controlling [spirit] not by encompassing the body. And among them are some who say: A part of God Most High reaches him, and another part reaches [elsewhere]. Ibn Shabib said: I heard from their converts that He (Jesus) was the son of adoption, not the son of birth, just as I heard that the wives of Muhammad (peace be upon him) are mothers, and as a man says to another: O my son.
Source pages p. 809

Commentary
This passage is a classification of Christian views on the nature of Christ. The author begins by stating that the Christians disagreed about the Messiah. He then lists several sects: (1) Some believe Jesus has two spirits: one created human spirit (ruh nasutiyya) resembling human souls, and one ancient divine spirit (ruh lahutiyya) that is a part of God, which entered the body. They also profess a Trinity: Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. (2) Others believe the spirit in Jesus is God Himself, not a part of God. Among these, one group holds that God dwells in the body after the manner of one thing being inside another (e.g., wine in a wineskin), while another group holds that God controls the body from without, not by encompassment. (3) Still others say that a part of God reaches Jesus, while another part reaches elsewhere. The author then reports from Ibn Shabib that he heard from Christian converts that Jesus was the 'son of adoption' (ibn al-tabanni), not a biological son, analogous to how the Prophet's wives are called 'mothers of the believers' or how a man might call another 'my son' as a term of endearment. This classification sets the stage for the subsequent refutation.

Key Terms
ناسوتية
لاهوتية
روح القدس
ابن التبني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Christians hold a single, uniform belief about Christ.
Correction: This passage shows multiple contradictory Christian sects with different views on Jesus's nature, including two spirits, indwelling modes, and adoptionism.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Christian group believes that the spirit in Jesus is a part of God?
A) Those who say he has two spirits.
B) Those who say the spirit is God Himself.
C) Those who say a part of God reaches him.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi classifies Christian views on Christ into distinct sects, showing their internal diversity and inconsistencies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even in a small way, attributed human limitations or divisions to Allah? How does seeing the Christians' confusion over Christ's nature because of such attributions make you examine your own speech about Allah? Will you commit today to using only the names and attributes Allah has revealed, without any resemblance to creation, and ask Him to protect you from misguidance?
?
The Christians divided because they tried to fit God into human categories. When you think about Allah’s attributes, do you find yourself using mental images or analogies? What will you do to train your heart to affirm what Allah affirmed without asking 'how'? Can you spend two minutes in sincere supplication asking Allah to keep you on the straight path of tawheed?

The Impossibility of the World Having a Son
Maturidi argues that the world cannot have a son due to logical contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical arguments against the concept of the world having an eternal son, and why such a notion is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
A son cannot be both smaller and co-eternal with its father.

Maturidi engages with a theological claim that the world could have a 'son' — a doctrine reminiscent of Christian Trinitarian theology. In this passage, he uses pure logical reasoning to demonstrate the absurdity of such a concept. By analyzing the relationships of parts and wholes, he shows that the notion of an eternal son of the world leads to inescapable contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct arguments: one about parts, one about the whole.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: if the world's parts are sons, and if the whole is the son. Identify each and note the contradiction Maturidi highlights.
Write down the contradictions: son smaller than father vs. both eternal; father being son to itself.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi uses reductio: assume the claim, then derive an absurd conclusion. Trace each step to see how the absurdity arises.
The absurdity is that the son cannot be both eternal and smaller, and the father cannot be the son of itself.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why this argument is important: it refutes any notion of an eternal son or co-eternal beings in a single hierarchy.
This argument shows that Islamic theology must reject any literal sonship applied to the world or God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من لزمه جعل كل أبعاض العالم البنين للاكبر منها ، ويلزمه أن يجعل كل بعض من البقية كذلك ، فيصير بكليته بنين . ثم المعروف أن الابن يكون أصغر الأب ، كيف صارا قديمين . وإن جعل الكل فى البذرا قيل له : أي شيء منه الابن ؟ فإن قال : الكل ، صير الكل ابنا وأبا ، وفى ذلك جعل الأب ابنا لنفسه
From him who requires making all parts of the world sons of the larger one, it also requires that he makes every part of the rest likewise, so it becomes entirely sons. Then it is known that the son is smaller than the father, how can they both be eternal? And if he makes the whole in the seed, it is said to him: which thing of it is the son? If he says: the whole, he makes the whole both son and father, and in that he makes the father a son to himself.
Source pages p. 810

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument against the concept of the world having a literal 'son'. Maturidi begins with a reductio: if the world's parts were sons of a larger part, then all parts would be sons, and the son would be smaller and younger than the father, contradicting eternal co-existence. Then he examines the possibility of the whole being the son, which leads to the absurdity of the father being a son to himself. This refutes the Christian notion of an eternal son.

Key Terms
ابن
أب
أبعاض
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world or God can have a literal son who is co-eternal and of the same nature.
Correction: Maturidi shows that an eternal son would be both smaller and larger, younger and co-eternal, which is contradictory. Also, the relationship of fatherhood and sonship cannot apply to the same entity without absurdity.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does Maturidi raise about the concept of the world having a son?
A) The son is smaller than the father, so both cannot be eternal.
B) The son is older than the father.
C) The world has no parts.
D) The father is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's logical argument shows that the concept of an eternal son of the world is rationally impossible because it leads to self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You accept that only Allah is eternal. But in your daily life, do you treat your career, your reputation, or your relationships as though they are eternal? How does Maturidi's argument expose the absurdity of making anything other than Allah co-eternal in your heart? Will you consciously realign your priorities today, giving Allah alone the eternal significance He deserves?
?
Maturidi refutes the idea of a literal son. But ask yourself: in what subtle ways do you treat your own 'sons' – your children, your achievements, your ideas – as if they possess an inherent worth that rivals Allah? How can you repent from that subtle shirk and declare that every created thing is dependent and fleeting?
?
The argument shows that a son must be lesser and younger. Do you approach Allah as a completely dependent servant, or do you sometimes bargain with Him as if you were an equal partner in your salvation? What specific habit or thought pattern do you need to change to fully submit to His Lordship?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Lamp Analogy
Maturidi refutes the lamp analogy for the son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the lamp analogy used by Christians to explain the eternal generation of the son.

Core Idea
The part taken from a lamp is created, not eternal, so the analogy fails.

Christians sometimes use the analogy of a lamp: one flame can light another without diminishing the first. They apply this to the son being generated from the Father. Maturidi examines this analogy carefully and exposes its flaws. In this passage, he shows that the transferred part is new and created, not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection, then a rebuttal, then a follow-up objection, and a final response.
The opponent's first claim is that the son is like a part taken from a lamp.
2
Identify Maturidi's first argument
Maturidi points out that the part taken from a lamp is originated (hadith), so the opponent cannot claim the spirit (son) is eternal if it is like that part.
This invalidates the claim of qidam (eternity) for the son.
3
Analyze the second scenario
If the opponent claims the son is transferred from God (like the lamp flame transferred), the same objection applies: the transferred part is originated.
4
Examine the observation-based objection
The opponent appeals to observation (we see the lamp flame seems continuous). Maturidi offers two possibilities: either God creates it anew, or it emerges like fire from stone—both are originated and created.
5
Conclude the refutation
Maturidi's final question: why allow something originated and created to be called a son? This shows the analogy is invalid for supporting the eternal son.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هو جزء فيه ، من غير أن كان فى كلية الأصل نقصان نحو الجزء المأخوذ من السراج ، عورض بما لو كان الجزء المأخوذ حادثا كما حدث في الذي يؤخذ من السراج ، فيبطل قوله فى قدم الروح ، وهو الابن . وإن زعم منقول من الله كالمأخوذ من السراج حل عليه ما سلف . وبعد ، فها أنه يدريه أن المأخوذ من السراج لا ينتقض ؟ فإن قيل : معاينتنا إياه كذلك ، قيل : لعل الله أحدثه ، أو يكون كالنار في الحجر فيخرج ، وأيهما كان فهو حادث ، والحادث مخلوق ، فلم جاز أن يكون ابنا ؟
If it is said: It is a part in Him, without there being any deficiency in the whole of the origin, like the part taken from a lamp, it is objected: if the part taken was originated as is the case with that which is taken from a lamp, then his statement about the eternity of the spirit is invalidated, and it is the son. And if he claims it is transferred from God like that taken from a lamp, then what preceded applies to him. Then, here he knows that the part taken from the lamp is not diminished? If it is said: we observe it thus, it is said: perhaps God created it, or it is like fire in stone that comes out, and whichever it is, it is originated, and the originated is created, so why is it allowed to be a son?
Source pages p. 810

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The opponent claims the son is like a part taken from a lamp without diminishing the source. Maturidi responds that the taken part is originated (hadith), not eternal, so the spirit cannot be eternal. If they claim transference from God, the same applies. When the opponent appeals to observation (the lamp flame seems continuous), Maturidi answers that either God creates it anew or it emerges like fire from stone—both originated and created. Thus, it cannot be an eternal son.

Key Terms
جزء
سراج
حادث
قدم
روح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The part taken from a lamp is eternal because it seems continuous.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the part is originated (hadith) and created, not eternal. The apparent continuity does not imply eternality.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main objection to the lamp analogy?
A) The part taken from the lamp is originated, not eternal.
B) The lamp is made of glass.
C) The lamp flame is not a good example.
D) The part taken is the same as the source.

Key Takeaway
The lamp analogy fails because the part taken is originated and created, so it cannot represent an eternal, uncreated son.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto an analogy or comparison for your beliefs that seemed convincing at first, only to later realize it was flawed? What did that realization cost you, and how does this lesson challenge you to test your assumptions more rigorously?
?
When faced with a seemingly plausible argument that contradicts core truths, like the eternality of God or His attributes, do you tend to question the argument or your own belief? How can you cultivate the courage to dismantle even comfortable errors in your understanding?
?
The opponent appealed to direct observation to defend his analogy. Where in your life do you rely on 'what seems obvious' rather than on revelation or sound reasoning? What would it mean for you to submit that area to the truth, even if it feels counterintuitive?

Jesus's Miracles Do Not Prove Divinity Alone
Maturidi compares Jesus's miracles to those of Moses and other prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the miracles of Jesus do not uniquely prove his divinity, as similar or greater miracles occurred with other prophets.

Core Idea
Miracles alone cannot prove prophetic uniqueness.

In this passage, Maturidi systematically refutes the claim that Jesus's miracles demonstrate his divinity. By comparing Jesus's wonders to those of Moses, Ezekiel, and others, he shows that miracles are not exclusive to any single prophet. This lesson walks through his logical responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the dialogue structure of objection and response.
The passage is a series of counterarguments.
2
Identify each counterargument
List the specific counterarguments Maturidi uses: Moses's wonders, supplication, weeping/teaching, raising the dead, frequency.
Each counter undermines the uniqueness claim.
3
Reflect on the key question
Ask yourself: If miracles don't prove divinity, what do they prove? Consider that they confirm prophethood but not divine nature.
This is the core of Maturidi's argument.
4
Consider the faqih's addition
The faqih mentions Moses's staff becoming a snake repeatedly as a greater miracle. Why does he add this?
It reinforces that Jesus's miracles are not superior.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp why Ezekiel's raising the dead is relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : من أن الله أظهر منه عجائب . قيل : وقد أظهر من موسى ، فقولوا : هو ابن آخر . فإن زعمتم أن ذلك كان بدعاء وتضرع فمثله أمر غيره مع ما يحكى عن عيسى أنه كان يقول ليلة الأحد اللهم إن كان من مشيئتك أن تصرف هذه الكأس المرة عن أحد فاصرفها عنى. فإن قيل : كان عن عيسى البكاء والتضرع ليعلم الناس ، قيل مثله من موسى . وبعد ، فإنه وموسى : كانا يصليان نحو بيت المقدس ويتضرعان ، ثم البكاء والتضرع فعل الطباع ، لا يمتنع عنها ، فما معنى التعليم ؟ ثم إن استحق هو ذلك بالعمل لزم ذلك فى موسى وغيره. فإن قيل : استحق ذلك باحياء الموتى لا غير ، قيل : قد أحيا حزقيل إنسانا . فإن عارض بالكثرة ، قيل : اليهود يقولون موسى كان أكثر منه . قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وله أحيا عصا ميتة ثعبانا غير مرة ، فهو أعظم .
He said: Because God manifested wonders through him. It was said: He also manifested through Moses, so say that he is a son. If you claim that that was through supplication and humility, then the like of that is the matter of others, along with what is reported about Jesus that he used to say on Sunday night: 'O God, if it is Your will to remove this bitter cup from anyone, remove it from me.' If it is said: Jesus's weeping and supplication was to teach people, it is said the like of it from Moses. Moreover, he and Moses used to pray towards Jerusalem and supplicate, and weeping and supplication are acts of nature, not impossible for it, so what is the meaning of teaching? Then if he deserves that by action, that would apply to Moses and others. If it is said: he deserves that by raising the dead alone, it is said: Ezekiel raised a human. If he objects by frequency, it is said: the Jews say Moses did more than him. The faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: and he (Moses) brought to life a dead staff as a snake more than once, so that is greater.
Source pages p. 810

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation. The opponent argues that God manifested wonders through Jesus. Maturidi responds that the same is true of Moses. If they claim Jesus's supplication was to teach, Moses did likewise. Tears and prayer are natural, not unique. If the claim is that Jesus deserved honor by raising the dead, Maturidi notes that Ezekiel also raised a dead person, and Moses turned a staff into a snake repeatedly—greater miracles according to Jewish tradition. Thus, miracles alone do not prove divinity.

Key Terms
عجائب
موسى
دعاء
بكاء
إحياء الموتى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jesus's miracles are unique and prove his divinity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that similar or greater miracles occurred with other prophets, so miracles alone do not prove divinity.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi mention Ezekiel raising a dead person?
A) To show that raising the dead is not unique to Jesus.
B) To prove Ezekiel is greater than Jesus.
C) To argue that all prophets raised the dead.
D) To deny Jesus ever raised the dead.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that miracles are not exclusive to Jesus, thus they cannot be used as definitive proof of divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because someone performs impressive spiritual acts, they must be closer to God or more favored? How does this passage challenge that assumption in your own life? What will you do differently the next time you are tempted to judge someone's status based on outward signs?
?
This passage shows that even great prophets like Moses and Jesus shared similar experiences of supplication and weeping. When you consider your own prayers and tears, do you ever feel they are insufficient or less valid than those of revered figures? How does knowing that even prophets performed natural human acts of humility change how you approach your own worship?
?
If miracles alone cannot prove divinity, then what does? Reflect on the basis of your own belief in God. Is it built on intellectual conviction from evidence like this, or on emotional experiences? What is one step you can take today to strengthen the intellectual foundation of your faith?

Parallels to Jesus's Miracles in Earlier Prophets
Maturidi refutes specific miracles of Jesus by citing parallels in earlier prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the argument for Jesus's unique divinity by showing that each of his miracles has a parallel in the lives of earlier prophets.

Core Idea
Jesus's miracles are not unique; they mirror earlier prophets' miracles.

In this passage, Maturidi systematically counters those who claim Jesus's miracles prove his special status. He takes each reported miracle—feeding the multitude, turning water to wine, walking on water, ascension, healing the blind—and shows that it was also performed by prophets like Elijah, Elisha, Joshua, or Muhammad. This argument undermines the idea that miracles indicate divinity, since many prophets performed similar acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi responds to each miracle claim in order.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify each miracle of Jesus
Underline the five distinct miracles attributed to Jesus: feeding many, water to wine, walking on water, ascension, healing the blind/leprous.
The passage also mentions crucifixion and fire as additional arguments.
3
Find the parallel for each
For each miracle, note the prophet Maturidi cites as having performed a similar or greater miracle. For example, Muhammad for feeding, Elisha for water to wine, etc.
4
Consider the broader argument
Reflect on how these parallels disprove the uniqueness claim. Also note the points about crucifixion (humiliation) and Elijah's fire (defense).
Maturidi uses these to further undermine the opponents' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختج باطعام البشر الكثير من طعام يسير عورض بنبينا ، إنه أحدث في إناء دقيقا لم يكن فيه . فإن قيل صير الماء خمرا قيل : اليسع أحدثه بلا عدة آنية لامرأة ثم صيره زيتا . وإن احتج بالمشى على الماء فهم يقرون بذلك ليوشع بن نون ولإليا واليسع . وإن استدلوا بالرفع إلى السماء فهم يقرون بذلك لإليا ، وقالوا ارتفع إلى السماء بمشهد من جماعة . وإن احتجوا بابراء الأكمه والأبرص ونحو ذلك فإحياء الميت أعظم منه ، وقد أقروا به لإليا واليسع . مع ما عليهم في إقرارهم أن اليهود صلبوه وهزوا به ، فإن كان الأول يدل على التعظيم فهذا يدل على التصغير . وهلا صنع كصنيع إيليا - حيث أتوه – أن أرسل عليهم نارا فأكلتهم ، أكرمه الله به . وإن رجعوا إلى إظهار العجائب في تحقيق التخصيص عورضوا بمن ذكرت.
And if he argues by feeding a multitude of people from a small amount of food, he is refuted by our Prophet (Muhammad), for he produced in a vessel of fine flour that which was not in it. If it is said: he turned water into wine, it is said: Elisha produced it without vessels for a woman then turned it into oil. And if he argues by walking on water, they acknowledge that for Joshua son of Nun and for Elijah and Elisha. And if they argue by ascension to heaven, they acknowledge that for Elijah, and they said he ascended to heaven in the presence of a group. And if they argue by curing the blind and leprous and the like, raising the dead is greater than that, and they have acknowledged it for Elijah and Elisha. Additionally, they are burdened in their acknowledgment that the Jews crucified him and mocked him, so if the former indicates glorification, this indicates belittlement. And why did he not do as Elijah did when they came to him – that he sent down fire upon them that consumed them, by which God honored him. And if they return to manifesting wonders in establishing particularization, they are refuted by those I mentioned.
Source pages p. 811

Commentary
This passage systematically refutes each miracle of Jesus by providing a parallel from earlier prophets. Feeding many with little: Muhammad. Water to wine: Elisha. Walking on water: Joshua, Elijah, Elisha. Ascension: Elijah. Healing blind: raising the dead is greater and done by Elijah/Elisha. The crucifixion is a humiliation contradicting glorification. Jesus did not defend himself like Elijah did with fire. Thus, miracles do not support unique divinity.

Key Terms
إطعام
ماء خمر
مشي على الماء
رفع إلى السماء
إبراء الأكمه والأبرص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each of Jesus's miracles is unique and unparalleled in history.
Correction: Maturidi shows that each miracle has a clear parallel in the lives of earlier prophets, such as Elijah, Elisha, Joshua, and Muhammad.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how does Jesus's ascension to heaven compare to other prophets?
A) It is unique to Jesus.
B) It was also done by Elijah.
C) It was done by Moses.
D) It never happened.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation shows that miracles are not exclusive to Jesus and therefore cannot be used to prove his unique divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Jesus was crucified and mocked while Elijah called down fire, do you see weakness or a different kind of strength? What does this reveal about your own understanding of divine help in moments of trial?
?
If miracles are not unique to Jesus, then what truly sets a prophet apart? Are you looking for external signs in your faith instead of the inner reality of submission to God?
?
Maturidi lists these parallels to strip away false uniqueness. Is there something in your life you hold as 'special' or 'unrepeatable' that God has actually granted to others before you? How does that humility reshape your worship?

Christ's Power and the Impossibility of Incarnation
Maturidi refutes Christ's divinity by examining the source of his power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that all possible explanations for Christ's miraculous power lead to contradictions, thereby proving that Christ cannot be divine.

Core Idea
Every explanation of Christ's power ends in contradiction.

Maturidi addresses a critical theological question: if Christ performed miracles, what was the source of his power? By systematically examining each possibility—whether Christ acted by a part of God, by a separate part, by connection to God, or by an independent power—Maturidi demonstrates that each leads to an absurdity. This passage thus forms a rigorous refutation of the divinity of Christ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the problem by first asserting God's universal power.
2
Follow the logical options
Identify the different options Maturidi considers for Christ's power: part of God, separate part, connection, created power, self-power. Trace how each option is examined and refuted.
3
Understand the key contradiction
Focus on the core argument: if Christ's power comes from a part of God, then God is the real agent, not Christ. This undermines Christ's divinity.
4
Appreciate the theological stakes
Reflect on why this argument matters for Islamic theology: it upholds the absolute oneness of God and rejects any association of created beings with divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ، فقولوا الله فى السماء وفى الأرض لما أظهر فى كل شيء منها عجائب، فيوجب تخصيص كل شيء من الوجه الذى يخصونه. فإن قال : قوى المسيح على فعله ، لا أن فعل هو به ، قيل : أكان يفعل الأجسام ؟ فإن قال : نعم ، قيل : أهو مخلوق ؟ فإذا قال : نعم ، قيل : بدنه وروحه كبدننا وروحنا ، ما باله قدر على ما لا نقدر عليه ، وقدر على ذلك بقوة هي جزء من الله أو قوة محدثة ، فإن قال بجزء هو يفعل أبطل قوله فى فعل المسيح وهو إله المسيح ويكون هو الله لا المسيح ، وإن انقطع الجزء عن الله فإذا فعل كثيرا من الأجسام غير هو الله . وإن ادعوا اتصاله بالله فيكون الفعل لها ، وهما لله ، فصار إلى أن الله الفاعل . وإن زعموا أن فيه قوة يفعل بها الأجسام لا أنها بفعله جعلوا إله المسيح بعضه يصرفه كيف شاء ، وإن قال : يفعل بنفسه لا بقوة حادثة عورض بناء . على ما قررنا .
Then, say: God is in the heaven and on the earth, because He manifests wonders in every thing of them, so it necessitates specifying every thing from the aspect they specify. If he says: Christ was empowered for his action, not that the action is by him, it is said: did he act upon bodies? If he says: yes, it is said: is he created? If he says: yes, it is said: his body and spirit are like our body and spirit, how is he able to do what we cannot? And he is able to do that by a power that is a part of God or a created power. If he says by a part he acts, he invalidates his statement about the action of Christ, and he is the God of Christ, and he is God not Christ; and if the part is separated from God, then if he does many bodies, he is other than God. And if they claim his connection to God, then the actions belong to Him, and both are for God, so it results that God is the agent. And if they claim that he has a power by which he acts upon bodies, not that it is by His action, they make the God of Christ a part of Him that He directs as He wills. And if he says: he acts by himself, not by an originated power, he is refuted according to what we have established.
Source pages p. 811

Commentary
This passage examines the nature of Christ's power. Maturidi first argues that if God manifests wonders everywhere, then particularizing Christ is arbitrary. Then he considers Christ's power: if Christ acts by a part of God, then God is the actual agent, not Christ. If the part is separate, Christ becomes another god. If connected, God acts. If by a created power, Christ is not divine. If by himself without originated power, the claim is refuted. Thus, all options lead to contradiction.

Key Terms
قوة
جزء من الله
فاعل
اتصال
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christ performed miracles by his own independent power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that either God is the agent or Christ would be another god, both contradicting orthodox Islamic monotheism.

Checkpoint
If Christ acts by a part of God, what problem arises according to Maturidi?
A) The part becomes greater than God.
B) Then God acts, not Christ, so Christ is not divine.
C) The part is created.
D) Christ becomes two persons.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi systematically refutes every possible explanation for Christ's miraculous power, concluding that none can be reconciled with the oneness of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel powerless, do you truly believe that all power belongs to Allah alone, or do you secretly rely on your own abilities? How can you train your heart to depend on Him in moments of weakness?
?
If Maturidi's argument shows that attributing divine power to anyone besides Allah leads to contradiction, where in your life have you unconsciously given someone or something the power that only Allah deserves? What will you do today to reclaim that tawhid?
?
Christ's miracles impressed people—but they were from Allah. Are you impressed by people's talents and successes without recognizing that the true source is Allah? How can you shift your admiration from the created to the Creator, starting right now?

Refuting Miracles and the Crucifixion Contradiction
Maturidi counters Christian miracles with prophetic parallels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes Christian claims for Christ's divinity by pointing to similar miracles by other prophets and the dishonor of the crucifixion.

Core Idea
Miracles alone do not prove divinity; even prophets performed them, and crucifixion contradicts honor.

Maturidi begins by countering each miracle Christians attribute to Christ with examples from earlier prophets. He then introduces a powerful objection: the crucifixion, which Christians admit, indicates dishonor, not honor. This lesson presents that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure of counters and the final crucifixion argument.
The passage lists miracles one by one and responds to each.
2
Identify each miracle and its counter
List each miracle Christians claim and the prophet Maturidi cites as having performed a similar or greater miracle.
There are five miracles and a final point about crucifixion.
3
Analyze the crucifixion argument
Focus on the statement: 'if the first indicates honor, this indicates belittlement'. Understand how the crucifixion admission undermines the honor of Christ.
Maturidi also notes Christ did not defend himself like Elijah.
4
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Why is it effective to show that other prophets performed similar miracles? How does the crucifixion contradiction weaken the Christian case?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختج باطعام البشر الكثير من طعام يسير عورض بنبينا ، إنه أحدث في يكن فيه . فإن قيل صير الماء خمرا قيل : اليسع [أحدثه] بلا عدة آنية لامرأة ثم صيره زيتا . وإن احتج بالمشى على الماء فهم يقرون بذلك ليوشع بن نون ولإليا واليسع . وإن استدلوا بالرفع إلى السماء فهم يقرون بذلك لإليا ، وقالوا ارتفع إلى السماء بمشهد من جماعة . وإن احتجوا بابراء الأكمه والأبرص ونحو ذلك فإحياء الميت أعظم منه ، وقد أقروا به لإليا واليسع . مع ما عليهم في إقرارهم أن اليهود صلبوه وهزوا به ، فإن كان الأول يدل على التعظيم فهذا يدل على التصغير . . وهلا صنع كصنيع إيليا - حيث أتوه – أن أرسل عليهم نارا فأكلتهم ، أكرمه الله به . وإن رجعوا إلى إظهار العجائب في تحقيق التخصيص عورضوا بمن ذكرت . و بعد ، فقولوا الله فى السماء وفى الأرض لما أظهر فى كل شيء منها عجائب، فيوجب تخصيص كل شيء من الوجه الذى يخصونه. فإن قال : قوى المسيح على فعله ، لا أن فعل هو به ، قيل : أكان يفعل الأجسام ؟ فإن قال : نعم ، قيل : أهو مخلوق ؟ فإذا قال : نعم ، قيل : بدنه وروحه كبدننا وروحنا ، ما باله قدر على ما لا نقدر عليه ، وقدر على ذلك بقوة هي جزء من الله أو قوة محدثة ، فإن قال بجزء هو يفعل أبطل قوله فى فعل المسيح وهو إله المسيح ويكون هو الله لا المسيح ، وإن انقطع الجزء عن الله فإذا فعل كثيرا من الأجسام غير هو الله . وإن ادعوا اتصاله بالله فيكون الفعل لها ، وهما لله ، فصار إلى أن الله الفاعل . وإن زعموا أن فيه قوة يفعل بها الأجسام لا أنها بفعله جعلوا إله المسيح بعضه يصرفه كيف شاء ، وإن قال : يفعل بنفسه لا بقوة حادثة عورض بناء . على ما قررنا .
And if he argues by feeding many people with little food, he is countered by our prophet, that he caused [something] to happen in it. And if it is said: He turned water into wine, it is said: Elisha [created it] without many vessels for a woman then turned it into oil. And if he argues by walking on water, they affirm that for Joshua bin Nun and for Elijah and Elisha. And if they argue by raising to heaven, they affirm that for Elijah, and they said: He ascended to heaven in the sight of a group. And if they argue by healing the blind and the leper and the like, then reviving the dead is greater than that, and they have affirmed it for Elijah and Elisha. Moreover, they admit that the Jews crucified him and mocked him; if the first indicates honor, this indicates belittlement. And why did he not do as Elijah did – when they came to him – that he sent fire upon them and it consumed them, by which God honored him. And if they return to manifesting wonders in confirming specification, they are countered by those I mentioned. Then, so say: God is in the heaven and in the earth because He has manifested wonders in everything, so it necessitates specifying everything from the aspect they specify. For if he says: The Messiah had power over his action, not that the action was by him, it is said: Did he create bodies? If he says: Yes, it is said: Is he created? If he says: Yes, it is said: His body and soul are like our body and soul, why was he able to do what we cannot, and he was able to that by a power that is either a part of God or an originated power? If he says by a part that acts, he nullifies his statement about the action of the Messiah while he is the god of the Messiah, and he would be God, not the Messiah. And if the part is separated from God, then if he acts many bodies, it would be other than God. And if they claim his connection to God, then the action would be for them, and they are for God, so it leads to God being the agent. And if they claim that in him is a power by which he acts the bodies, not that it is by His action, they make the god of the Messiah a part of him that he directs as he wills. And if he says: He acts by himself not by an originated power, he is countered on the basis of what we have established.
Source pages p. 811

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Christian arguments claiming Christ's miracles prove his divinity. Maturidi addresses each miracle by showing it was also performed by earlier prophets: feeding many with little food (by our prophet), turning water to wine (Elisha), walking on water (Joshua, Elijah, Elisha), ascension to heaven (Elijah), and healing the blind and leper (Elijah, Elisha — with raising the dead being greater and also attested). He then points out a decisive objection: Christians admit that the Jews crucified and mocked Christ; if miracles indicate honor, the crucifixion indicates dishonor, contradicting their claim. Moreover, Christ did not defend himself with fire like Elijah did, which would have been greater honor. If they argue that wonders manifest specification (for Christ alone), Maturidi counters that God's wonders in all of creation necessitate specifying everything from the same aspect, not just Christ. Then Maturidi shifts to an analysis of Christ's power: Christians may claim Christ had power to act, but not that the action was his own. Maturidi asks: Did Christ create bodies? If they say yes, he asks: Is Christ created? If yes, then why can he do what we cannot? That power must be either a part of God or an originated power. If a part of God, then the agent would be God, not Christ; if the part is separate, then the agent would be other than God. If they claim the power is connected to God, then the action is God's. If they claim Christ has a power by which he acts, then they make God a part controlled by Christ. If they say he acts by himself without an originated power, they are refuted by what was established. Thus Maturidi systematically dismantles Christian arguments for Christ's divinity.

Key Terms
المعجزات
التعظيم
التصغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles performed by Christ prove he is divine.
Correction: Other prophets performed similar or greater miracles, so miracles alone do not prove divinity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which prophet does Maturidi cite as having walked on water?
A) Moses
B) Joshua, Elijah, and Elisha
C) Elijah only
D) Elisha only

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes Christian claims by showing that all Christ's miracles were performed by earlier prophets, and the crucifixion undermines any claim to honor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness someone performing extraordinary deeds, do you immediately attribute truth or divine favor to them? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your criteria for belief beyond outward miracles?
?
Have you ever experienced humiliation or dishonor? How does the contrast between Christ's crucifixion and Elijah's defense with fire affect your understanding of how God honors His prophets? What does this say about true honor in God's sight?
?
If miracles are not proof of divinity, what is the ultimate basis for your faith in Allah's oneness? Are you willing to submit to the Quran's evidence even when it challenges popular narratives?

The Specification Argument and the Nature of Christ's Power
Maturidi rebuts specification and begins analysis of Christ's power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the Christian argument that miracles specify Christ and his analysis of whether Christ created bodies and the source of his power.

Core Idea
God's wonders in all creation undermine the claim that miracles specify Christ alone; Christ's power must be either part of God or originated.

Maturidi first argues that if miracles specify special honor, then God's wonders throughout creation would require specifying everything. He then turns to a key question: Did Christ create bodies? If yes, then his power must be either a part of God or an originated power. This lesson examines that logical progression.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two main sections: the specification rebuttal and the questions about Christ's power.
The passage moves from a general argument to a specific inquiry.
2
Understand the specification rebuttal
Maturidi says that if wonders indicate God's specification, then God's wonders in all creation would necessitate specifying everything, not just Christ.
This counters the claim that Christ's miracles are unique markers of divinity.
3
Follow the power inquiry
Trace the questions: Did Christ create bodies? Is he created? If so, why can he do what we cannot? Then the conclusion about his power: it must be either a part of God or an originated power.
This sets up the logical choices to be refuted in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why is it important to determine whether Christ's power is part of God or created? How does this undermine Christian claims?
This leads to the contradictions detailed in the next lesson.

Misconception: Christ's power is unique and proves he is divine.
