Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if Christ created bodies, what must be the nature of the power enabling that?
A) It is part of Christ's own essence
B) It is either a part of God or an originated power
C) It is a miracle granted temporarily
D) It is a natural ability

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that if Christ created bodies, his power must be either part of God or created, both problematic for Christian claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a blessing or a miracle in your life, do you secretly believe it is because you are specially chosen? How does Maturidi's reminder that God's wonders are in all creation humble that assumption, and what would it look like to truly see yourself as no more special than any other creature?
?
If Christ's power must have a source—either divine or created—what does that expose about your own tendency to view your talents and abilities as self-originated? Are you willing to admit that every power you have is created and dependent on God, or do you cling to a sense of autonomous control?
?
This logical dilemma forces the Christian to choose between two problematic options for Christ's power. In what ways do you still ascribe divinity to created things—your career, your reputation, your relationships—and what would it mean to treat them as purely originated and accountable to God alone?

7 min
Logical Consequences of Christ's Power Options
Maturidi analyzes consequences of Christ's power being part of God or originated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradictions Maturidi derives from each possibility for the source of Christ's power, affirming that only God is the true agent.

Core Idea
Each option for Christ's power leads to a contradiction: if part of God, God acts; if separate, not God; if connected, God acts; if Christ controls it, he controls God.

Maturidi now examines the two possibilities for Christ's power: that it is a part of God or an originated power. He shows that each leads to absurd conclusions that undermine Christian doctrine. This lesson traces those consequences and the final refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the series of conditional statements (if...then).
The passage is structured as a logical analysis of each possibility.
2
Trace the first option: power as part of God
If the power is a part of God that acts, then the agent is God, not Christ. If the part is separated, then the agent is other than God.
Both sub-options contradict Christian claims.
3
Trace the second option: power as originated
If they claim the power is connected to God, then God is the agent. If they claim Christ has a power he controls, then God is controlled by Christ.
These are also problematic.
4
Consider the final refutation
If they claim Christ acts by himself without an originated power, Maturidi says this is refuted by what was already established.
This closes the argument.

وإن اختج باطعام البشر الكثير من طعام يسير عورض بنبينا ، إنه أحدث في يكن فيه . فإن قيل صير الماء خمرا قيل : اليسع [أحدثه] بلا عدة آنية لامرأة ثم صيره زيتا . وإن احتج بالمشى على الماء فهم يقرون بذلك ليوشع بن نون ولإليا واليسع . وإن استدلوا بالرفع إلى السماء فهم يقرون بذلك لإليا ، وقالوا ارتفع إلى السماء بمشهد من جماعة . وإن احتجوا بابراء الأكمه والأبرص ونحو ذلك فإحياء الميت أعظم منه ، وقد أقروا به لإليا واليسع . مع ما عليهم في إقرارهم أن اليهود صلبوه وهزوا به ، فإن كان الأول يدل على التعظيم فهذا يدل على التصغير . . وهلا صنع كصنيع إيليا - حيث أتوه – أن أرسل عليهم نارا فأكلتهم ، أكرمه الله به . وإن رجعوا إلى إظهار العجائب في تحقيق التخصيص عورضوا بمن ذكرت . و بعد ، فقولوا الله فى السماء وفى الأرض لما أظهر فى كل شيء منها عجائب، فيوجب تخصيص كل شيء من الوجه الذى يخصونه. فإن قال : قوى المسيح على فعله ، لا أن فعل هو به ، قيل : أكان يفعل الأجسام ؟ فإن قال : نعم ، قيل : أهو مخلوق ؟ فإذا قال : نعم ، قيل : بدنه وروحه كبدننا وروحنا ، ما باله قدر على ما لا نقدر عليه ، وقدر على ذلك بقوة هي جزء من الله أو قوة محدثة ، فإن قال بجزء هو يفعل أبطل قوله فى فعل المسيح وهو إله المسيح ويكون هو الله لا المسيح ، وإن انقطع الجزء عن الله فإذا فعل كثيرا من الأجسام غير هو الله . وإن ادعوا اتصاله بالله فيكون الفعل لها ، وهما لله ، فصار إلى أن الله الفاعل . وإن زعموا أن فيه قوة يفعل بها الأجسام لا أنها بفعله جعلوا إله المسيح بعضه يصرفه كيف شاء ، وإن قال : يفعل بنفسه لا بقوة حادثة عورض بناء . على ما قررنا .
And if he argues by feeding many people with little food, he is countered by our prophet, that he caused [something] to happen in it. And if it is said: He turned water into wine, it is said: Elisha [created it] without many vessels for a woman then turned it into oil. And if he argues by walking on water, they affirm that for Joshua bin Nun and for Elijah and Elisha. And if they argue by raising to heaven, they affirm that for Elijah, and they said: He ascended to heaven in the sight of a group. And if they argue by healing the blind and the leper and the like, then reviving the dead is greater than that, and they have affirmed it for Elijah and Elisha. Moreover, they admit that the Jews crucified him and mocked him; if the first indicates honor, this indicates belittlement. And why did he not do as Elijah did – when they came to him – that he sent fire upon them and it consumed them, by which God honored him. And if they return to manifesting wonders in confirming specification, they are countered by those I mentioned. Then, so say: God is in the heaven and in the earth because He has manifested wonders in everything, so it necessitates specifying everything from the aspect they specify. For if he says: The Messiah had power over his action, not that the action was by him, it is said: Did he create bodies? If he says: Yes, it is said: Is he created? If he says: Yes, it is said: His body and soul are like our body and soul, why was he able to do what we cannot, and he was able to that by a power that is either a part of God or an originated power? If he says by a part that acts, he nullifies his statement about the action of the Messiah while he is the god of the Messiah, and he would be God, not the Messiah. And if the part is separated from God, then if he acts many bodies, it would be other than God. And if they claim his connection to God, then the action would be for them, and they are for God, so it leads to God being the agent. And if they claim that in him is a power by which he acts the bodies, not that it is by His action, they make the god of the Messiah a part of him that he directs as he wills. And if he says: He acts by himself not by an originated power, he is countered on the basis of what we have established.
Source pages p. 811

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Christian arguments claiming Christ's miracles prove his divinity. Maturidi addresses each miracle by showing it was also performed by earlier prophets: feeding many with little food (by our prophet), turning water to wine (Elisha), walking on water (Joshua, Elijah, Elisha), ascension to heaven (Elijah), and healing the blind and leper (Elijah, Elisha — with raising the dead being greater and also attested). He then points out a decisive objection: Christians admit that the Jews crucified and mocked Christ; if miracles indicate honor, the crucifixion indicates dishonor, contradicting their claim. Moreover, Christ did not defend himself with fire like Elijah did, which would have been greater honor. If they argue that wonders manifest specification (for Christ alone), Maturidi counters that God's wonders in all of creation necessitate specifying everything from the same aspect, not just Christ. Then Maturidi shifts to an analysis of Christ's power: Christians may claim Christ had power to act, but not that the action was his own. Maturidi asks: Did Christ create bodies? If they say yes, he asks: Is Christ created? If yes, then why can he do what we cannot? That power must be either a part of God or an originated power. If a part of God, then the agent would be God, not Christ; if the part is separate, then the agent would be other than God. If they claim the power is connected to God, then the action is God's. If they claim Christ has a power by which he acts, then they make God a part controlled by Christ. If they say he acts by himself without an originated power, they are refuted by what was established. Thus Maturidi systematically dismantles Christian arguments for Christ's divinity.

Key Terms
القوة
الإله
المسيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christ acted by his own independent divine power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any understanding of Christ's power leads to God being the true agent, or to absurdities like Christ controlling God.

Checkpoint
If Christians claim Christ's power is a part of God that acts, what is Maturidi's conclusion?
A) The power is independent
B) God is the agent, not Christ
C) Christ acts by his own will
D) The part is separate from God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi systematically demonstrates that the Christian understanding of Christ's power leads to theological contradictions, affirming that only God is the true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim that God alone is the true agent in all things, do you actually live as if your own choices, efforts, and abilities are entirely dependent on Him—or do you still quietly assume that you control the outcomes of your life? Where do you catch yourself acting as if your own 'power' is independent of God?
?
Maturidi's argument destroys every attempt to ascribe independent agency to anyone other than God. Are there people or systems in your life—a leader, a teacher, a institution—that you subconsciously treat as having 'divine-like' control over your well-being? Will you actively remind yourself today that only God truly acts?
?
The logic of this passage leaves no room for Christ to 'direct' a part of God. Yet how often do you try to direct God's blessings, guidance, or forgiveness as if He were a tool to be wielded for your purposes? What would it look like for you to fully surrender your plans and desires, acknowledging that God is the Agent and you are the servant?

Refuting the Claim of Divine Sonship as Honor
Maturidi refutes the honorific 'son' as implying divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the argument that calling Christ 'son' is the greatest honor, showing that 'father' is a greater honorific and that the term is shared with others.

Core Idea
Honorifics like 'son' do not imply divinity; they are shared and less than 'father'.

Maturidi addresses a Christian justification for calling Christ 'son of God' as a form of honor. He counters that 'O my Father' is actually a greater honor, and even if such terms are used for honor, they are applied to other prophets and disciples, so Christ is not unique. This lesson walks through his dialectical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Christian claim
The passage begins by presenting the Christian argument that calling someone 'son' is the highest honor. Read the opening sentence and identify the claim.
2
Follow the counter-argument
Maturidi responds that 'O my Father' is greater in glorification, and that if 'son' implies precedence, it undermines the honor meaning. Trace how he rebuts each point.
Notice the back-and-forth style typical of kalam.
3
Examine the examples of shared usage
Maturidi shows that 'son' is used for others like disciples and prophets, and also that 'brother' is used without literal brotherhood. This shows the term is not exclusive to Christ.
4
Conclude with the limits of prophethood
He argues that prophethood requires shared species, so calling a non-human 'son' is impermissible, and God may have friends but not sons.
This sets up the next lesson on lordship vs servitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض من يقول ليس من الإكرام أعظم من قوله : يا بني ، قيل : بلى : يا أبي أكبر فى التعظيم . ولو قال : يوجب التقدم ، أبطل اعتباره بالتعظيم ؛ ذلك الوجه لا يراد بذلك . ثم إذ ثبت ذا لعل غيره ممن قد سماه به . فإن من قيل : فى ذلك تسوية بنفسه ، قيل : قد يقول الرجل لآخر : يا أخي ، ولا يريد وبعد ، فإن فى خلقه الكرام ، ولعل غيره سمی به ، فيشركه فيه الحواريون والأنبياء . وعورض بالجليل من الأمور أنه يجوز القول به على الإكرام ، قيل : أما النبوة فلا تجوز إلا فى متفق الجنس ؛ لأنه لا يجوز أن يقول للحمار والكلب فى الأول ، وفى الجملة جهة المحبة والولاية ، ويكون في غير الجنس كما يجب الحق في جهة الولاية والمحبة والملائكة ونحو ذلك . مع ما يجوز أن يكون الله أخلاء وأحباب من الخلق ولا يجوز مثله فى البنين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل في هذا عندنا أن الاختلاف رجع إلى وجهين : أحدهما الربوبية ، والله تعالى جل ثناؤه قد بين إحالة ذلك بأكله وشربه ، ودفع الحاجات إلى مكان ، ووصفه بالصغر والكهولة ، وعبادته لله تعالى وتضرعه له وخضوعه ، ودعائه الخلق إلى عبادة الله وتوحيده ، وبشارته بمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وإيمانه بالرسل ، ثم جعل جل ثناؤه عليه جميع آيات الحدث وإمارات العبودة لنفسه ما جعل في جميع العالم ، وكذلك هو صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يدع
Then he counters the one who says: There is no honor greater than His saying: O my son. It is said: Yes, O my Father is greater in glorification. And if he says: It necessitates precedence, he nullifies consideration by honor; that aspect is not intended by that. Then since this is established, perhaps another whom he has named by it. For if it is said: In that is an equalization of himself, it is said: A man may say to another: O my brother, without intending [brotherhood]. And then, for in his creation are the noble, and perhaps another is named by it, so the disciples and prophets share with him in it. And he is countered by the great of matters that it is permissible to say it for honor. It is said: As for prophethood, it is only permissible in agreement of species; for it is not permissible to say to the donkey and dog initially, and in sum, the aspect of love and loyalty, and it may be in other than the species as the truth requires in the aspect of loyalty, love, and the angels and the like. Additionally, God may have close friends and beloved ones from creation, but the like is not permissible for sons, and there is no power except with God. The principle in this according to us is that the disagreement returns to two aspects: One of them is lordship, and God the Exalted, may His praise be glorified, has explained the impossibility of that by his eating and drinking, and relieving needs in a place, and his description by childhood and old age, and his worship of God the Exalted, his supplication and humility, and his calling creation to worship God and His oneness, and his giving good news of Muhammad peace be upon him and his faith in the messengers, then God the Exalted placed upon him all the signs of temporality and marks of servitude for Himself which He placed in all the world, and likewise he peace be upon him did not claim...
Source pages p. 812

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian claim that calling Christ 'son' (ya bani) indicates great honor. Maturidi counters that 'O my Father' (ya abi) is greater in glorification. If someone argues that calling 'son' implies precedence in honor, that nullifies the consideration of honor as the intended meaning. Even if the term is used for honor, others (disciples, prophets) are also called by it, so Christ is not unique. Regarding prophethood, it is only permissible within the same species (human), so the title has limits. God may have friends and beloved ones from creation, but not sons. The fundamental principle is that the disagreement ultimately returns to two aspects: lordship (rububiyya) and servitude (ubudiyya). God showed the impossibility of Christ's divinity through his human attributes: eating, drinking, physical needs, growing from child to old, worshipping God, supplicating, calling to worship God alone, announcing the coming of Muhammad (peace be upon him), and believing in the prophets. Thus God placed upon Christ all signs of temporality and servitude that He placed in all creation. Christ himself never claimed divinity. This concludes the refutation of Christian doctrines by highlighting Christ's complete dependence on God.

Key Terms
يا بني
يا أبي
التعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling Jesus 'son of God' in the Bible is an honorific that proves his divinity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that honorifics like 'son' are not exclusive to Jesus and are less than 'father' in honor, so they do not imply divinity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the expression 'O my Father' indicate compared to 'O my son'?
A) They are equal in honor
B) 'O my Father' is greater in glorification
C) 'O my son' is greater in glorification
D) Neither indicates honor

Key Takeaway
Honorific titles like 'son' do not prove divinity; they are shared, lesser than 'father', and do not make Christ unique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek honor from others, do you ever demand titles that elevate you above your true station? How does Maturidi’s argument about 'son' being less than 'father' challenge your own desire for recognition?
?
This lesson shows that even the greatest honorifics are shared among creation. Are you willing to accept that no title or status can make you unique before God, and that true honor comes only from Him?
?
Maturidi argues that prophethood is limited to the same species. Could your own pride cause you to claim a spiritual rank that is not yours, like the Christians claimed for Christ? How will you guard against this?

Distinguishing Lordship from Servitude in Christ
Maturidi distinguishes lordship from servitude through Christ's human attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental principle that the debate about Christ's nature reduces to two aspects: lordship and servitude, and how Maturidi uses Christ's human limitations to establish his servitude.

Core Idea
Christ's human attributes conclusively prove his servitude, not lordship.

After refuting the honorific argument, Maturidi presents the core principle: the disagreement returns to two aspects—lordship and servitude. He then uses compelling evidence from Christ's life—eating, drinking, growing old, worshipping, and praying—to show that Christ displayed all the signs of temporality and servitude, confirming he is a servant of God, not divine. This lesson explores that conclusive argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects
Read the opening sentence: 'The principle in this is that the disagreement returns to two aspects: lordship and servitude.' Understand that the entire debate hinges on which aspect applies to Christ.
2
List the evidence of Christ's humanity
Maturidi lists specific actions and attributes of Christ that show his need and dependence: eating, drinking, relieving needs, growing from child to old, worshipping, supplicating, calling to God, announcing Muhammad (PBUH), and believing in prophets. Note each one.
These are all signs of createdness (ayat al-hadath).
3
Understand the conclusion
God placed upon Christ all the signs of temporality and marks of servitude that He placed on all creation. This shows Christ is fully human and a servant, not divine.
The passage ends with 'and likewise he (PBUH) did not claim' – implying Christ never claimed divinity.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this evidence conclusively distinguishes lordship from servitude, confirming Islamic tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your daily life—your need to eat, sleep, and relieve yourself—do you recognize these as constant reminders that you are entirely dependent on Allah? What one habit can you adopt this week to turn these ordinary acts into conscious acts of servitude?
?
Christ's worship and supplication were marked by deep humility. When you pray, do you truly humble yourself before Allah, or do you rush through it with pride? Commit to performing your next salah with complete mental and physical humility as if you are standing before your Lord alone.
?
Maturidi argues that even giving glad tidings of a future prophet is evidence of being a servant. Do you ever think that your acts of worship or service make you independent or special? How can you constantly remind yourself that every good deed is a gift from Allah and a mark of your need for Him, not a claim to status?

Refutation of 'O my Son' as Greatest Honor
Maturidi counters the claim that 'O my son' is the highest honor by noting a greater honorific and metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the honorific 'O my son' does not imply divinity because a greater honorific exists and such titles are used metaphorically.

Core Idea
Honorific titles like 'son' are not proof of divinity.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to a Christian claim that calling Jesus 'son' in the Gospel is a mark of highest honor, implying divinity. He counters by pointing out that 'O my father' is an even greater honor, and that such titles are often used metaphorically among humans without implying literal relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Notice the opponent's claim and Maturidi's response.
The passage is dense with objections and answers.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that 'O my son' is the greatest honor. Find this in the text.
3
Understand Maturidi's first counter
Maturidi says 'O my father' is greater. Why? Because it implies higher veneration.
This shows that not every honorific implies divinity.
4
See the metaphorical use
Maturidi gives the example of a man saying 'O my brother' without intending brotherhood. This shows such titles are used metaphorically.
5
Recognize sharing
Prophets and disciples are also called by such honors, so it's not unique to Jesus.
This undermines the claim of divinity.

ثم عارض من يقول ليس من الإكرام أعظم من قوله: يا بني، قيل: بلى: يا أبي أكبر فى التعظيم. ولو قال: يوجب التقدم، أبطل اعتباره بالتعظيم؛ ذلك الوجه لا يراد بذلك. ثم إذ ثبت ذا لعل غيره ممن قد سماه به. فإن من قيل: فى ذلك تسوية بنفسه، قيل: قد يقول الرجل لآخر: يا أخي، ولا يريد وبعد، فإن فى خلقه الكرام، ولعل غيره سمی به، فيشركه فيه الحواريون والأنبياء. وعورض بالجليل من الأمور أنه يجوز القول به على الإكرام، قيل: أما النبوة فلا تجوز إلا فى متفق الجنس؛ لأنه لا يجوز أن يقول للحمار والكلب فى الأول، وفى الجملة جهة المحبة والولاية، ويكون في غير الجنس كما يجب الحق في جهة الولاية والمحبة والملائكة ونحو ذلك. مع ما يجوز أن يكون فلذلك لم يجز الله أخلاء وأحباب من الخلق ولا يجوز مثله فى البنين، ولا قوة إلا بالله الأقدار.
Then he who says, 'There is no greater honor than His saying: O my son,' is opposed. It is said: Rather, 'O my father' is greater in veneration. And if he said: it necessitates precedence, his consideration is invalidated by veneration; that aspect is not intended by that. Then, when this is established, perhaps another [person] whom he has named by it. For he who said: in that is equating himself, it is said: a man may say to another: O my brother, and he does not intend [brotherhood]. And moreover, among His creatures there are the honorable, and perhaps another is named by it, so the disciples and the prophets share in it with him. And it is objected by 'the great of matters' that it is permissible to say it for honor. It is said: As for prophethood, it is not permissible except in a agreeing genus; for he may not say to the donkey and the dog in the first [instance], and in summary the aspect of love and loyalty, and it exists in a different genus as the truth requires in the aspect of loyalty and love and the angels and the like. With what may be, therefore God did not permit friends and beloved ones from creation, and the like of it is not permissible for sons, and there is no power except through God, the decrees.
Source pages p. 812

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument about the use of the term 'son' (يا بني) as an honorific. The opponent claims that calling Jesus 'son' in the Gospel is the greatest honor. Maturidi retorts that 'O my father' (يا أبي) is actually a greater honor. He then argues that such titles (like 'brother') are often used metaphorically and do not imply literal sonship or divinity. Moreover, prophets and disciples are also called by similar terms of honor. He further refutes the idea that such a title could be used for honor across different categories (human vs. animal). Ultimately, he concludes that God does not allow such honorifics to be used for sons in a literal sense, and the whole argument collapses.

Key Terms
الإكرام
يا بني
يا أبي
التعظيم
الحواريون
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The title 'son' is the greatest honor one can receive from God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'O my father' is actually greater in veneration, and such titles are metaphorical and shared among many prophets.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi counter the claim that 'O my son' is the greatest honor?
A) He agrees but says it only applies to Jesus.
B) He says 'O my father' is a greater honor.
C) He says the phrase is not found in scripture.
D) He says the phrase implies sonship by nature.

Key Takeaway
Honorific titles like 'son' are not proof of divinity because they are used metaphorically and shared among many.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the phrase 'O my son' addressed to Jesus, do you feel a pull to grant him a status beyond human? Examine your heart: are you still tying worth to titles and unique designations? What would it take for you to see all human titles as mere metaphors before God's absolute transcendence?
?
The lesson shows that honorifics like 'son' are shared among many prophets and disciples. Where in your own life do you cling to a title or role as if it sets you apart from others in God's sight? Are you willing to release that attachment and see yourself as one among many servants?
?
If the greatest honor is not in being called 'son' but in sincere submission, what honor are you truly seeking? When you pray, do you seek the status of a name, or the reality of servitude? How can you reorient your desires today to seek only His pleasure, regardless of what you are called?

Category Restriction on Honorifics
Maturidi refutes the objection that honorifics are permissible by invoking category restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that honorific titles like 'son' cannot be used across different categories, refuting the Christian claim.

Core Idea
Honorifics are restricted by category; calling Jesus 'son' of God is invalid.

In this continuation, Maturidi addresses a further objection: that honorifics can be used for veneration. He responds by explaining that such uses are only permissible within a common genus, and that applying a term like 'son' to a human in relation to God would cross categories, which is not allowed. He also notes that God did not permit such honorifics for sons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this section.
This part introduces a new objection.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that it is permissible to use honorifics for veneration. Find where that appears.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi states that prophethood requires agreeing genus, and you cannot say such things to a donkey or dog.
This shows a category restriction.
4
See the broader principle
Honorifics for love and loyalty exist, but not across different genera.
5
Grasp the conclusion
God did not allow such honorifics for sons, so the argument fails.

Why does Maturidi use the example of a donkey and dog?

?

?
Do you ever use terms of endearment or honor for created beings that elevate them to a status that only Allah deserves? Think of a specific relationship or person. How can you reform your speech to keep every honorific within its proper genus?
?
When you hear someone say 'son of God' today, does your heart reject it with the same logical clarity that Maturidi demonstrates, or does it seem like a mere difference of metaphor? Ask yourself: what category error might you be tolerating in your own beliefs about Allah that needs correction?

Refutation of the Deification of Christ
Maturidi refutes Jesus' divinity using logical and historical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that claiming divinity for Jesus is logically and historically inconsistent.

Core Idea
Calling Jesus divine is self-contradictory and undermines his prophetic role.

Maturidi opens his refutation by pointing out the absurdity of ascribing divinity to a human prophet. He highlights the inconsistency of those who rejected Jesus' prophethood during his life but later elevated him to lordship after his departure. This passage exposes the logical flaw in the Christian position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the structure: Maturidi starts with the claim of divinity and then contrasts it with the historical behavior of Christians.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Find the logical argument (if divinity for Jesus, then for any human) and the historical argument (Christians rejected his prophethood but later claimed lordship).
These two arguments are intertwined.
3
Reflect on the inconsistency
Why does Maturidi emphasize that Christians did not accept Jesus even as a prophet during his life? How does this strengthen his refutation?
It shows their claim is not based on evidence but on later invention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبودة والرسالة . فالقول له بالإلهية قول لا معنى له مع ما لو جاز ذلك لجاز لكل من البشر . والعجب أنهم لم يكونوا فى حياته ومقامه في الأرض يرضون له رتبة الرسالة مع ما له من البراهين . ثم بعد رفعه ، أو موته عند عامتهم ، لم يرضوا له بالعبودة والرسالة حتى جعلوا له رتبة الربوبية ليشهد عليهم بالخلقة والجوهر والبيان وكل شيء منه بالكذب فى الابتداء والانتهاء . والثانى أن يكون ابنه ، وذلك يخرج على وجوه : أحدها الولاد ، وذلك محال / فاسد لغنى الرب عن أن تمسه الحاجة أو تغلبه الشهوة أو تعتريه الوحشة ، وهن أسباب طلب الولاد ، على إحالة كون الولاد من غير جوهر الوالد ، والله تعالى بذاته خارج عن شبه الخلق ، أو عن المعنى الذى يحتمل ذلك الوجه ، وعلى ما بين الله أنه لو اتخذ لهوا لما احتمل أن يتخذ مما عندنا . رب يوجب ذلك ، وبعد ، فإن كل ذى ولد يحتمل الشرك وزوال ملكه إليه ، ومن هو بذاته ملك قادر لا يحتمل ذلك . ومن يقول لا معنى له أن يكون جزء من الشيء ولده ، ويجب أن يكون غير كامل حتى يوجد ، وجهة الآيات لا لأن طريق معرفة البنوة في الشاهد ليس الآيات مع ما قد شورك فيها . وبعد ، هو يدعى الصدق فى الخلوص له بالعبودة ، لا غير ، أو من جهة الفضل ينسب إلى ذلك ، والأمر المعروف في الشاهد أن ذلك ليس من أسماء التعظيم ، بل تسميته المسيح والرسول أجل وأعظم في ذلك . وبعد ، فقد [أعطيت] لكثير من الخلق من الله تعالى كرامات خصوا بها ، لم يوجب شيء منها اسم البنوة ، على أن البنوة فى الكلام إنما هو من الصغار أصحاب القوة والرفعة ، وهكذا شأن أثر البنين فيكون بها إكرامه . والضعاف ، لا من التعظيم وتعظيمه بصغره ؛ إذ قد يكون ذلك من العظماء فى الصغار ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .. أو أن يكون الله من حيث مفزعه وملجأه فى كل أمر ونائبة ، فمن ذا الوجه كل الخلق كذلك ، وتلك كتسمية الهاوية أم أهلها ، والأرض أم أهلها ، فمن ذا الوجه يكون من حيث المفزع للخلق والمعمود إليه وإن / كان لا يتكلم بمثله إلا بإذن ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Servitude and messengerhood. So the statement of divinity for him is a statement with no meaning, given that if that were permissible, it would be permissible for every human. And amazing is that they were not satisfied during his life and his stay on earth that he should have the rank of messengerhood despite the proofs he had. Then after his raising, or his death according to their majority, they were not satisfied with servitude and messengerhood for him until they gave him the rank of lordship, so that they might witness against themselves regarding creation, substance, and speech, and everything from him (being) false from beginning to end. And the second is that he be His son, and that proceeds upon aspects: one of them is birth, and that is impossible/corrupt, because the Lord is free from need being touched by want, or desire overcoming Him, or loneliness afflicting Him, and these are the causes of seeking birth, given the impossibility of birth without the substance of the parent, and God most high in His essence is outside similarity to creation, or to the meaning that admits that aspect, and according to what God clarified that if He had taken some amusement, it would not be possible that He take it from what we have. The Lord necessitates that. And moreover, every one who has a son is susceptible to partnership and the passing of his dominion to him, while He who is essentially a sovereign, powerful, is not susceptible to that. And whoever says (that a part of a thing is its son) has no meaning, because it must be that (the son) is not complete until it exists, and the indication of the signs is not because the way of knowing sonship in the witnessed world is not (through) signs, since they have been participated in. And moreover, he claims truthfulness in his exclusive devotion to Him in servitude, not otherwise, or in terms of excellence, he is attributed to that, and the known matter in the witnessed world is that this is not among the names of glorification; rather, calling him the Messiah and messenger is more dignified and greater in that. And moreover, I have given to many of creation from God most high miracles with which they were distinguished; none of them necessitated the name of sonship, given that sonship in speech is only from the young ones, the possessors of strength and high rank, and such is the effect of sons in that he is honored by it. And the weak ones, not of glorification and magnifying him by his smallness; for that may be from the great ones in the small, and there is no power except with God. Or that God be in terms of being his refuge and shelter in every matter and calamity; then from this aspect all of creation are like that, and that is like naming the pit of hell the mother of its inhabitants, and the earth the mother of its inhabitants. So from this aspect (he is) in terms of being the refuge for creation and the one sought, although he does not speak like that except by permission, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 813

Commentary
This passage refutes Christian claims about Jesus being God or the son of God. Maturidi first argues that claiming divinity for Jesus is meaningless because if it were permissible for him, it would be permissible for any human. He points out the inconsistency: during Jesus' life on earth, people were not even willing to grant him the rank of prophethood despite clear proofs, but after his ascension (or death according to most Christians), they went beyond servitude and prophethood to assign him lordship—thus contradicting themselves and falsifying all evidence about him. Then he refutes the claim of sonship. He examines the possible meanings of 'son': first, biological birth, which is impossible for God because He is free from need, desire, and loneliness—the reasons for seeking offspring. Moreover, birth requires a substance like the parent, and God is beyond similarity to creation. Also, God stated that if He desired amusement, He would not take it from what we have. Second, the concept of having a son implies possibility of partnership and transfer of dominion, while God is essentially sovereign and omnipotent, above that. Third, the notion that a part of something is its son is meaningless because the part is incomplete until it exists, and signs (miracles) do not prove sonship since many prophets received miracles without being called sons. Fourth, calling Jesus 'son' is not a term of honor; rather, 'Messiah' and 'messenger' are greater titles. Fifth, the term 'son' is used metaphorically for those who are small and weak, not for glorification. Sixth, if 'son' means refuge, then all creation are like that, just as hell is called 'mother' of its inhabitants. Therefore, the claim of sonship is refuted from all angles.

Key Terms
الإلهية
الرسالة
العبودة
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the consequence if claiming divinity for Jesus were permissible?
A) It would prove Jesus is uniquely divine.
B) It would be permissible for every human to claim divinity.
C) It would confirm Jesus' prophethood.
D) It would show God's power.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that claiming Jesus is God is both logically inconsistent and historically contradicted by the Christians' own behavior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you held onto a belief about God or religion because it was comfortable, even though it conflicted with clear evidence from revelation or reason? How can you train yourself to submit to truth even when it humbles you?
?
The Christians rejected Jesus' prophethood during his life but later accepted his divinity. Are there areas in your life where you consistently reject God's guidance in the moment, only to later claim you believe in His power? What specific step will you take today to close that gap?
?
If Maturidi's argument were applied to your own theological convictions—that claiming divinity for a human is a 'meaningless statement'—would any of your beliefs about God's attributes or actions crumble under logical scrutiny? Will you risk examining them, or are you satisfied with inconsistency?

10 min
Refutation of the Divine Sonship of Christ
Maturidi refutes divine sonship by examining and rejecting six possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's multifaceted refutation of the claim that Jesus is the son of God, covering biological, metaphorical, and honorific senses.

Core Idea
Calling Jesus 'son of God' is meaningless in every possible sense—biological, metaphorical, or honorific.

Maturidi systematically dismantles the concept of sonship by examining its possible meanings. He shows that none of them can apply to God, and even if taken metaphorically, they either contradict God's nature or are shared by all creation. This passage is a masterclass in logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly. It lists several 'aspects' (wujuh) of sonship. Try to identify each one.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Examine each aspect of sonship
For each aspect Maturidi mentions, ask: Why does he reject it? What is his reasoning?
Aspects include: biological birth, partnership, part-whole, signs, honorific, metaphorical refuge.
3
Reflect on the cumulative argument
How do these separate arguments work together to completely refute the claim? Is there any possible meaning left?
Maturidi leaves no loophole.

Servitude and messengerhood. So the statement of divinity for him is a statement with no meaning, given that if that were permissible, it would be permissible for every human. And amazing is that they were not satisfied during his life and his stay on earth that he should have the rank of messengerhood despite the proofs he had. Then after his raising, or his death according to their majority, they were not satisfied with servitude and messengerhood for him until they gave him the rank of lordship, so that they might witness against themselves regarding creation, substance, and speech, and everything from him (being) false from beginning to end. And the second is that he be His son, and that proceeds upon aspects: one of them is birth, and that is impossible/corrupt, because the Lord is free from need being touched by want, or desire overcoming Him, or loneliness afflicting Him, and these are the causes of seeking birth, given the impossibility of birth without the substance of the parent, and God most high in His essence is outside similarity to creation, or to the meaning that admits that aspect, and according to what God clarified that if He had taken some amusement, it would not be possible that He take it from what we have. The Lord necessitates that. And moreover, every one who has a son is susceptible to partnership and the passing of his dominion to him, while He who is essentially a sovereign, powerful, is not susceptible to that. And whoever says (that a part of a thing is its son) has no meaning, because it must be that (the son) is not complete until it exists, and the indication of the signs is not because the way of knowing sonship in the witnessed world is not (through) signs, since they have been participated in. And moreover, he claims truthfulness in his exclusive devotion to Him in servitude, not otherwise, or in terms of excellence, he is attributed to that, and the known matter in the witnessed world is that this is not among the names of glorification; rather, calling him the Messiah and messenger is more dignified and greater in that. And moreover, I have given to many of creation from God most high miracles with which they were distinguished; none of them necessitated the name of sonship, given that sonship in speech is only from the young ones, the possessors of strength and high rank, and such is the effect of sons in that he is honored by it. And the weak ones, not of glorification and magnifying him by his smallness; for that may be from the great ones in the small, and there is no power except with God. Or that God be in terms of being his refuge and shelter in every matter and calamity; then from this aspect all of creation are like that, and that is like naming the pit of hell the mother of its inhabitants, and the earth the mother of its inhabitants. So from this aspect (he is) in terms of being the refuge for creation and the one sought, although he does not speak like that except by permission, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 813

Commentary
This passage refutes Christian claims about Jesus being God or the son of God. Maturidi first argues that claiming divinity for Jesus is meaningless because if it were permissible for him, it would be permissible for any human. He points out the inconsistency: during Jesus' life on earth, people were not even willing to grant him the rank of prophethood despite clear proofs, but after his ascension (or death according to most Christians), they went beyond servitude and prophethood to assign him lordship—thus contradicting themselves and falsifying all evidence about him. Then he refutes the claim of sonship. He examines the possible meanings of 'son': first, biological birth, which is impossible for God because He is free from need, desire, and loneliness—the reasons for seeking offspring. Moreover, birth requires a substance like the parent, and God is beyond similarity to creation. Also, God stated that if He desired amusement, He would not take it from what we have. Second, the concept of having a son implies possibility of partnership and transfer of dominion, while God is essentially sovereign and omnipotent, above that. Third, the notion that a part of something is its son is meaningless because the part is incomplete until it exists, and signs (miracles) do not prove sonship since many prophets received miracles without being called sons. Fourth, calling Jesus 'son' is not a term of honor; rather, 'Messiah' and 'messenger' are greater titles. Fifth, the term 'son' is used metaphorically for those who are small and weak, not for glorification. Sixth, if 'son' means refuge, then all creation are like that, just as hell is called 'mother' of its inhabitants. Therefore, the claim of sonship is refuted from all angles.

Key Terms
البنوة
الولاد
الرب
الشهوة
الوحشة
الآيات
الصغار
الضعاف
المفزع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That calling Jesus 'son of God' is an honorific title that elevates him above other prophets.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'son' in language refers to young/weak or to refuge, not to honor, and that 'Messiah' and 'messenger' are greater titles.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is biological birth impossible for God?
A) Because God does not have a body.
B) Because God is free from need, desire, and loneliness.
C) Because Jesus was not born from a human father.
D) Because God cannot create something from nothing.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the concept of sonship is incompatible with God's transcendence and unity, leaving no valid interpretation for calling Jesus 'son of God'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that calling Jesus 'son of God' is meaningless in every sense. Yet many people around you accept this term without question. Where do you stand? Have you ever simply accepted a religious label without thinking through its implications? Are you willing to challenge such labels even if it means standing alone?
?
Maturidi points out that the term 'son' in language refers to the young and weak, not the honored. Think about the titles you cherish—are you seeking honor from worldly labels that actually imply weakness? Or are you content with being a servant of God, as Jesus himself claimed?
?
This lesson shows that even for prophets, miracles do not prove a special status like sonship. What miracles or blessings have you received? Have you ever let them make you feel superior? How can you guard against pride in God's favors and remain purely His servant?

Refuting Dualist Claims on Divine Action
Maturidi reports dualist and monotheist views on divine action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist argument that God's actions must benefit Him and the monotheist response affirming divine wisdom and justice.

Core Idea
God's actions transcend human notions of benefit.

Maturidi here confronts a sophisticated challenge from dualist thinkers who argued that any action without benefit to the agent is futile. They applied this reasoning to God, concluding that His actions must bring Him some advantage. This passage sets up the dualist position and the counter-arguments from the people of Tawhid, including two differing views on whether God's actions can cause real harm without compensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Note the challenging vocabulary and the flow of argument.
The Arabic has OCR issues; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the dualist premise
Find where the dualists claim that an action without benefit to the agent cannot be from wisdom. Understand their reasoning based on observation of the witnessed world.
3
Recognize the monotheist opposition
The text says the monotheists opposed the dualists in 'these two respects'. See how they reject both the benefit requirement and the impossibility of creation from nothing.
4
Examine the two subgroups
After the refutation, some monotheists debated whether harmful actions require compensation. Note the two positions: one denying real harm, the other affirming it and requiring compensation.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider how Maturidi uses these views to affirm that God's actions are not bound by human standards of benefit or harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما ينفع غيره أو يدفع عن غيره الضرر ، وجعلوا ذلك علة فعله ؛ ليخرج عندهم فعله عن معنى العبث. وبهذا الوجه خالفوا الثنوية ؛ إذ أبوا الفعل بغير نفع لالفاعل فيه أن يكون من الحكمة ، فأثبتوا الفعل بجوهر الحكمة عند المزاج ، ليكون في خلاصه من جنس جوهر السفه ، فيصير فعله حكمة على التقدير بالشاهد ما كون شيء لا من شيء ممتنعا فى الشاهد ، فأوجبوا لكلية العالم أصلا جعل وأنشى ؛ لأنه لا فصل بين خروج الفعل عن حق الوجود في الشاهد ، فيلزم دفعه أن يكون ذلك من حكيم " . فخالفهم أهل التوحيد في هذين ، ثم ألزم فريق منهم إياه ما في العقل علة لم يكن له العقل دونها لما وجدوا فعل مثله في الشاهد عبثا ، وألزموا فى فعل المضار لو كان لغير نفع ، يعقب سفها على ما ذكرت الثنوية فى فعل لا ينتفع به الفاعل منه ثم تفرقوا ، فزعم قوم أنه لا ضرر فى الحقيقة على المفعول به ، وإن منه التضرع والشكوى . وزعم قوم أن عليه في الحقيقة ضرراً ، لكن عليه أن يعوضه عن ذلك ؛ ليصير الفعل به حكمة ، كالموجود في الشاهد ممن يحمل المؤن العظام وشرب الأدوية الكريهة مع القصد ، وقصد الخراج لتقع العواقب ، وليس له فعل الضار بغير إلا بعوض .
By what benefits another or repels harm from another, and they made that the cause of His action, so that according to them His action would exit from the meaning of futility. And in this respect they opposed the Dualists; for they rejected that an action without benefit to the agent can be from wisdom, so they affirmed action by the essence of wisdom upon mixture, so that in his salvation from the genus of the essence of folly, his action becomes wisdom according to estimation by the witnessed world, that making something from nothing is impossible in the witnessed world, so they made it necessary for the totality of the world a principle of making and creation; because there is no distinction between the exit of action from the right of existence in the witnessed world, so its repulsion necessitates that it is from a wise one. So the people of Tawhid opposed them in these two respects, then a group among them imposed upon Him that which in the intellect is a cause without which the intellect would not exist, for they found an action like His in the witnessed world to be futile, and they imposed upon the action of harm if it were for no benefit, it would result in folly, according to what the Dualists mentioned concerning an action from which the agent does not benefit. Then they divided: some claimed that there is no real harm upon the one acted upon, and from it are supplication and complaint. And some claimed that upon him there is real harm, but He must compensate him for it, so that the action becomes wisdom thereby, like what is found in the witnessed world of one who bears great burdens and drinks unpleasant medicines with a purpose, and aims for extraction for consequences, and he does not have the act of harming except with compensation.
Source pages p. 815

Commentary
This passage presents the view of dualistic thinkers (likely Manichaeans or similar) who argue that any action must have a benefit for the agent or it would be futile, and they extend this to God. Maturidi explains that they rejected action without benefit for the agent as being from wisdom, and instead posited that action arises from the essence of wisdom only at a 'mixture' (possibly a reference to their cosmological principles). They also considered creation from nothing impossible based on empirical observation. The monotheists (Ahl al-Tawhid) oppose them on two points (left unspecified here). Then some of them further argued that if an action has no benefit for the agent, reason would not accept it, and they compared God's actions to human actions they deem futile. Others argued that harmful actions are only justified if the recipient is compensated. The passage ends with two subgroups: one denying real harm, another affirming it but requiring compensation. Throughout, Maturidi reports these views to set up his own refutation.

Key Terms
ثنوية
عبث
حكمة
شاهد
عوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions must benefit Him to be wise.
Correction: God is self-sufficient and His actions are wise regardless of benefit to Himself.

Checkpoint
What did the Dualists (الثنوية) claim about an action that does not benefit the agent?
A) It is possible if it benefits others.
B) It cannot be from wisdom.
C) It is always evil.
D) It requires compensation.

Key Takeaway
The monotheists affirm that God's actions are wise and just, independent of any benefit to Himself or compensation to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that brings you pain, do you truly believe that God does not need to benefit from it, or do you secretly feel He should 'compensate' you immediately? What does this lesson teach you about trusting His wisdom beyond your own sense of fairness?
?
The dualists insisted that any action without benefit to the agent is futile. Have you ever judged God's commands or decrees by whether they seem 'useful' to you personally? How can you surrender that standard and accept that His actions are wise even when you see no benefit?
?
Some monotheists said there is no real harm, only supplication. Are you willing to reframe your sufferings as opportunities for drawing closer to Him, rather than as injustices that need to be repaid? What would change in your heart if you truly embraced that perspective?

The Two Modes of Divine Testing
Maturidi introduces the two divine tests and how each group mistakenly claims the unambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's concept of divine testing through submission (taslīm) and seeking (ṭalab), and the servant's duty of obedience.

Core Idea
God tests by submission and seeking; the servant's duty is obedience within the command.

Maturidi begins by distinguishing two modes of divine testing: submission and seeking. He emphasizes that the servant's role is obedience according to the command. This foundational idea sets the stage for understanding how people relate to the Qur'an and why disagreement arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure of the two modes.
2
Identify the two types of test
Find the two words for testing: التسليم (submission) and الطلب (seeking). Notice how they are introduced.
3
Observe the servant's duty
The passage states 'وإنما على العبد الطاعة في قدر الأمر'. Reflect on what this implies about human responsibility.
Duty is defined by the command, not by the outcome.
4
Consider the people's response
Notice that people affirm the Qur'an but each group claims to have the unambiguous part, leading to division.
This sets up the problem Maturidi will resolve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ الله تعالى يمتحن بوجهين: بالتسليم مرة وبالطلب ثانيا، وإنما على العبد الطاعة في قدر الأمر. ولما جمع جل ثناؤه كتابه على الأمرين يعرف الناس الدين، أقروا بالكتاب أنه حق من عند الله لا العدول عنه، وأن من لزمه أفلح ونجا، ومن مال عنه شقى وخسر، حتى ظن كل فريق أنه قد أصاب المحكم من ذلك ولزمه، وأن عليه فيما ذهب إليه خصومه أن يقف في ذلك أو يجمله على ما تقرر عنده فيما اعتقده، فألزم تفرقهم الحاجة، كل لا يعرف المحكم من المتشابه لزوم العلم بالمتشابه أن لا يتناقض المحكم. ثم معلوم أنه لا يحتمل القرآن الاختلاف، وبه وصف الله أنه وَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ عِنْدِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلَافًا كَثِيرًا. وفي العقل إن تناقض أدلة له الأدلة، من وهو دليل سفهه وجهله، فثبت بذلك أن الذي له تفرقوا ليس من حيث القرآن، ولا لما ليس فيه بيان، بل دل تكليف الرد إلى القرآن ولزوم اتباعه على أن بيان ذلك.
For God, exalted, tests in two ways: by submission (taslīm) once, and by seeking (ṭalab) a second time. And upon the servant is only obedience to the extent of the command. When He, glorified, compiled His book upon the two ways so that people would know the religion, they affirmed that the Book is truth from God, not to be deviated from, and that whoever adheres to it succeeds and is saved, and whoever turns away from it is wretched and lost, until every group thought that it had attained the unambiguous (al-muḥkam) from that and adhered to it, and that it was incumbent upon it regarding what its opponents held to stop at that or to summarize it according to what was established with it in its belief. So necessity forced their division: each does not know the unambiguous from the ambiguous due to the necessity of knowing the ambiguous such that the unambiguous does not contradict. Then it is known that the Qur'an cannot bear contradiction, and by it God described that 'if it had been from other than God, they would have found in it much contradiction' [4:82]. And in the intellect, if the evidences for the evidences contradict, that is proof of its foolishness and ignorance. So it is established that that for which they divided is not from the Qur'an, nor for what has no clarification in it; rather, the obligation to refer to the Qur'an and the necessity of following it indicate that its clarification is [there].
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage identifies two modes of divine testing: submission (taslīm) and active seeking (ṭalab). The servant's duty is obedience within the command. God composed the Qur'an to teach religion, and all confess it is truth. However, each faction (firqa) mistakenly believes it has grasped the unambiguous parts (muḥkam) and that opponents must stop or conform to its view. This leads to division—yet the Qur'an itself is free of contradiction, as proven by the verse (4:82). Contradiction among proofs indicates foolishness. Therefore, the real cause of division is not the Qur'an itself, nor any lack of clarity, but the obligation to refer to the Qur'an and follow it shows that its clarification is complete.

Key Terms
التسليم (al-taslīm)
الطلب (al-ṭalab)
المحكم (al-muḥkam)
المتشابه (al-mutashābih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two modes of divine testing according to Maturidi?
A) Submission and seeking
B) Obedience and disobedience
C) Certainty and doubt
D) Knowledge and ignorance

Key Takeaway
God tests people through both submission and active seeking; the servant's duty is obedience within the command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that seems to contradict your current understanding, do you immediately try to reinterpret it (ṭalab) or do you submit (taslīm) and trust that your understanding is flawed? Which mode did you resort to last time, and was that the right response?
?
Can you identify a belief you hold so strongly that you have assumed it is the only correct interpretation (muḥkam)? How might that assumption be causing you to dismiss others without fair consideration of the ambiguous? Will you commit to re-examining that belief in light of this lesson?
?
The source says the servant's duty is obedience 'within the command.' Are you more focused on obeying God's commands as they are, or on seeking to understand them on your own terms? What is one command you have been rationalizing instead of simply submitting to?

The Source of Disagreement
Maturidi argues that the Qur'an is free of contradiction and that disagreement arises from human error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Qur'an is free of contradiction and that disagreement among Muslims stems from human misunderstanding, not from the Qur'an itself.

Core Idea
Disagreement does not come from the Qur'an but from human error.

After establishing the two modes of testing, Maturidi addresses the problem of disagreement. He argues that the Qur'an itself is free of contradiction, and any apparent conflict among proofs indicates foolishness. The real source of division is not the Qur'an's clarity but human misinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the verse cited
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the verse (4:82) that Maturidi uses as evidence that the Qur'an has no contradiction.
The verse is: 'If it had been from other than God, they would have found in it much contradiction.'
2
Understand the logical argument
Maturidi adds an intellectual argument: if proofs contradict, that proves foolishness. Notice how he connects this to the division.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the division is not from the Qur'an nor from any lack of explanation. The obligation to refer to the Qur'an shows it is clear.
This corrects the misconception that the Qur'an is ambiguous.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this argument addresses sectarianism: the fault lies with human understanding, not the divine text.

Source Text

For God, exalted, tests in two ways: by submission (taslīm) once, and by seeking (ṭalab) a second time. And upon the servant is only obedience to the extent of the command. And in the intellect, if the evidences for the evidences contradict, that is proof of its foolishness and ignorance. So it is established that that for which they divided is not from the Qur'an, nor for what has no clarification in it; rather, the obligation to refer to the Qur'an and the necessity of following it indicate that its clarification is [there].

?

?
If the Qur'an is truly free of contradiction and fully clear, why do you still harbour doubts about its teachings on certain issues? Is it possible that your confusion is not the Qur'an's fault but your own unwillingness to submit? What one area of your life would change if you genuinely believed that the Qur'an's answer is already complete and sufficient?
?
This lesson places the responsibility for disagreement squarely on human error—not on the Qur'an. Can you point to one specific instance in your life where you blamed 'contradictions in the Qur'an' for your confusion, when in reality you had not yet humbled yourself before its clear command? What would repentance look like right now?

Refutation of the Jabriyya: The Self-Defeating Nature of Compulsion
Maturidi refutes the Jabriyya by showing their position is self-undermining.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Jabriyya position is self-refuting because it denies the reality of speech and action, thereby undermining the very basis of argumentation.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya claim is self-defeating because it negates the reality of all speech and action.

Maturidi presents a powerful critique of the Jabriyya (extreme predestinarians). Rather than engaging in lengthy refutation, he shows that their position is inherently self-defeating. By denying human agency, the Jabriyya also deny the reality of the very speech and action by which they argue, making their own claim impossible. In this passage, Maturidi exposes the logical contradiction at the heart of jabr.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the conditional phrase 'فلو جاز' as it sets up the analogy.
2
Identify the key components of the argument
Look for the analogy with sensory knowledge, the characterization of the Jabriyya view, and the conclusion that it destroys the basis of disputation.
3
Reflect on the self-refutation
Consider why denying the reality of speech and action makes the Jabriyya argument impossible. How does this compare to other refutations you have seen?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو جاز صرف مثله مما طريق العلم به الحس ، وإبطاله نحو العلم لجميع العالم مثله ، وذلك مهجور ، فمثله قول أهل الجبر ، وهذا قول يغنى الحكاية عن الإطناب فيه ؛ لما ليس له كثير اتباع ، ولما ليس لهذا القول معنى تكلم عليه صاحبه ؛ إذ هو ينفى عن نفسه حقيقة كل قول وفعل ، وإذا انتفى بطل القول ، وبه يناظر ويحاج فزال الذى به يكون الحجاج واضمحل .
For if it were permissible to turn aside something like that which is known by sensory means, and its invalidation would be like knowledge for all the world the same, that would be abandoned. So its like is the statement of the people of jabr (compulsion). This statement dispenses with elaboration due to it having few followers, and because this statement has no meaning that its proponent discusses; for he negates from himself the reality of every speech and action. When reality is negated, speech becomes invalid, and by it one argues and disputes, so that by which disputation occurs ceases and vanishes.
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabriyya (extreme predestinarian) position. Maturidi begins by stating that if we allow turning aside from sensory knowledge, then all knowledge would be similarly invalidated, which is an abandoned (unacceptable) position. The view of the Jabriyya is analogous to this. He then argues that this view does not merit extensive refutation because it has few followers and inherently denies the reality of all speech and action. Since the proponent of jabr denies the very reality that makes argument and discussion possible, his own speech becomes self-defeating; the basis for disputation (haajaj) disappears. The argument is that the Jabriyya position is self-refuting: in claiming that all actions are from God alone, the speaker negates his own capacity to speak and argue meaningfully.

Key Terms
الجبر (jabr)
حقيقة (reality)
قول (speech/statement)
فعل (action)
حجاج (disputation/argument)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi dismisses the Jabriyya merely because they are a small group.
Correction: Actually, his main point is logical: the Jabriyya position denies the reality of speech and action, making its own claim impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the Jabriyya position ultimately invalid?
A) It has too few followers to be taken seriously.
B) It denies the reality of all speech and action, making its own argument impossible.
C) It contradicts the Quran.
D) It relies on sensory knowledge which is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya claim is self-refuting because it denies the reality of speech and action, undermining its own argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you deny the reality of your own choices—perhaps by claiming 'I can't change'—while still using language that assumes you can? How does this lesson expose the inconsistency in your own heart?
?
Maturidi says that the Jabriyya's speech becomes invalid because it negates reality. In your daily interactions, do your actions ever contradict your words, making your speech lose its weight? What reality are you denying?
?
When you argue with others—about faith or life—do you hold positions that would, if true, make your own argument impossible? How can you align your beliefs with the reality of your own responsibility before Allah?

The Consensus of Muslims and Qur'an Refute Partners in Creation
Maturidi invokes consensus and the verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' against dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses both the consensus of Muslims and a key Qur'anic verse to refute the idea that any creature shares in God's creative power.

Core Idea
Consensus and the Qur'an together silence any claim of partners with God.

Maturidi begins by noting the unanimous agreement of the Muslim community: the Maker of the world is one. He then brings the decisive Qur'anic argument that nothing is like God, and further shows that even if an opponent tries to argue that acts are not like God's, the same refutation applies. This lesson unpacks that powerful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi starts with consensus and moves to the Qur'anic proof.
2
Identify the two Qur'anic proofs
Find the two verses Maturidi cites: 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' and 'Ilah kulli shay'. Understand how each refutes the opponent's position.
3
Understand the refutation of the trick
The opponent might try to say the servant's act is different from God's act. Maturidi shows this trick still implies likeness. See why the first refutation is stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يختلف أهل الإسلام في إطلاق القول بأن صانع العالم واحد ، وقول من يبطل قوله عند التحصيل هذه الجملة التى شارك فيها الجميع مردود بالجملة على نحو قول الله تعالى : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ، وقوله : إله كل شيء . إن قول الناس في التحصيل يجعل له شبها وعدلا فى العباد منقوض بتلك الجملة ، وإن احتال فمثله الأول ، بل الأول أحق ؛ لأنه طريق العلم بالحرف الثانى وهو أن في تحقيق العالم تحقيق الوحدانية للخالق ، وبه يسلم له القول لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ، وأنه الواحد لا شريك له ، فإذ أثبت العالم بشركاء له فيه لم يكن هو أحق بأن أن يكون لمثله أشياء ، أو أن يكون إلها لما أنشأه وأخرجه من ليس كمثله شيء من العدم إلى الوجود من غيره فى ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the people of Islam have not differed in the unqualified statement that the Maker of the world is one. And the saying of the one who nullifies his own saying upon investigation—this phrase in which all have shared—is refuted in its entirety in the manner of Allah's saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11), and His saying: 'Lord of everything.' Indeed, the saying of people upon investigation ascribes to Him likeness and equality in servants, and it is refuted by that phrase. And if he uses a trick, it is like the first, rather the first is more worthy, because it is the path of knowledge to the second point: that in the realization of the world is the realization of the oneness of the Creator, and by it the statement 'There is nothing like unto Him' is secured for Him, and that He is the One, no partner for Him. So if he establishes the world with partners for Him in it, then He is not more worthy that things be for His like, nor that He be a god for what He originated and brought out—there is nothing like Him from non-existence to existence—with another in that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 832

Commentary
Maturidi appeals to the consensus (ijma') of Muslims that the Maker of the world is one. He then argues that any opponent—referring to those who attribute acts to creatures—effectively contradicts themselves because their own words imply a likeness or partner for God. The Qur'anic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' (There is nothing like unto Him) negates any likeness. If someone tries to circumvent this by arguing that the servant's act is not like God's act, the same refutation applies: any attribution of an independent act to a creature implies a similarity (shabah) or equality ('idl) with God in power. Moreover, the very existence and order of the world leads to the realization of tawhid. If the world had partners in its coming-to-be, then God would not be the exclusive object of worship and lordship. Thus, the opponents' position is self-refuting.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma')
ليس كمثله شيء (laysa kamithlihi shay')
وحدانية (wahdaniyya)
إله كل شيء (ilahu kulli shay')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that consensus alone is sufficient to prove that God creates all acts, without needing Qur'anic evidence.
Correction: Maturidi uses consensus as a starting point but immediately reinforces it with the Qur'anic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'', showing that the primary authority is the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse does Maturidi use to refute any partner in creation?
A) إله كل شيء
B) ليس كمثله شيء
C) A and B
D) هو الأول والآخر

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that both the consensus of Muslims and Qur'anic verses establish that God is one and incomparable, refuting any notion of partners in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something through your own effort, do you feel a sense of pride that subtly attributes the outcome to yourself? How does Maturidi's refutation of partners expose this inward shirk? Take a moment to disown any independent power and return to 'There is no power except with Allah.'
?
The opponent used a 'trick' to justify partners. What 'tricks' do you use in your daily life to excuse relying on people or systems while forgetting that Allah alone is the true agent? Identify one situation and renew your tawhid.
?
Read the verse again: 'There is nothing like unto Him.' Does your heart accept this completely, or do you sometimes imagine God resembling creation? Confess any such thought and affirm His incomparability.

Maturidi's Reductio: Comparing Multi-Agent Creation to Dualism
Maturidi compares opponent to dualists in reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the comparison with dualists and Magians to argue against ascribing creation to many agents.

Core Idea
Even heretics limit creation to two; opponents who ascribe it to countless are worse.

Maturidi employs a striking rhetorical strategy: he compares the position of those who ascribe creation to many agents (such as the Mu'tazila) to the dualists and Magians. His point is that even the dualists, who are far from tawhid, agree that the divine is one and wise, and they restrict creation to two principles. The opponents' position—attributing creation to every human agent—is actually more extreme and absurd. This fragment illustrates Maturidi's method of refutation by comparison to more obviously false views.

Guided Path
1
Read the fragment and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The text breaks off mid-sentence, so the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the two groups
Maturidi mentions the dualists and Magians on one side, and 'those who assigned creation to one who cannot be counted' on the other. Identify who each refers to.
‘Dualists’ likely refer to Zoroastrians or Manichaeans; ‘Magians’ are Zoroastrian priests.
3
Note what the dualists affirm
Despite their dualism, they agree with Ahl al-Tawhid that the divine is wise, true, one, all-knowing, powerful. This shows they respect tawhid in some form.
4
Understand the comparison
Maturidi asks: 'Who is more excessive than them until they assigned creation to one who cannot be counted?' The implication is that the opponents are worse than the dualists.
The fragment ends with 'and they nullified...' suggesting the opponents have nullified tawhid entirely.
5
Reflect on the argumentative purpose
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if even heretics (dualists) limit the number of creators, those who attribute creation to countless agents are even more irrational and farther from truth.
Maturidi uses this to defend the Ash'ari position that only Allah creates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن مذهب الثنوية والمجوس فى صرف خلق العالم إلى اثنين، وأن يوافقوا أهل التوحيد على أن الإله الحكيم الحق / الذى لم يجز ولا يجوز واحد عليم قدير، فمن أربى عليهم حتى جعل خلق العالم لمن لا يحصى عددهم، وأبطلوا
Then the foundation is that the doctrine of the dualists and the Magians in ascribing the creation of the world to two [principles], and they agree with the people of tawhid that the divine is the wise, the true, who is not and cannot be other than one, all-knowing, powerful. So who is more excessive than them until they assigned the creation of the world to one who cannot be counted? And they nullified...
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage (which is a fragment due to the page break) draws a comparison: the dualists and Magians at least ascribe creation to only two principles, and they agree with the people of tawhid that the divine is one, wise, true, etc. However, the opponents (likely the Mu'tazila or others who deny that God creates all actions) go even further by assigning creation to countless agents (i.e., every human). This is even more excessive and absurd. The text breaks off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
الثنوية
المجوس
التوحيد
الإله الحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is defending dualism or equating the opponents with dualists.
Correction: Maturidi uses the dualists as a foil: even they, who are heretical, limit creation to two. The opponents are even more wrong because they attribute creation to countless agents, which is a more extreme departure from tawhid.

Checkpoint
What point does Maturidi make by comparing the opponents to dualists?
A) The opponents are also dualists
B) The opponents are worse than dualists because they ascribe creation to many agents
C) Dualists are correct in limiting creation to two
D) The opponents agree with dualists on all points

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the comparison to dualists as a reductio: if even they limit creation, attributing creation to countless agents is even more irrational and contrary to tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, what 'countless creators' do you unconsciously turn to—luck, people, your own efforts—instead of recognizing Allah as the sole Creator? What would it take to uproot that from your heart today?
?
When you see others attributing success or misfortune to multiple sources (e.g., fate, karma, astrology), does it stir in you the same urgency Maturidi felt to defend tawhid? If not, why are you so comfortable with their error?
?
Maturidi used a reductio to show the absurdity of attributing creation to many. What subtle forms of shirk in your daily routines (like worrying about tomorrow as if your provision depends on anything other than Allah) need to be exposed and repented from right now?
