Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazila by Comparison with Dualists
Maturidi compares Mu'tazila unfavorably to dualists and Magians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses a comparative refutation to argue that the Mu'tazila position on human acts is more problematic than dualism.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila multiplied creators worse than dualists.

Imam Maturidi employs a striking rhetorical strategy in this passage. Rather than simply rejecting the Mu'tazila doctrine on human acts, he compares it to dualist and Magian views, arguing that the Mu'tazila are even more extreme. This lesson examines his argument and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the flow and the rhetorical question.
Pay attention to the phrase 'who has surpassed them?'
2
Identify the comparison
What do dualists and Magians believe? How does Maturidi use them as a baseline?
They attribute creation to two principles.
3
Understand the criticism
Why does Maturidi say the Mu'tazila are 'more deserving of blood'?
They attribute creation of human acts to countless agents.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical effect
How does this comparison strengthen his refutation?
It shows the absurdity of the Mu'tazila position by contrasting with a known deviant view.
5
Connect to broader theology
How does this argument relate to the debate on divine power and human acts?
It emphasizes God's exclusive power over all creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن مذهب الثنوية والمجوس فى صرف خلق العالم إلى اثنين ، وأن يوافقوا أهل التوحيد على أن الإله الحكيم الحق / الذى لم يجز ولا يجوز واحد عليم قدير ، فمن أربى عليهم حتى جعل خلق العالم لمن لا يحصى عددهم ، وأبطلوا أن يكون للإله الذي قال الخلق بألوهيته قدرة خلق أكثر العالم ، [فهم] أحق بالدم ممن نزهوه عن الشرور والقبائح ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the principle is that the dualists and Magians attribute the creation of the world to two [principles], and they agree with the people of tawhid that the wise, true God—who is not and cannot be other than one, all-knowing, all-powerful—so who has surpassed them until he made the creation of the world to those whose number cannot be counted, and they nullified that the God who creation said by His divinity has power to create most of the world. They are more deserving of blood than those who declared Him free from evils and abominations. And there is no power except from God.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical refutation by comparison. The author states that the dualists (thnawiyya) and Magians (majus) attribute creation of the world to two principles (good and evil). They at least agree with monotheists that the true God is one, wise, all-knowing, all-powerful. But the Mu'tazila, by attributing creation of human acts to countless human servants, have outdone the dualists. They have effectively denied that the true God has power to create most of the world (i.e., human acts). Therefore, the Mu'tazila are more deserving of severe condemnation (literally 'blood') than those dualists who at least declared God free from evils. The point is to show the absurdity of the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
ثنوية
مجوس
توحيد
خلق
إله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Maturidi is endorsing dualism or considering it acceptable.
Correction: Maturidi uses dualism as a rhetorical baseline to show that the Mu'tazila position is even worse; he is not endorsing dualism.

Checkpoint

B) They attribute creation to countless beings instead of two.
C) They reject divine attributes.
D) They believe in only one God.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila are more blameworthy than dualists because they attribute the creation of human acts to countless agents, thereby undermining God's power.

Maturidi's Polemical Introduction to Refuting the Mu'tazila
Maturidi sets up the refutation of the Mu'tazila's arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rhetorical strategy Maturidi uses to set up his refutation of the Mu'tazila and the key claims he makes about their methodology.

Core Idea
Maturidi exposes the Mu'tazila's overconfidence to undermine their authority before refuting them.

This passage marks a shift from discussion of Sunni doctrine to direct engagement with the Mu'tazila. Maturidi does not immediately argue but first frames the opponent as arrogant and misguided, preparing the reader for a detailed critique. The lesson will examine how he establishes this contrast and the promises he makes about exposing their errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the tone—it is direct and critical.
The author uses strong language like 'audacity' and 'concealed'.
2
Identify Maturidi's critique
Look for how Maturidi characterizes the Mu'tazila's self-perception versus their actual standing. He calls them 'knights of kalam' but says they are far from true knowledge.
This is a rhetorical move to discredit them before engaging their arguments.
3
Understand his promises
Note that Maturidi promises to show their deviation from sound reasoning and their concealment of Quranic verses. This sets the agenda for the subsequent refutations.
He implies that if they had properly studied the verses, they would have succeeded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما تعلق به هذه الفرقة التى ظنت أنهم فرسان الكلام ، وأنهم المخصوصون في العلم به من بين الأنام ؛ ليعلموا بذلك جرأتهم في الدعوى وبعدهم عند التحصيل عن احتمال اسم عوام أهله ، فضلا عن مجاوزة أخطاء حذاقهم، ونظهر إن شاء الله تعالى لمن تأمل ما ذكرت عدولهم عما توجبه حقيقة النظر ، ونبين ما استتروا به من الآيات ليعلم أنهم لو دققوا على طرف منها لنالوا خير الدارين، أن يظفروا بحقيقتها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then we mention what this sect held on to, those who thought that they are the knights of kalam, and that they are the elite in knowledge of it among people; so that they may know thereby their audacity in claiming and their distance, upon examination, from bearing the name of the common folk of its people, let alone surpassing the errors of their experts. And we will show, if God wills, to whoever ponders what I have mentioned, their deviation from what sound consideration requires, and we will explain what they have concealed from the verses, so that they may know that if they had scrutinized a small part of them, they would have attained the good of both abodes—if only they could attain their reality. And there is no power except from God.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage is a transitional introduction. The author declares his intention to present and refute the arguments of the Mu'tazila (these 'knights of kalam'). He criticizes their arrogance and distance from true knowledge, and claims they are even worse than common laypeople. He promises to expose their deviations from sound reasoning and their misinterpretations of Quranic verses. If they had properly examined the verses, they would have achieved success in this world and the next. This sets a polemical tone for the subsequent refutations.

Key Terms
فرقة
كلام
دعوى
نظر
آيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is simply insulting the Mu'tazila without substance.
Correction: The author is not merely insulting but establishing that the Mu'tazila have strayed from proper methodology, paving the way for a reasoned refutation.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except from God.' Do you truly believe that you cannot attain truth without God's help? Or have you relied on your own intellect? How will you actively depend on God in your next study session?

The Mu'tazilite View on Movement and Thingness
Maturidi presents the Mu'tazilite view on the paralyzed person's movement and the concept of thingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim about the movement of the paralyzed person and how they use the concept of 'thingness' to justify their view.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites say the paralyzed person's movement is a thing in itself because they apply thingness to the non-existent.

This lesson introduces the Mu'tazilite perspective on human actions, specifically their controversial claim about the paralyzed person. They argue that God creates the movement, but the servant performs the movement as an action, and that movement has its own 'thingness' even before existence. Understanding this concept is crucial for grasping the heart of the debate between Maturidi and the Mu'tazilites.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the passage and identify the two aspects: creation from God and movement from the servant.
The Mu'tazilites are trying to preserve human responsibility while maintaining God's sole creative power.
2
Understand thingness (shay'iyya)
The passage states that the movement is a thing in itself because thingness applies to the non-existent. Consider why the Mu'tazilites hold this view.
Thingness is a metaphysical property that makes something an object of knowledge even before it exists.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this view connects to unbelief and punishment. This sets the stage for Maturidi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الآخر قول من يقول، إن خلق الشيء هو ذلك، فقد بينا اختلاف الجهات في ذلك، فجائز القول بالخلق من جهة هي غير جهة القول بالكفر، على ما بينا من الشيئية. وقد زعم المعتزلة فى حركة المفلوج أنها لله خلقا وللعبد حركة، وهى شيء لنفسها؛ إذ الشيئية عندهم في المعدوم، وهى دلالة حدث الجسم، وفى الكفر حجة الله على العبد فى التعذيب ودلالة سفهه في التحقيق. على أنا بينا أنه يحيل من حيث لا يكون مثل ذلك في الخلق، وقد أوضحنا الفصل بين الأمرين، وأن من قاس أحد الوجهين بالآخر فهو مغفل. على أن المعتزلة إذ لا الله إلى الخلق سوى أنه أوجد [هم] بعد أن لم يكونوا، ولا ذلك معنى يجعلون من فعل العباد، إنما هو معالجات وعناء وجهد، والموجود فيهما نحن فيه مع المعنى الذى من العباد واقعان جميعا، فلا وجه لإنكاره. ثم يقال فيما لا يكون مثله من العباد إحالته: أرأيت لو عارضك إخوانك فقالوا: تجعل للذي ذكرته أصلا ثم كون الجواهر بالخلق محال، ثبت قدمها به، وكون فعل لا ينفع فاعله ولا يدفع عنه الضرر ليس بحكمة، فدل أن الذى صنع العالم انتفع به، وقال: ما يوجب كون شيء من شيء خارج عن احتمال الخلق، فمثله أمر الواحد الذى به كانا العالم. وإذا كان دعوى الإحالة توجب قول الزنادقة والدهرية في قدم العالم أظهر ذلك صدق من قال: الاعتزال طرف من الزندقة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The other statement is the statement of one who says: 'The creation of a thing is that thing itself.' We have already explained the difference of aspects in that. So it is permissible to speak of creation from one aspect which is other than the aspect of speaking of unbelief, according to what we have explained about thingness (al-shay'iyya). The Mu'tazilites have claimed regarding the movement of the paralyzed person that it is from God as creation and from the servant as movement, and it is a thing in itself, because thingness for them is in the non-existent, and it is an indication of the temporality of the body. And in unbelief it is God's argument against the servant for punishment and an indication of his foolishness in reality. We have shown that it is impossible from the aspect that such a thing does not occur in creation. We have clarified the distinction between the two matters, and that whoever compares one of the two aspects with the other is a fool. Moreover, the Mu'tazilites do not attribute to God anything other than bringing into existence after non-existence, and they do not make that meaning apply to the actions of servants; rather, they (servants' actions) are simply efforts, toil, and striving. And what exists in both [the creation and the action] is the same meaning that occurs from the servant [in acquisition?], both are real, so there is no justification for denying it. Then it is said regarding what has no likeness from the servants: its impossibility. Do you not consider that if your brothers were to oppose you and say: 'You make what you mentioned as an origin, then the origination of atoms by creation is impossible, so their eternity is established by it, and an action that neither benefits its doer nor repels harm is not wise, so it indicates that the one who made the world benefited from it'? And he said: 'What necessitates the existence of something from something is outside the possibility of creation, so similarly the affair of the One by whom the world came to be.' And if the claim of impossibility necessitates the statement of the heretics and materialists about the eternity of the world, then that manifests the truth of the saying: 'Mu'tazilism is a branch of heresy.' There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position on human actions, especially their view on the movement of a paralyzed person. The author starts by referencing those who claim that creation of a thing is that thing itself, and he says it is permissible to speak of creation from an aspect other than unbelief, based on the concept of thingness (shay'iyya). The Mu'tazilites claim that the movement of a paralyzed person is from God as creation and from the servant as movement, and it is a thing in itself because thingness applies to the non-existent. Maturidi argues that this is impossible because such a case is not found in creation. He then points out that the Mu'tazilites only consider creation as bringing into existence after non-existence, and they do not apply that meaning to human actions, which they see as mere efforts. But the action and the creation both exist simultaneously, so there is no basis for denial. He then presents a counter-argument: if someone claims that something cannot be created because it leads to the impossibility of creation, then that would imply the eternity of atoms, which is the view of heretics and materialists (Dahriyya). Thus, the Mu'tazilite position leads to heresy, confirming the saying that Mu'tazilism is a branch of heresy.

Key Terms
خلق
حركة المفلوج
الشيئية
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite view is that the servant creates his own movement.
Correction: Actually, the Mu'tazilites hold that God creates the movement, but the servant performs the movement as an act. The movement itself is a thing distinct from both.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Mu'tazilites claim about the movement of the paralyzed person?
A) God creates it and the servant owns it as movement.
B) The servant creates it.
C) It is not a thing.
D) It is a thing only after existence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites use the concept of thingness to argue that the paralyzed person's movement is a real entity created by God but performed by the servant, which Maturidi finds problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions—good or bad—do you truly believe they are simultaneously created by Allah and performed by you? How does neglecting either aspect distort your relationship with your Lord?
?
The Mu'tazilites applied thingness to non-existent actions to justify punishment. Have you ever justified a harsh judgment of others based on a flawed understanding of their actions' reality? What would it mean to align your judgments with the truth of Allah's creation?
?
If the paralyzed person's movement is both from Allah and from the servant, what does that say about your own dependence on Allah for every motion? How would a constant awareness of that change your attitude in prayer and daily life?

Maturidi's Refutation: Impossibility and Heresy
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim by showing its impossibility and its heretical implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments against the Mu'tazilite view and why he concludes that Mu'tazilism leads to heresy.

Core Idea
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite distinction is impossible because it has no parallel in creation and leads to the heretical view of the eternity of the world.

In this lesson, Maturidi delivers his refutation of the Mu'tazilite position. He first argues that such a separation between creation and action is impossible, as it has no example in God's creation. Then he critiques the Mu'tazilite reduction of creation to mere bringing into existence, and finally he shows that their logic would imply the eternity of atoms, a view held by heretics and materialists.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's impossibility argument
He states that the Mu'tazilite claim is impossible because no similar case exists in creation. Understand why this is a strong rebuttal.
Maturidi relies on the principle that God's creation is consistent and does not contain such dual attributions.
2
Critique of Mu'tazilite definition of creation
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazilites limit creation to bringing into existence, while human actions are just efforts. Note how he contrasts the two.
3
Analyze the counter-argument about eternity
Maturidi presents a hypothetical: if claiming impossibility leads to denying creation, it would imply atoms are eternal. This is the view of heretics (Zanadiqa) and materialists (Dahriyya).
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the Mu'tazilite position logically leads to a heretical conclusion.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Maturidi concludes that Mu'tazilism is a branch of heresy (zindiq). Reflect on the weight of this accusation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
If a sincere brother or sister in faith showed you that your reasoning about a divine attribute aligns with the materialists, how would you respond? Would you defend your logic or fear for your faith? Which matters more to you: being logically consistent or being safe from heresy?

The Flawed Analogy: Act vs. Speech in Witnessed and Unseen
Al-Maturidi presents the opponent's analogy and initial rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Maturidi argues that the analogy between a single act by two agents and a single statement by multiple speakers fails because it ignores the distinction between act and speech in the witnessed realm.

Core Idea
What works in the witnessed may not apply to the unseen.

Al-Maturidi engages a common opponent's argument that likens a single act performed by two agents to a single statement uttered by many speakers. Such analogies were used to support certain theological positions. In this passage, he begins his refutation by examining the analogy in the light of the witnessed world and then showing its inconsistency when applied to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the structure of objection and response.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
The opponent likens a single act by two doers to a single statement by many speakers. Underline where this is mentioned.
The analogy is presented in the opening line.
3
Follow Al-Maturidi's response
Al-Maturidi first concedes that in the witnessed world a statement can be attributed to a group. But then he turns the argument: if the witnessed is evidence for the unseen, then the same distinction between act and speech must be maintained, which shows the opponent's inconsistency.
Notice the conditional 'if' (لو) that introduces the counter.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The passage concludes that the opponent's analogy is deluded. Ask yourself: why would the opponent think the analogy holds?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض فعلا واحدا لفاعلين بقول واحد وخبر واحد. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يجوزان في الشاهد ، قد يقال : هذا قول جماعة ، وخبر المتواتر وهو قول فلان وفلان وخبر فلان وفلان ، فلئن كان ذا أصله فيجب به جواز الآخر ؛ إذ به يلزم الآخر ، ولو كان ما يجوز في الشاهد هو دليل الغائب ليجب التفريق بين الفعل والقول في الغائب كما وجب في الشاهد ، وهذا يبين وهمه . ثم جائز القول بأن الله خالق كل شيء ، وهو خالق وما سواه مخلوق . ولا يجوز أن يقال : هو قائل كل قول ، ولا مخبر كل خبر ، ولا هو مخبر وقائل : وما سواه خبر وقول ، فدل أن أحدهما ليس بنظير الآخر ، مع ما يجوز عندهم فعل كل واحد بقدرة هي فعل لله تعالى ، ثم لم يجز في قول كل أحد وخبره أنه قول الله تعالى وخبر . ويقال له : إذا لم يسم هو متحركا بما حرك غيره ، فقل أيضا إنه لا يسمى خالقا بما خلق حركة غيره ، أو إذ فصل بينهما بالعموم والخصوص أو بما شئت فافصل بينهما . على أن المعنى الذي به سمي في فعل كل شيء ، والمعنى الذي به سمي خالقا يوجد قائلا يوجد ؛ لذلك اختلفا ، والله أعلم
Then he countered a single act by two doers with a single statement (qawl) and a single report (khabar). The Sheikh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Both are permissible in the witnessed world. It can be said: This is the statement of a group, and the mutawatir report is the statement of so-and-so and so-and-so and the report of so-and-so and so-and-so. So if this is its basis, then the permissibility of the other must follow; because the other is implied by it. But if what is permissible in the witnessed world is evidence for the unseen, then it would be necessary to differentiate between act and speech in the unseen as it is necessary in the witnessed, and this shows his delusion. Then it is permissible to say that Allah is the creator of everything, and He is the creator and what is other than Him is created. But it is not permissible to say that He is the speaker of every statement, nor the reporter of every report, nor is He the reporter and speaker, while what is other than Him is report and statement. This indicates that one is not the counterpart of the other, along with what they consider permissible: the act of each one is by a power that is an act of Allah, yet it is not permissible regarding the statement and report of everyone that it is the statement and report of Allah. And it is said to him: If He is not called 'mover' by what He moves other than Him, then also say that He is not called 'creator' by what He creates of the movement of other than Him; or if you differentiate between them by generality and specificity or whatever you wish, differentiate between them. Because the meaning by which He is named in the act of everything, and the meaning by which He is named 'creator' and 'speaker' differ; therefore they differ, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 840

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the analogy between a single act attributed to two agents and a single statement attributed to multiple speakers. Al-Maturidi first presents the objection: the opponent likens a single act by two agents to a single statement (qawl) or report (khabar) made by many. The Sheikh responds that in the witnessed world, a statement can indeed be attributed to a group or to multiple individuals (as in mutawatir reports), but this does not imply that the same applies to acts. If the witnessed world were a guide to the unseen, then one would have to distinguish between act and speech in the unseen just as in the witnessed. The opponent's analogy fails because Allah is called the creator of everything, but He is not called the speaker of every statement or the reporter of every report. Moreover, the Mu'tazila themselves hold that every human act is done by a power that is an act of Allah, yet they do not say that every human statement is Allah's statement. Al-Maturidi then offers a further refutation: if God is not called 'mover' by the movement He creates in others, then He should also not be called 'creator' by the movement He creates in others—unless a distinction is made. Ultimately, the meanings (ma'ani) by which God is named 'creator' and 'speaker' are different, so the analogy does not hold.

Key Terms
فعل
قول
خبر
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent thinks that because a statement can be attributed to many speakers, an act can be attributed to two agents.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that in the witnessed world, acts and speech are attributed differently, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Maturidi first concede about the witnesse world regarding the opponent's analogy?
A) A single act can be performed by two agents.
B) A single statement can be attributed to a group.
C) The witnesse world is irrelevant to the unseen.
D) The opponent's analogy is correct.

Key Takeaway
The analogy between a single act by two agents and a single statement by many speakers fails because the witnesse world shows that acts and speech are attributed differently, and this distinction must be maintained in the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about God—whether in your personal reflections or in discussions with others—do you unconsciously borrow assumptions from your experience of created things? How has this lesson shown you the danger of doing so, and what will you do to guard against it?
?
Think of a time you used a comparison between something in your life and something about God to make a point. In light of this passage, how might that analogy have been flawed? Will you commit to re-examining that analogy with the distinction between act and speech in mind?
?
This passage forces a distinction between act and speech in the unseen, even though in our experience they seem similar. What does this tell you about the limits of human reason in understanding divine attributes? Are you willing to accept that God's speech is not like human speech in its attribution, even if you cannot fully grasp it? How will this affect your relationship with the Qur'an as God's speech?

Divine Names: Creator vs. Speaker
Al-Maturidi uses divine names and internal inconsistency to refute the analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Maturidi argues that Allah is called the creator of everything but not the speaker of every statement, and how this refutes the analogy.

Core Idea
Creator and speaker are not analogous divine attributes.

Having shown the flaw in the opponent's analogy from the witnesse world, Al-Maturidi now provides a theological argument based on how we describe Allah. He points out that while we say Allah is the creator of everything, we do not say He is the speaker of every statement or the reporter of every report. This asymmetry reveals that the relationship between act and speech is not parallel in the divine context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the second part starting from 'ثم جائز القول بأن الله خالق كل شيء...' to the end.
2
Identify the key claim
Allah is creator of everything, but not speaker of every statement. Note this contrast.
3
Examine the internal inconsistency
The opponent's own position holds that every human act is by a power that is an act of Allah, yet they do not say every human statement is Allah's statement. This is an inconsistency.
4
Follow the final analogy
Al-Maturidi argues: if God is not called 'mover' by what He moves, then He should not be called 'creator' by what He creates of movement. This further distinguishes creator from speaker.
5
Understand the root cause
The meanings (ma'ani) by which God is named creator and speaker differ, so they are not analogous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض فعلا واحدا لفاعلين بقول واحد وخبر واحد. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يجوزان في الشاهد ، قد يقال : هذا قول جماعة ، وخبر المتواتر وهو قول فلان وفلان وخبر فلان وفلان ، فلئن كان ذا أصله فيجب به جواز الآخر ؛ إذ به يلزم الآخر ، ولو كان ما يجوز في الشاهد هو دليل الغائب ليجب التفريق بين الفعل والقول في الغائب كما وجب في الشاهد ، وهذا يبين وهمه . ثم جائز القول بأن الله خالق كل شيء ، وهو خالق وما سواه مخلوق . ولا يجوز أن يقال : هو قائل كل قول ، ولا مخبر كل خبر ، ولا هو مخبر وقائل : وما سواه خبر وقول ، فدل أن أحدهما ليس بنظير الآخر ، مع ما يجوز عندهم فعل كل واحد بقدرة هي فعل لله تعالى ، ثم لم يجز في قول كل أحد وخبره أنه قول الله تعالى وخبر . ويقال له : إذا لم يسم هو متحركا بما حرك غيره ، فقل أيضا إنه لا يسمى خالقا بما خلق حركة غيره ، أو إذ فصل بينهما بالعموم والخصوص أو بما شئت فافصل بينهما . على أن المعنى الذي به سمي في فعل كل شيء ، والمعنى الذي به سمي خالقا يوجد قائلا يوجد ؛ لذلك اختلفا ، والله أعلم
Then he countered a single act by two doers with a single statement (qawl) and a single report (khabar). The Sheikh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Both are permissible in the witnessed world. It can be said: This is the statement of a group, and the mutawatir report is the statement of so-and-so and so-and-so and the report of so-and-so and so-and-so. So if this is its basis, then the permissibility of the other must follow; because the other is implied by it. But if what is permissible in the witnessed world is evidence for the unseen, then it would be necessary to differentiate between act and speech in the unseen as it is necessary in the witnessed, and this shows his delusion. Then it is permissible to say that Allah is the creator of everything, and He is the creator and what is other than Him is created. But it is not permissible to say that He is the speaker of every statement, nor the reporter of every report, nor is He the reporter and speaker, while what is other than Him is report and statement. This indicates that one is not the counterpart of the other, along with what they consider permissible: the act of each one is by a power that is an act of Allah, yet it is not permissible regarding the statement and report of everyone that it is the statement and report of Allah. And it is said to him: If He is not called 'mover' by what He moves other than Him, then also say that He is not called 'creator' by what He creates of the movement of other than Him; or if you differentiate between them by generality and specificity or whatever you wish, differentiate between them. Because the meaning by which He is named in the act of everything, and the meaning by which He is named 'creator' and 'speaker' differ; therefore they differ, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 840

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the analogy between a single act attributed to two agents and a single statement attributed to multiple speakers. Al-Maturidi first presents the objection: the opponent likens a single act by two agents to a single statement (qawl) or report (khabar) made by many. The Sheikh responds that in the witnessed world, a statement can indeed be attributed to a group or to multiple individuals (as in mutawatir reports), but this does not imply that the same applies to acts. If the witnessed world were a guide to the unseen, then one would have to distinguish between act and speech in the unseen just as in the witnessed. The opponent's analogy fails because Allah is called the creator of everything, but He is not called the speaker of every statement or the reporter of every report. Moreover, the Mu'tazila themselves hold that every human act is done by a power that is an act of Allah, yet they do not say that every human statement is Allah's statement. Al-Maturidi then offers a further refutation: if God is not called 'mover' by the movement He creates in others, then He should also not be called 'creator' by the movement He creates in others—unless a distinction is made. Ultimately, the meanings (ma'ani) by which God is named 'creator' and 'speaker' are different, so the analogy does not hold.

Key Terms
خالق
قائل
مخبر
متحرك
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since Allah is creator of all acts, He must also be the speaker of all statements, by analogy.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that the divine names 'creator' and 'speaker' are based on different meanings, so the analogy does not hold.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Maturidi say about the opponent's own position regarding human acts and statements?
A) They hold that human acts and statements are both from Allah.
B) They hold that human acts are from Allah's power but human statements are not from Allah.
C) They hold that human acts are from human power, not Allah's.
D) They hold that human statements are from Allah's power but acts are not.

Key Takeaway
Allah's being creator is not analogous to being a speaker; the divine names are based on distinct meanings, so the opponent's analogy is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God's attributes are all analogous to each other or to human attributes? How does this lesson's demonstration of asymmetry between Creator and Speaker challenge that assumption, and what does it demand of your understanding of tawhid?
?
When you speak, do you consider that your speech is not God's speech, even though He creates it? How does this distinction affect your sense of responsibility for your words, knowing they are truly yours?
?
If the meaning of 'Creator' is unique and not transferable to other attributes, what does that imply about how you should approach learning and contemplating the divine names? What step will you take today to deepen your awe of Allah's uniqueness?

Why the Magians are Better than the Qadariyya in Their View of Evil
Maturidi compares the statements of Magians and Qadariyya, arguing Magians are superior in exalting God from evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Magians' dualistic view is superior to the Qadariyya's denial of God's creation of evil, because the Magians at least affirm God's goodness while the Qadariyya inadvertently deny God's creation of good.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya's attempt to protect God from evil leads them to deny His creation of good.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages in a comparative theological critique. He argues that the Magians (dualists who posit a source of evil separate from God) are actually better than the Qadariyya (who deny God's creation of evil) when you examine their statements closely. Why? Because the Magians at least exalt God from evil and ascribe good to Him, while the Qadariyya, in their effort to distance God from evil, end up nullifying His creation of good deeds as well. Maturidi then exposes their inconsistency through a vivid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the comparison
Read the passage and note how Maturidi compares the Magians and Qadariyya. What is his initial verdict?
Focus on 'قول المجوس خيرا من قول القدرية'
2
Identify the Qadariyya's error
See how they 'divert the verse' and nullify God's creation of good. Why does Maturidi say they are worse?
They deny God's creation of both evil and good.
3
Examine the evasion analogy
Maturidi illustrates their inconsistency with the example of someone who says 'God is everywhere' but refuses to say He is in filthy places. Discuss the parallel.
This shows the Qadariyya's principle is self-contradictory.
4
Reflect on the theological point
Why does Maturidi say the Magians are better? What does this teach about attributing evil to God?
It underscores the importance of consistency in tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كان قول المجوس خيرا من قول القدرية عند التحصيل؛ لأنهم نزهوا الله عز وجل عن قول الشر وما يذم الفاعل عليه وحققوا له فعل الخير وما يحمد عليه، ثم القدرية بالوجه الذي أنكر المجوس صرفوا الآية عن المفهوم تسترا، وأبطلوا عنه أيضا خلق كل شيء يحمد عليه من الخيرات، ونسأل الله العصمة. ثم من حيدهم أن سئل عن حكم الآية فأعرض عن ذلك واشتغل في الإجابة عن نوع الأفعال، وحقيقته أن يقول به في الجملة، وعند التفسير فيما يقبح لا يقول كما يقول: الله في كل مكان، فإذا سئل عنه في الحشوش والأمكنة القذرة أبى ذلك، ثم هو رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء، ثم عند التفسير فيما يقبح يأبى إلا أنه لما يصير بحيث ذلك الإطلاق يدفع أصله، فمثله ما نحن فيه، والله الموفق.
Then the statement of the Magians was better than the statement of the Qadariyya upon examination; because they (Magians) exalted God Almighty from the statement of evil and what the doer is blamed for, and they affirmed for Him the act of good and what He is praised for. Then the Qadariyya, in the aspect that the Magians denied, diverted the verse from its understood meaning as a pretext, and also nullified for Him the creation of everything that is praised of good deeds. And we ask God for protection. Then among their evasion is that if one is asked about the ruling of the verse, he turns away from that and occupies himself with answering about the type of actions, and its reality is that he affirms it in general, but in interpretation regarding what is ugly he does not say like he says: 'God is in every place.' So if he is asked about Him in latrines and filthy places, he refuses that. Then He is the Lord of everything and God of everything, but in interpretation regarding what is ugly he refuses, except that when it comes to that unrestricted affirmation, it contradicts his principle. So similar is what we are in. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 846

Commentary
This passage is a comparative argument. Maturidi compares the statement of the Magians (dualists) with that of the Qadariyya (a sect that denies God's creation of evil). He says the Magians' statement is actually better upon analysis because they at least exalt God from evil and ascribe good to Him, while the Qadariyya, in their attempt to deny God's creation of evil, also end up denying His creation of good deeds. They do this by misinterpreting the verse to exclude evil from God's creation, but in doing so they nullify God's creation of all good that is praised. Then Maturidi points out their evasive behavior: when questioned about the ruling of the verse, they avoid direct answer and talk about types of actions. They affirm God's creation in general but when it comes to specific ugly things they refuse to attribute them to God, similar to someone who says 'God is everywhere' but then refuses to say He is in filthy places. This inconsistency shows their principle is self-contradictory.

المجوس
القدرية
الشر
الخير
تسترا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya are correct to deny God's creation of evil, while the Magians are completely wrong.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the Magians' view is better because they at least affirm God's goodness, while the Qadariyya's denial leads to nullifying God's creation of good and logical inconsistency.

Checkpoint

A) The Magians are monotheists
B) The Magians affirm God's creation of evil while Qadariyya deny it
C) The Magians exalt God from evil and ascribe good to Him, while Qadariyya also deny God's creation of good
D) The Magians are more rational

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya's attempt to protect God from evil leads them to inadvertently deny His creation of good and to logical contradictions, making them worse than the Magians in this respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself, like the Qadariyya, subtly denying God's hand in certain negative events in your life because you think it protects His honor? What would it mean to affirm His lordship over even those difficult moments while still taking responsibility for your own choices?
?
Maturidi points out that the Qadariyya are inconsistent—they affirm God's creation in general but deny it for specific 'ugly' things. Where in your own life do you hold a similar double standard, attributing good to God but distancing Him from the messy details?
?
The Magians, despite their dualism, are praised for consistently attributing good to God. Is there any area of your life where you hesitate to credit God fully for a good outcome, perhaps because you feel you 'earned' it? How would acknowledging Him as the sole source of all good change your gratitude and humility?

The Writer and Painter Analogy: Proving God's Unique Creation
Al-Maturidi uses the writer/painter analogy to prove God's exclusive creation and refute Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Maturidi uses the writer/painter analogy to demonstrate that human inability to produce perfect copies proves all creation is from God alone, refuting Qadariyya claims of independent human power.

Core Idea
Human inability to replicate proves the first creation is from God.

Al-Maturidi presents a profound argument from everyday experience: a writer or painter cannot produce an identical copy of their work. This inability, he argues, is not due to lack of skill but reveals that the original was created by God, not by human power alone. The passage then engages with objections from the Qadariyya concerning causes and capability, ultimately showing that human action is dependent on divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and literal translation. Notice the dialectical structure: argument, objection, reply. Pay attention to key terms like 'causes' (أسباب) and 'power' (القدرة).
The passage is dense; take notes on each exchange.
2
Identify the core analogy
Understand the analogy: a writer or painter cannot produce a perfect copy of their own work. Why does this prove that the original was not produced by them?
The key point is that if the human had full control, they could replicate exactly.
3
Follow the objections and replies
Map out the sequence: Qadariyya objection (why not produce like?), reply (causes never align), further objection (does inability imply another maker?), reply (if second is better, first is his), final objection (what prevents?), reply (tools, treatment, thought are not equal and are from God).
This dialectic refutes the idea of independent human power.
4
Connect to Qadariyya refutation
See how the entire argument undermines Qadariyya claims: if humans had full power, they could replicate perfectly; since they cannot, their power is limited and dependent on God who alone creates.
The conclusion: 'this shows that it proceeds contrary to your estimation'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : واحتج بالكاتب والمصوّر إنه لو أراد أن يخرج الثاني على ما عليه الأول لم يمكنه ، دل أن الأول لم يخرج على ذلك به ، فزعم أولا أنه يجوز أن يكون كذلك ما ألقاه الله فيه تلك القدرة. فإن قيل : لم لا يأتي بمثله ؟ فأجاب بأنا وإن كنا نفعل بتلك القدرة نفعله بأسباب لا تجتمع بكليتها حتى لا يخرج منها شيء من نحو الذهن والحفظ وأنواع الأشغال . قال : ولو وجب بهذا محدث آخر ليجب به مصوّر آخر . وعارض بالفعل : إنه لم يدل العجز على أنا نفعل ، ولو كان العجز يدل على ما ذكروا كان الثاني إذا كان أحسن ، فدل أن الأول له . ثم عارض أنه ما يمنعه ؟ فقال : لأنا نفعل بآلة وقدرة وعلاج وفكر ولا مستوى هذه ، ولو استوت أمكننا ذلك . فيقال له : الوجوه التي تمنعك فعلك أو لا ، فإن قال : لا ، أعظم القول أن علاجه وفكره ونحو ذلك الله ، وهو الذي أنكر خلق ذلك ، فقد أقر به . وإن قال : بلى قيل : السؤال عن ذلك كله أن القدرية زعمت أن الله لو أبقاها ، وقد أبقاها للفعل وكل ذلك أفعال لها القدرة [عليها] ، فما بالها لم تستو ، وقد قصدت أن تستوى ، وكانت لك القدرة ، فهذا يبين أنه على غير تقديرك تخرج. وما ذكر من المعنى هو الدليل الواضح
He said: And he argued by the writer and the painter: If he wanted to produce a second [copy] exactly like the first, he could not. This indicates that the first was not produced by him. So he claimed first that it is possible that it be so, what God cast into him of that power? If it is said: Why does he not produce its like? He answered: Even if we act with that power, we do it through causes that do not come together in their entirety so that nothing from them emerges, such as the mind, memory, and types of occupations. He said: And if by this another maker were necessitous, then another painter would be necessitous. And he objected by action: It did not indicate incapability that we act, and if incapability indicated what they mentioned, then if the second were better, it would indicate that the first is his. Then he objected: What prevents him? He said: Because we act with tools, power, treatment, thought, and these are not equal, and if they were equal, we could do that. So it is said to him: The aspects that prevent your action or not? If he says: No, the speech is greater: that his treatment, thought, and so on are from Allah, and he who denied the creation of that, has admitted it. And if he says: Yes, it is said: The question about all that is that the Qadariyya claimed that if Allah left them, and He left them for action, and all that are actions for which they have power, why then are they not equal, when they intended to be equal, and you had power? This shows that it proceeds contrary to your estimation. And what was mentioned of the meaning is the clear proof.
Source pages p. 851

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument using the analogy of a writer or painter who cannot produce a perfect copy of his work. The inability to replicate perfectly shows that the first work was not produced solely by the human. The author discusses objections and replies concerning causes, capability, and the role of God. It refutes the Qadariyya who claim humans have independent power, arguing that if humans had full power, they could replicate perfectly, but they cannot, so the true creator is God.

Correction: The text argues that even with full intention and power, perfect replication is impossible because the original creation involved divine factors beyond human control, proving that God is the true creator.

?
Al-Maturidi argues that the inability to produce a perfect copy is not a failure but a clear proof of God's unique creation. Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Claim on Prohibition and Power
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that prohibition alone does not prevent action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite distinction between prohibition and loss of power, arguing that both equally prevent action.

Core Idea
Prohibition itself makes action impossible, just as loss of power does.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to a key claim of the Mu'tazila regarding divine prohibition and human power. They argued that a prohibited action becomes impossible only when power is lost, not because of the prohibition itself. Maturidi counters by equating the effect of prohibition and loss of power, and emphasizing that action cannot occur while prohibition is in place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The passage begins by stating what the Mu'tazila claimed. Identify that claim: that a prohibited action is made impossible only by loss of power, not by the prohibition itself.
The phrase 'لا بفوت القدرة يقع له الفعل' indicates their position.
3
Understand Maturidi's counter
Maturidi argues that loss of power and prohibition are one in rendering action impossible. He also states that action cannot occur while prohibition is in place. Reflect on why this refutes the Mu'tazilite distinction.
The key phrase: 'وفوت القدرة في إحالة الفعل معه واحد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر مما زعم جماعة المعتزلة أن الممنوع لا بفوت القدرة يقع له الفعل مع الإطلاق ، فما أنكروا ذلك بفوت القدرة والمنع ، وفوت القدرة في إحالة الفعل معه واحد ، مع ما لا يجوز وجود الفعل في حال وقوع المنع بحال ، ويجوز فقد القدرة بما تقدم من القدرة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And another aspect of what a group of the Mu'tazila claimed: that the prohibited one, by the loss of power, does not have action occur from him absolutely. So they did not deny that by loss of power and prohibition, and loss of power in rendering action impossible is one with it, along with the fact that the occurrence of action in the state of the occurrence of prohibition is not permissible at all, and it is permissible to lose power by what preceded of power, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 861

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the Mu'tazilite position that a prohibited action becomes impossible only when power is lost, not because of the prohibition itself. The author counters that both loss of power (fawt al-qudra) and prohibition (man') equally render action impossible; they are the same in effect. Furthermore, it is impossible for an action to occur while prohibition is in place. The loss of power can occur due to a previously existing power (i.e., God could remove power). Thus, the Mu'tazilite distinction between prohibition and loss of power is invalid; both prevent action.

Key Terms
منع (man')
فوت القدرة (fawt al-qudra)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
فعل (fi'l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prohibition alone does not make an action impossible; only loss of power does.
Correction: Maturidi argues that prohibition itself renders action impossible, just as loss of power does, and that both are one in effect.

Checkpoint
How does the author equate prohibition and loss of power?
A) Both are causes of action.
B) Both make action impossible equally.
C) Both are eternal.
D) Both are chosen by the agent.

Key Takeaway
Prohibition is not merely a label; it actually makes the action impossible, just as loss of power does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God's prohibitions as mere suggestions, thinking you can still act if you have the power? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed that the prohibition itself makes the sin impossible in a real sense?
?

?
If Allah's prohibition alone is enough to prevent action, what is really holding you back from obedience? Is it your lack of true belief in the effectiveness of His command, or are you still clinging to your own strength?

The Second Dispute with al-Husayn: Merely Terminological
Maturidi dismisses a dispute as terminological and states his own principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the disagreement between Maturidi and al-Husayn over the term 'power' (quwwa) is merely terminological, and that Maturidi's principle asserts that no act can exist without its doer having power over it.

Core Idea
A dispute about mere names cannot affect the underlying reality.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a second point of contention with al-Husayn. He argues that the dispute is essentially meaningless because both sides agree on every substantive point except for the label 'power' (quwwa). He then provides his own school's foundational principle: the existence of an act without power on the doer's part would nullify the act itself. This clarifies why the terminological disagreement is irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the core disagreement
Find what al-Husayn says and what the Mu'tazila agree to. Al-Husayn says the disbeliever has everything except infallibility and success; they all agree the disbeliever lacks these.
The only difference is whether to call that thing 'power' or not.
3
Notice the terminological point
Maturidi declares the dispute 'meaningless' because it's only about naming. Recognise that theology sometimes has such verbal disputes.
This is a key insight: not all disagreements are real.
4
Absorb Maturidi's principle
He states that an act without power from the doer nullifies the act. Also, an act cannot issue from one ignorant of it. This grounds the necessity of divine address based on knowledge.
This principle underlies the school's position on human capacity.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this relates to the qadar debate: human power and divine address. Consider why Maturidi finds the terminological dispute irrelevant in light of his principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المسألة الثانية عندهم بينهم وبين الحسين لا معنى لها ؛ لأن الحسين يقول : كل شيء يكون به فعل الطاعة كان مع الكافر سوى العصمة والتوفيق . وهم وافقوه بأنه لا يوصف بعصمة ولا توفيق ، فحصل اختلافهم على تسميته " قوة أو لا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
ثم الأصل عندنا في المسألة وجود الفعل ولا قوة لمن له الفعل عليه يبطل معنى الفعل ويصرفه إلى غيره ، وكذلك وجود الفعل ممن هو جاهل به ، وهو غير جائز ، ثم كان الخطاب لازما بسبب العلم ، وإن لم يكن حقيقته مما لو طلب
Then the second issue between them and al-Ḥusayn is meaningless; because al-Ḥusayn says: everything by which the act of obedience occurs, it was with the disbeliever except for infallibility and success. And they agreed with him that he is not described with infallibility nor success. So their disagreement came down to naming it 'power' or not. And there is no power except by Allah. Then the principle according to us in the issue is: the existence of the act without power for the one who does the act upon it nullifies the meaning of the act and diverts it to another. Likewise, the existence of the act from one who is ignorant of it is not permissible. Then the address becomes necessary due to knowledge, even if its reality is not such that...
Source pages p. 864

Commentary
This passage addresses a second issue between the author and al-Ḥusayn (likely al-Ḥusayn al-Najjār). The author declares that this issue is meaningless because both sides agree on the substance. Al-Ḥusayn says that everything by which an act of obedience occurs is present with the disbeliever except for infallibility (ʿiṣma) and success (tawfīq). The Muʿtazila agree that the disbeliever is not described with infallibility or success. The only disagreement is terminological: whether to call that thing 'power' (quwwa) or not. The author then states the foundational principle of his own school: the existence of an act without any power on the part of the doer nullifies the meaning of the act and transfers it to something else. Similarly, an act cannot issue from one who is ignorant of it. Therefore, the divine address (khiṭāb) becomes necessary only when there is knowledge, even if its full reality is not... (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
المسألة الثانية
عصمة
توفيق
قوة
خطاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the disagreement with al-Husayn is a substantive theological difference.
Correction: Maturidi explains it is merely terminological; both parties agree on the underlying reality.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the disagreement regarding calling something 'power' (quwwa) or not?
A) It is a fundamental doctrinal difference.
B) It is meaningless because both parties agree on the substance.
C) It proves that the disbeliever has no power at all.
D) It shows that power is the same as infallibility.

Key Takeaway
The dispute with al-Husayn over 'power' is merely terminological, and Maturidi's principle affirms that acts require power from the doer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself debating labels with others—whether in religion, politics, or personal relationships—are you more concerned with winning a verbal argument or understanding the reality behind the words? What would it cost you today to drop a terminological dispute and focus on what both sides actually agree on?
?
Consider an area of your life where you attribute your success to your own ability. According to this lesson, true power comes only from Allah. Can you identify one success you once credited to yourself and now see as a gift of tawfiq? How can you daily remind yourself that 'no power except by Allah'?
?
Al-Maturidi says that an act without power from the doer nullifies the act. Do you ever perform religious acts—prayer, charity, fasting—while feeling powerless or disconnected, relying on routine rather than seeking Allah's enabling? What would it mean for you to refuse to perform an act of worship until you consciously seek power from Allah?

Al-Ka'bi's Objection on Power and Inaction: Maturidi's Refutation
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's objection that a powerful being could remain inactive indefinitely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaws in al-Ka'bi's argument that a powerful being could remain inactive for many moments, and Maturidi's response distinguishing divine and creaturely action.

Core Idea
God's power is not constrained by the contradictions that limit creaturely action.

This passage is a dense theological exchange. Al-Ka'bi challenges the notion that God can remain inactive for extended periods, analogizing to human power. Imam al-Maturidi responds by dissecting the question into two aspects and then pointing out a critical oversight regarding the nature of contradiction (tadādd) in divine versus creaturely actions. As you read, notice how Maturidi shifts the ground from temporal duration to the very nature of power and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic and translation of al-Ka'bi's objection and Maturidi's opening remark that he erred. Understand the claim: if a powerful being can be inactive for one moment, why not for many moments?
Al-Ka'bi is drawing an analogy from human power to divine power.
2
Identify the two aspects of the question
Maturidi says the question has two aspects. Find these two: first, that power is only for action, so if it can be without action for one time, it can for many. Second, the continuity of power across times; if power is absent at a later time, how can action occur then?
The first aspect is about the nature of power, the second about temporal continuity.
3
Understand the role of contradiction
Maturidi then introduces the concept of contradiction (tadādd). He contrasts beings upon whom actions are contradictory (creatures) and beings upon whom they are not (God). The objection fails because it assumes God's power is like human power, which is subject to contradiction.
For humans, action and inaction are contradictory at the same moment; for God they are not.
4
Reflect on the concluding point
The passage ends with a reference to the 'first state of the body' being empty of motion and rest. This is a further argument: if the body can initially be without motion or rest, why can't it remain so for many times? This undermines the objection's assumption.
The text is corrupt here, but the idea is that the opponent's own example can be used against him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبى : إن قال قائل : لو جاز أن يبقى القادر وقتا لا يفعل فيه لا جاز كذلك أوقاتا كثيرة ، كما يوصف بذلك الله تعالى قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : وقد أخطأ فى التقدير ، وإنما السؤال فيه من وجهين : أحدهما أن القدرة إذ ليست إلا للفعل ، وقد تخلوا عنه وقتا جاز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا أوقاتا ، وقد حققت هذا الوصف لله . والثانى أنه للوقت الثانى من وقت القدرة ليس بواجد لها ، وجاز الفعل بها ، ليم لا كان للوقت العاشر كذلك؟ وان لم يجدها ، أو إذ لم يجز الفعل بها بعد فنائها بأوقات وجب أن لا يجوز بوقت الأول أن الله كذلك بما لا تتضاد عليه الأفعال ويقدر على ما لا فأجاب عن أن يوجد أوقاتا غير ضده . فيقال : وما فما ذكرت ما قوبلت به ، بل قيل لك بل قيل لك : ما منع أن يكون من يتضاد عليه لا يجوز وجوده ، ولا فعل شيء أو ضده وقتا واحدا ، ومن في لا يتضاد عليه يجوز ، ثم يقال : للتضاد لا يجيز وقت القدرة أو له يوجب الوقت الثانى ، فأى الأمرين أجاب فهو فى الجالين واحد . وما قال على الله فهو فاسد : لما ليس عنده فعل الله غير خلقه، وهو متضاد كالموت والحياة ، وغير ذلك . ثم أجاب بأول أحوال الجسم أنه يخلو عن الحركة والسكون ليم لم يجب به خلاؤه عنها أوقاتا ؟
Then al-Ka'bi said: If someone says: If it is permissible for the powerful to remain for a time not acting, then it would be permissible similarly for many times, just as God is described with that. Al-Faqih Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: He erred in the estimation. Rather the question is from two aspects: One is that power is only for action, and if they abandon it for one time, it is permissible to abandon it for times, and I have verified this description for God. The second is that for the second time from the time of power, he does not have it, and action is permissible with it, why then not for the tenth time similarly? And if he does not have it, or if action with it is not permissible after its expiry by times, then it is necessary that it not be permissible at the first time? That God is like that with that which actions are not contradictory upon Him and He has power over what does not... So he answered by that He exists times other than its opposite. Then it is said: What? What you mentioned is not answered, rather it was said to you: What prevents that one upon whom contradiction applies should not be permissible in existence, and neither action of a thing nor its opposite at one time, and one upon whom contradiction does not apply is permissible? Then it is said: Contradiction does not permit the time of power, or it necessitates the second time. Whichever of the two he answers, it is the same in both cases? And what he said about God is corrupt: because for him, the action of God is none other than His creation, and it is contradictory like death and life and other than that. Then he answered with the first state of the body that it is empty of motion and rest, why not then answer with its being empty of them for times?
Source pages p. 876

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. Al-Ka'bi raises an argument: if a powerful being (qādir) can remain without acting for one moment, then it can remain inactive for many moments—just as God is described as sometimes not acting. Al-Maturidi responds that al-Ka'bi's estimation is mistaken. The question involves two aspects: first, since power exists only for action (faʿl), if it can exist without action for one moment, it could for many; and this is actually true of God. Second, regarding the continuity of power over time: if power does not exist at a later moment, how can action occur then? And if it cannot act after the power is gone, then it should also be unable at the first moment. The argument then shifts to the concept of contradiction (taḍādd) in actions. Al-Maturidi points out that for God, actions are not contradictory (lā tataḍāddu ʿalayhi al-afʿāl), whereas for creatures they are. The objection that God's power could be inactive for many moments fails because it does not account for the difference between God and creatures regarding the possibility of simultaneous contradictory actions. The response ends with a reference to the first state of the body being empty of motion and rest, which the opponent should have addressed.

Key Terms
الكعبى
القدرة
الفعل
التضاد
أول أحوال الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is analogous to human power, so if God can be inactive for one moment, He can be inactive for many moments.
Correction: God's actions are not subject to the contradictions that limit human actions; therefore, the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's response, what is the fundamental difference between God and creatures regarding actions?
A) God's actions are eternal, while creatures' actions are temporal.
B) God can act without power, while creatures cannot.
C) God's actions are not subject to contradiction, while creatures' actions are.
D) God always acts, while creatures can remain inactive.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's objection by showing that the analogy between divine and human power fails because God's actions are free from the contradictions inherent in creaturely action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ka'bi assumed that if God can be inactive for one moment, He can be inactive for many—reducing God to human limitations. In what area of your life have you assumed that God's power follows the same limits as yours? What would it mean for you to truly believe that His actions are not held back by the contradictions that frustrate you? Write down one situation where you have been limiting God and commit to praying about it tonight.
?
Maturidi says that for God, actions are not contradictory. This means God can bring both mercy and justice, creation and destruction, without conflict. Where in your heart do you doubt God's consistency because you project your own contradictory desires onto Him? Repent for any bitterness or accusation against God's character and ask Him to show you His non-contradictory nature.
?
The objection from al-Ka'bi is based on an analogy from creatures to God. When have you used human logic to tell God what He can or cannot do in your life? Name a specific prayer request you have given up on because it seemed impossible by human standards. Will you today ask God again, trusting that His power is not constrained by the contradictions of creaturely existence? Make a firm resolve to bring that request before Him in sincere du'a.

First Response: Claim of Divine Command
First of Maturidi's three responses to the polytheists' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response: the polytheists' argument is a false claim of divine command, similar to their past distortions.

Core Idea
The polytheists twist divine will into a false command.

Imam al-Maturidi begins his refutation by exposing the polytheists' tactic. They cite divine will as if it were a command from God, but this is a distortion akin to their earlier justifications for immorality. Let's examine his first response and its Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi frames the first response.
2
Identify the Quranic parallels
Maturidi quotes two Quranic verses. Understand how they show the polytheists' claim of divine command is false.
The verses highlight distortion of scripture.
3
Reflect on the fallacy
Recognize that the polytheists are not truly appealing to God's will but are fabricating a command. This undermines their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من احتج منهم بقوله: {سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا لَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا}، فالجواب لذلك من أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها أنهم ادعوا به الأمر كقوله: {وَإِذَا فَعَلُوا فَاحِشَةً قَالُوا وَجَدْنَا عَلَيْهَا آبَاءَنَا وَاللهُ أَمَرَنَا بِهَا}، وكذلك قوله: {وَإِنَّ مِنْهُمْ لَفَرِيقًا يَلْوُونَ أَلْسِنَتَهُمْ بِالْكِتَابِ}. والثاني أنهم لما أوعدوا في ذلك قلة أمهلوا فلما أمهلوا ظنوا كذب الرسل وحسبوا أن ذلك مما لله فيه الرضا وإلا لم يكن يمهلهم، وكذلك ظن أصحاب السبت، وذلك كقوله [تعالى]: {حَتَّى إِذَا اسْتَيْأَسَ الرُّسُلُ}. والثالث أن يكونوا قالوه على الاستهزاء بالمؤمنين بما يدعون أن كل شيء بمشيئة الله كقول الإنسان: إذا ما مت لسوف أخرج حيا، إنه قال ذلك على الاستهزاء بالمؤمنين، وإن كان ذلك حقا، وكذلك قول المنافقين: نشهد أنك لرسول الله، ولكن ذلك لما كان الهزو طعنوا به، فمثله الأول، والله أعلم.
Then among those who argue using His saying: {Those who associated partners would say, 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated...'}, so the answer to that is from three aspects: One of them is that they claimed by it a command, like His saying: {And when they commit an immorality, they say, 'We found our fathers upon it, and Allah has commanded us to do it.'}, and likewise His saying: {And indeed, among them is a party who distort their tongues with the Book.} The second is that when they were threatened in that matter, they were given respite; so when they were given respite, they thought the messengers were lying and assumed that God was pleased with that, otherwise He would not have given them respite. Similarly the people of the Sabbath thought. That is like His saying [Exalted]: {Until when the messengers despaired...} The third is that they said it out of mockery towards the believers, due to their claim that everything is by God's will, like a man's saying: 'When I die, I will come out alive' — he said that to mock the believers, even if it is true. Similarly the saying of the hypocrites: 'We bear witness that you are the Messenger of God' — but when it was mockery, they attacked by it. So the first is similar to it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 901

Commentary
This passage refutes the polytheists' argument that their shirk was by God's will, using three distinct answers. The author first states that the polytheists cite the verse from Surah al-An'am (6:148) as a proof. Then he presents three responses. The first response is that the polytheists are claiming that their statement is a command from God, similar to when they said 'Allah commanded us' regarding immoral acts, which is a distortion of divine command. The second response is that the delay of punishment led them to think God was pleased with their actions, but this is a misinterpretation of God's respite, as seen with the people of the Sabbath. The third response is that they said this mockingly, like a person who says he will be resurrected after death out of ridicule, even if the statement itself is true. Thus the polytheists' use of the verse is invalid.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
ادعاء (claim)
تحريف (distortion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that citing divine will for one's actions is like claiming a divine command.
Correction: Maturidi shows this is a distortion; divine will does not imply divine approval or command.

Checkpoint
What is the first response of Maturidi to the polytheists?
A) They were mocking the believers.
B) They claimed their action was commanded by God.
C) They mistook respite for approval.
D) They misquoted the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The polytheists' argument from divine will is exposed as a false claim of divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any actions in your life that you justify by saying 'Allah commanded' or 'this is Allah's will' when deep down you know it is your own desire? How can you distinguish between true divine command and self-justification?
?
When you read the verse about distorting tongues with the Book, does it remind you of any ways you might twist the meaning of Quranic verses to fit your own preferences rather than submitting to what they actually say? What is one verse you have done that with?
?
The polytheists used the claim of divine command to continue in sin. Have you ever used a similar excuse to avoid changing a bad habit? What would it cost you to honestly submit to Allah's command without distortion?

Second Response: Respite Mistaken for Approval
Second of Maturidi's three responses to the polytheists' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second response: the polytheists mistakenly interpreted God's respite as approval of their actions.

Core Idea
Delay of punishment is not a sign of divine pleasure.

The polytheists thought that because God did not immediately punish them, He must be pleased with their shirk. Maturidi refutes this by pointing to the example of the people of the Sabbath and the Quranic verse about messengers despairing. Let's explore this second response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logic: threat, respite, then mistaken assumption.
2
Understand the error
The polytheists wrongly concluded that respite implies approval. Maturidi uses the example of the Sabbath-breakers to illustrate.
Respite is a trial, not a sign of pleasure.
3
Connect to the Quranic reference
The verse {Until when the messengers despaired...} shows that delay can lead to false hope.

Source Text

Similarly the people of the Sabbath thought. That is like His saying [Exalted]: {Until when the messengers despaired...} The third is that they said it out of mockery towards the believers, due to their claim that everything is by God's will, like a man's saying: 'When I die, I will come out alive' — he said that to mock the believers, even if it is true. Similarly the saying of the hypocrites: 'We bear witness that you are the Messenger of God' — but when it was mockery, they attacked by it. So the first is similar to it. And God knows best.

Commentary
This passage refutes the polytheists' argument that their shirk was by God's will, using three distinct answers. The author first states that the polytheists cite the verse from Surah al-An'am (6:148) as a proof. Then he presents three responses. The first response is that the polytheists are claiming that their statement is a command from God, similar to when they said 'Allah commanded us' regarding immoral acts, which is a distortion of divine command. Thus the polytheists' use of the verse is invalid.

Third Response: Mockery Disguised as Truth
Third of Maturidi's three responses to the polytheists' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third response: the polytheists said this mockingly, even if the statement itself is true.

Core Idea
Truth can be spoken in mockery, invalidating the argument.

Maturidi's third response addresses the possibility that the polytheists' statement could be true in itself, but they said it to mock the believers. He gives an analogy of a man saying he will be resurrected mockingly, and the hypocrites' testimony. Let's see how this refutes their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the analogy and the comparison with hypocrites.
2
Understand the role of intention
The polytheists' intention was mockery, not belief. Even if the statement is true, it does not validate their position.
Mockery nullifies the argument.
3
Reflect on the broader principle
This shows that the context and intention matter in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من احتج منهم بقوله: {سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا لَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا}، فالجواب لذلك من أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها أنهم ادعوا به الأمر كقوله: {وَإِذَا فَعَلُوا فَاحِشَةً قَالُوا وَجَدْنَا عَلَيْهَا آبَاءَنَا وَاللهُ أَمَرَنَا بِهَا}، وكذلك قوله: {وَإِنَّ مِنْهُمْ لَفَرِيقًا يَلْوُونَ أَلْسِنَتَهُمْ بِالْكِتَابِ}. والثاني أنهم لما أوعدوا في ذلك قلة أمهلوا فلما أمهلوا ظنوا كذب الرسل وحسبوا أن ذلك مما لله فيه الرضا وإلا لم يكن يمهلهم، وكذلك ظن أصحاب السبت، وذلك كقوله [تعالى]: {حَتَّى إِذَا اسْتَيْأَسَ الرُّسُلُ}. والثالث أن يكونوا قالوه على الاستهزاء بالمؤمنين بما يدعون أن كل شيء بمشيئة الله كقول الإنسان: إذا ما مت لسوف أخرج حيا، إنه قال ذلك على الاستهزاء بالمؤمنين، وإن كان ذلك حقا، وكذلك قول المنافقين: نشهد أنك لرسول الله، ولكن ذلك لما كان الهزو طعنوا به، فمثله الأول، والله أعلم.
Then among those who argue using His saying: {Those who associated partners would say, 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated...'}, so the answer to that is from three aspects: One of them is that they claimed by it a command, like His saying: {And when they commit an immorality, they say, 'We found our fathers upon it, and Allah has commanded us to do it.'}, and likewise His saying: {And indeed, among them is a party who distort their tongues with the Book.} The second is that when they were threatened in that matter, they were given respite; so when they were given respite, they thought the messengers were lying and assumed that God was pleased with that, otherwise He would not have given them respite. Similarly the people of the Sabbath thought. That is like His saying [Exalted]: {Until when the messengers despaired...} The third is that they said it out of mockery towards the believers, due to their claim that everything is by God's will, like a man's saying: 'When I die, I will come out alive' — he said that to mock the believers, even if it is true. Similarly the saying of the hypocrites: 'We bear witness that you are the Messenger of God' — but when it was mockery, they attacked by it. So the first is similar to it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 901

Commentary
This passage refutes the polytheists' argument that their shirk was by God's will, using three distinct answers. The author first states that the polytheists cite the verse from Surah al-An'am (6:148) as a proof. Then he presents three responses. The first response is that the polytheists are claiming that their statement is a command from God, similar to when they said 'Allah commanded us' regarding immoral acts, which is a distortion of divine command. The second response is that the delay of punishment led them to think God was pleased with their actions, but this is a misinterpretation of God's respite, as seen with the people of the Sabbath. The third response is that they said this mockingly, like a person who says he will be resurrected after death out of ridicule, even if the statement itself is true. Thus the polytheists' use of the verse is invalid.

Key Terms
استهزاء (mockery)
هزو (ridicule)
نفاق (hypocrisy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a statement is true, it cannot be used to mock or deceive.
Correction: Maturidi shows that truth can be stated mockingly, and the intention invalidates the argument.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi give for the third response?
A) A person saying he will be resurrected mockingly.
B) The people of the Sabbath.
C) Those who distort scriptures.
D) The prophets who despaired.

Key Takeaway
Mockery can disguise truth; therefore, the polytheists' argument is not sincere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken a true Islamic statement—like 'Everything is by God's will' or the shahada—with a hint of mockery or sarcasm in your heart? How does this lesson expose the danger of that, and what will you do to guard your tongue and intention?
?
When you affirm God's decree or the truth of resurrection, is your heart sincere and humble, or could there be hidden pride or ridicule? This lesson shows that mockery invalidates even truth. Repent and commit to sincerity today.
?
Think of a time you used a true fact to belittle someone else's faith. How did that mockery poison the truth? Based on this passage, what must you change about how you speak truth to others?
