Refuting Al-Ka‘bi: Allah's Decree of Kufr is True and Just
Maturidi refutes al-Kaʻbī's claim that Allah does not decree kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi rejects al-Ka‘bi's claim that Allah cannot decree disbelief, and how the truth of a decree is independent of the nature of the decreed act.

Core Idea
A decree about falsehood is itself true; the nature of the decree is distinct from the nature of the decreed.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with a Mu‘tazili opponent, al-Ka‘bi, who argued that because disbelief (kufr) is false and contradictory, Allah—who only decrees truth—cannot decree it. Maturidi exposes the logical error: the decree of a false thing is a true and just decree, not itself false. He uses the example of a human judge to illustrate the point. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Ka‘bi's claim
Read the first sentence: 'Al-Kaʻbī claimed that Allah does not decree kufr.' Note that his denial is absolute, but Maturidi hints that it could be allowed in some aspects of qada'.
Al-Ka‘bi is a Mu‘tazili figure known for his rationalist theology.
2
Identify al-Ka‘bi's reasoning
He argues that kufr is mutafāwit (disparate/contradictory) and bāṭil (false), while Allah's decree is truth and correctness. Therefore, Allah cannot decree what is false.
This reasoning assumes that the decree must share the nature of the decreed.
3
Grasp Maturidi's counter-argument
Maturidi points out that the decree of falsehood is not itself false: 'the decree of falsehood is not false.' The truth or justice of a decree is independent of the thing decreed.
A decree can be true about a falsehood; e.g., 'This statement is false' is a true statement about falsehood.
4
Appreciate the analogy and conclusion
Maturidi uses judges: a judge's decree about an unjust act is itself just, not unjust. Ignorance of this basic distinction shows a lack of understanding of kalam.
The phrase 'wa lā quwwata illā billāh' emphasizes reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكعبى زعم أن الله لا يقضى الكفر، ثم فسر وجوه القضاء وجعلها في بعض ما فسر، فإنكاره في الجملة على احتمال ذلك في أحد الوجوه خطأ. ثم احتج بأن الكفر متفاوت وباطل، وقضاء الله حق وصواب لمن لا يعلم أن القضاء بالباطل إنه باطل، وبالمتفاوت إنه متفاوت عدل وحق، وكذا قضاء الحكام فأفعال الجود والظلم إنها جود غير باطل ولا متفاوت حتى كاد يعرفها الصبيان، فمن جهل ذلك، ثم ادعى حدود الكلام، فحق الكلام عليه أن يعرف ما الكلام، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Al-Kaʻbī claimed that Allah does not decree kufr (disbelief). Then he interpreted the aspects of qadā’ and placed them among some of what he interpreted. So his denial absolutely, considering the possibility of that [decree of kufr] in one of the aspects, is a mistake. Then he argued that kufr is differing/disparate (mutafāwit) and false (bāṭil), and that the decree of Allah is truth and correctness – he does not know that the decree of falsehood is itself false, and the decree of what is disparate is disparate. Justice and truth are like the decree of judges: the acts of generosity and injustice are [in themselves] generous, not false and not disparate, such that even children almost know that. So whoever is ignorant of that and then claims the boundaries of kalām, it is incumbent upon him to know what kalām is. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 906

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific theological opponent, al-Kaʻbī (a Muʻtazilī figure), who denied that Allah decrees kufr (disbelief). Maturidi begins by stating al-Kaʻbī's position: 'zаʻama anna Allāh lā yaqḍī al-kufr' – he claimed that Allah does not decree disbelief. Maturidi notes that al-Kaʻbī then interpreted the various aspects of qadā’ and placed the denial within some of them, but Maturidi judges that his absolute denial is an error because qadā’ has multiple aspects, one of which could include the decree of kufr. Then he presents al-Kaʻbī's argument: kufr is 'mutafāwit' (disparate/contradictory) and 'bāṭil' (false), and Allah's decree is truth and correctness, so it cannot involve falsehood. Maturidi counters by pointing out that this logic is flawed: the decree of a false thing is not itself false; it is a true decree about the false thing. Similarly, the decree of a disparate thing is not itself disparate but is just and true. He uses an analogy: a human judge's decree regarding unjust acts is itself just, not unjust. The wrongness is in the act, not in the decree itself. Maturidi ends with a sharp criticism: those who fail to grasp this distinction and yet claim expertise in kalām should first learn what kalām truly is. This underscores Maturidi's commitment to precise linguistic and logical analysis.

Key Terms
الكفر
القضاء
المتفاوت
الباطل
الحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah decrees kufr, His decree becomes false or unjust.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the decree itself is true and just; it is the decreed act that is false. The truth of the decree is independent.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main counter-argument to al-Kaʻbī's claim that Allah cannot decree kufr because it is false?
A) Allah does decree kufr because He is the creator of all things
B) The decree of a false thing is itself true and just, not false
C) Kufr is not actually false
D) Al-Kaʻbī misinterpreted the Quran

Key Takeaway
Allah's decree of disbelief is a true and just decree; the reality of the decreed act does not affect the truth of the decree itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or difficulty, do you ever complain that Allah’s decree is unfair? Reflect: does your feeling about the event determine whether the decree itself is just, or does the decree remain true and just regardless of your perception? What would it take for you to trust Allah’s decree even when you cannot see the wisdom behind it?
?
Al-Ka‘bī argued that Allah cannot decree kufr because that would make His decree false. Examine your own heart: have you ever assumed that something about Allah’s actions must align with your limited understanding of ‘good’? Are you willing to accept that His decree of something you consider ‘bad’ is still perfectly just? Commit to saying ‘قضاء الله حق’ (Allah’s decree is truth) even when it hurts.
?
Maturidi warns those who claim knowledge of kalām while lacking foundational understanding. Do you study religion more to argue and impress, or to know the truth and submit? How would your approach to learning change if you truly believed that your ignorance could lead you to misrepresent Allah? Take one concrete step this week to learn with humility rather than debate.

Allah's Decree of Disbelief: Refuting al-Ka'bi's Error
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that Allah does not decree disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ka'bi's denial that Allah decrees disbelief is an error, and how Maturidi uses the multiple meanings of qada' and an analogy to refute it.

Core Idea
Denying qada' of disbelief is a mistake if you don't understand its multiple aspects.

Maturidi directly engages a prominent Mu'tazilite view. Al-Ka'bi claimed that Allah does not decree disbelief. Maturidi argues that this denial is too simplistic because the term 'qada'' carries various meanings, and in one sense, it can apply to disbelief without contradicting God's justice. In this passage, he refutes al-Ka'bi by exposing the nuance in the concept of divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of argument.
Notice the repetition of the claim for emphasis.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Al-Ka'bi denies that Allah decrees disbelief. He also interprets aspects of qada' to support his denial.
Maturidi says his interpretation only covers some aspects.
3
Understand Maturidi's objection
Maturidi points out that the denial in general is an error because there is possibility in one of the aspects. He then shows that al-Ka'bi's reasoning is flawed by comparing divine decree to a judge's decree.
Focus on the phrase 'because of the possibility of that in one of the aspects.'
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider how a human judge's decree regarding generous or unjust acts is not itself unjust; similarly, Allah's decree of varied and false things is just and true because it corresponds to the reality of the act.
Even children understand this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكعبى زعم أن الله لا يقضى الكفر أن الله لا يقضى الكفر، ثم فسر وجوه القضاء وجعلها في بعض ما فسر، فإنكاره في الجملة على احتمال ذلك في أحد الوجوه خطأ. ثم احتج بأن الكفر متفاوت و باطل، وقضاء الله حق وصواب لمن لا يعلم أن القضاء بالباطل إنه باطل، وبالمتفاوت إنه متفاوت عدل وحق، وكذا قضاء الحكام فأفعال الجود والظلم إنها جود غير باطل ولا متفاوت حتى كاد يعرفها الصبيان، فمن جهل ذلك، ثم ادعى حدود الكلام، فحق الكلام عليه أن يعرف ما الكلام، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And al-Ka'bi claimed that God does not decree disbelief—that God does not decree disbelief. Then he interpreted the aspects of qada' and placed it in some of what he interpreted. So his denial in general is an error because of the possibility of that in one of the aspects. Then he argued that disbelief is varying and false, and God's decree is truth and right. For one who does not know that the decree with falsehood is false, and with variation is variation, justice and truth. Likewise the decree of rulers: the acts of generosity and injustice—they are generosity, not false nor varying, to the point that children almost know it. So whoever is ignorant of that, then claims the boundaries of speech (kalam), it is incumbent on him to know what speech is. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 906

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazilite) who denies that Allah decrees disbelief, repeating the claim for emphasis. Al-Ka'bi then interprets the various meanings of qada' and places his denial within some of those meanings. Maturidi points out that his outright denial is mistaken because it is possible that Allah decrees disbelief in one sense of qada'. Al-Ka'bi argues that disbelief is varying and false, whereas Allah's decree is truth and right. Maturidi counters that this reasoning fails because a decree with falsehood is itself false, and a decree with varying things is varying, yet it remains just and true from God—similar to how a human judge's decree regarding acts of generosity or injustice is not itself false or varying in its essence but corresponds to the act. Even children can grasp this distinction. Therefore, whoever is ignorant of these nuances and then claims to speak about the limits of kalam (theological discourse) should first understand what kalam actually means.

Key Terms
الكعبي
القضاء
الكفر
المتفاوت
الباطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that Allah decrees disbelief is a straightforward denial based on God's justice.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the term qada' has multiple meanings, and denying it in all senses is an error because it can apply to disbelief in one valid sense without contradicting justice.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider al-Ka'bi's denial that Allah decrees disbelief an error?
A) Because al-Ka'bi does not believe in qada' at all.
B) Because al-Ka'bi failed to consider that qada' has multiple aspects, and his denial may be valid in one sense but not generally.
C) Because al-Ka'bi uses a hadith incorrectly.
D) Because al-Ka'bi says disbelief is not created by God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that the term qada' has multiple aspects, and one cannot deny Allah's decree of disbelief in a blanket manner without considering those meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness widespread disbelief in the world, does your heart ever question whether Allah's decree is entirely just? How does Maturidi's distinction between the act of decreeing and the content of the decree challenge you to trust in Allah's wisdom even when you do not understand?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological truth because it seemed contradictory on the surface? Reflect on a time you rejected something about Allah's actions because it didn't fit your expectations. What would it cost you to submit to the full reality of qada' as Maturidi explains it?
?
Maturidi says that even children understand the difference between a judge's decree and the act decreed. Are you willing to admit that your intellectual pride may have blinded you to this simple truth? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you fully embraced that His decree of evil is just and true?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite View on Calamities
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistency in accepting calamities as from God unless compensated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazilite inconsistency regarding God's decree of diseases and calamities.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's selective acceptance of God's decree reveals a deeper inconsistency.

This passage from Imam Maturidi exposes a logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite position on calamities. While they reject that God decrees diseases and afflictions, they simultaneously affirm that eternal punishment in Hell is from His decree. Maturidi challenges them to be consistent or admit they seek a lord other than Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position
The text states: 'The Mu'tazila say: God does not decree diseases and calamities in religion; they have no sin except for compensation.' Understand what they claim.
3
Notice the inconsistency
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila themselves accept that eternal punishment in Hell, abandonment (khidhlan), and misguidance (idlal) are from God's decree. Ask: why do they accept these but not calamities?
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the hadith
The passage quotes a hadith meaning 'let him seek a lord other than Me' for those not pleased with God's decree. Consider what it means to be truly pleased with Allah's decree.
The hadith emphasizes complete submission.
5
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi implies that the Mu'tazila are inconsistent and thus not truly pleased with God's decree. The commentary clarifies that he has elsewhere explained how to be pleased with it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن حقيقة الخير في الأمراض والمصائب ألا بری أن التخليد في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة وكذلك الخذلان والإضلال ونحو ذلك، فليرضى الكعبي لنفسه ذلك، وإلا طلب ربا سواه. والمعتزلة يقولون: ليس لله القضاء بالأمراض والمصائب في الدين، لا ذنب لهم إلا بالعوض، فإذا لا يرضون بها حتى يعطوا عليها العوض، وذلك معنى ما روى فليتخذ ربا سواى. وقال: علينا الرضا بقضاء الله. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وقد بينا كيف يرضى به، وما عليه فى ذلك أيضا. ولا قوة إلا بالله
Moreover, the reality of good in diseases and calamities—do you not see that eternal abiding in the fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, likewise abandonment and misguidance and the like? So let al-Ka'bi be pleased with that for himself; otherwise he would seek a lord other than Him. And the Mu'tazila say: God does not decree diseases and calamities in religion; they have no sin except for compensation. So they are not pleased with them until they are given compensation for them. And that is the meaning of what is narrated: 'let him seek a lord other than Me.' And he said: 'It is incumbent upon us to be pleased with the decree of God.' The shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: And we have explained how to be pleased with it, and what is upon him in that also. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage attacks the Mu'tazilite position regarding calamities and diseases. Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila themselves consider eternal punishment in Hellfire as from God's decree, as well as abandonment (khidhlan) and misguidance (idlal). So if al-Ka'bi denies that God decrees diseases and calamities, let him be consistent and be pleased with those matters—otherwise he would be seeking another lord, as the hadith says. The Mu'tazila claim that God does not decree diseases and calamities in matters of religion, and that people only deserve compensation for them, so they are not content with them unless compensated. This contradicts the command to be pleased with God's decree. The shaykh (likely Maturidi) reiterates that they have already explained how to be pleased with God's decree.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الأمراض والمصائب
العوض
الخذلان
الإضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila consistently deny God's decree of evil, but they accept some evils like eternal punishment as from God.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are inconsistent: they accept some evils as from God but deny others, thus they are not truly pleased with God's decree.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite view on diseases and calamities according to this passage?
A) They are from God and must be accepted.
B) God does not decree them in religion, and people deserve compensation.
C) They are purely from human actions.
D) They are punishments for sin.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view on calamities is inconsistent because they accept some evils from God's decree but not others, contradicting the command to be pleased with all of God's decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When affliction comes, do you find yourself silently demanding compensation from God before you can be content? How does the Mu'tazilite attitude of 'not pleased without compensation' mirror your own heart when things don't go your way? Will you repent of this and submit unconditionally?
?
You easily accept that God's decree includes eternal punishment for disbelievers, yet you struggle when He decrees a minor illness for you. Is this not the very inconsistency Maturidi exposes? What does this reveal about your faith in God's wisdom?
?
The hadith says: 'Let him seek a lord other than Me.' If you are not pleased with God's decree, you are effectively seeking another lord. Can you honestly say you are pleased with whatever God decrees for you today—even if no compensation is offered?

The Qadariyya as Magians: Blame and Dualism
Maturidi explains the hadith condemning Qadariyya as Magians and details the shared dualistic doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basis for the prophetic condemnation of the Qadariyya as the Magians of this ummah, particularly the shared dualistic concept of good and evil creators.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya are blamed for adopting a dualism akin to Magian beliefs about good and evil.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi explains the well-known hadith that labels the Qadariyya as the Magians of the Muslim community. He shows that the blame is not merely rhetorical but stems from a doctrinal similarity: both groups assign the creation of good and evil to separate agents, undermining Allah's absolute power. This lesson unpacks the Magian myth and how the Mu'tazila inadvertently share in it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from consensus to hadith to explanation.
Pay attention to key terms like Qadariyya, Majus, and sharr.
2
Understand the Magian dualism
Identify the described Magian doctrine: Allah alone, then an evil thought leads to Iblis, who creates evil while Allah creates good. Neither has power over the other's domain.
This opposes the Islamic belief that Allah is the sole creator of both good and evil.
3
Link to the Mu'tazila
See how the Mu'tazila share in this dualism by denying that Allah creates evil and attributing it to human free will entirely.
This is why they were called Qadariyya, implying blame.

منهم مسألة في ذم القدرية - و قال الشيخ رحمه الله : أجمع أهل الكلام على ذم اسم القدرية ، وتبرأ كل عنه . وقد روى في ذلك عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ما يمكن السبيل إلى معرفة من له حقيقة هذا الان له حقيقة هذا الاسم وهو قوله : القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة . أنه أراد به ذم أهلها ، بمعنى شاركوا فيه المجوس فيما خالف به المجوس أهل الأديان من القول ، لا بد من تأمل ذلك ؛ ليظهر حقيقة أهل هذا الان الاسم و معلوم قوة إلا بالله . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وكان الأصل الذي ذم به المجوس مما خالفوا به أهل الأديان من أوجه : أحدها أنهم قالوا : كان الله يتحدا لا شريك له ثم حدثت منه فكرة ردية ، إما لما أصابته عينه ، أو لما ظن أن يكون له عدوا ينازعه ، فإذا ابليس حدث من تلك الفكرة الردية ، فخلق هو شر العالم ، والله خيره ، من غير أن كان لله قدرة على خلق شيء من الشر والفساد ونحو ذلك ، أو لإبليس قدرة على خلق شيء من الخير والصلاح فقام العالم بهما ، وبهذا كله خالفوا به أهل الأديان . ومعلوم أن هذا كله أوصاف ذم ونعوت شين . ثم للمعتزلة عن كل صفة من هذه الصفات نصيب ؛ فلذلك لقبوا باسم القدرية ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ووجه ذلك أن المعتزلة زعمت أن الله تعالى كان ولا شيء غيره ، ثم حدثت
From among them: A question on the blame of the Qadariyya. And the Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The people of kalām are agreed on the blame of the name 'Qadariyya,' and everyone disassociates from it. And it has been related from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, what guides to recognizing whoever truly deserves this name, namely his saying: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah.' He intended by it blame of its adherents, meaning that they share with the Magians in what the Magians opposed the people of religions in terms of doctrine. It is necessary to reflect on this to make clear the reality of the people of this name. And there is no power except by Allah. And the basis for which the Magians were blamed, by which they opposed the people of religions, is in several aspects: One of them is that they said: Allah was alone, no partner with Him, then there occurred from Him an evil thought, either because his eye afflicted him, or because he thought there would be an enemy contending with Him. So Iblis came into being from that evil thought, and he created the evil of the world, and Allah created its good, while Allah had no power to create any of the evil and corruption and the like, nor did Iblis have power to create any of the good and righteousness. So the world came to be through both of them. And by all this they opposed the people of religions. It is known that all these are attributes of blame and qualities of disgrace. Then the Mu'tazila have a share of each of these attributes; therefore they were given the name 'Qadariyya.' And there is no power except by Allah. And the reason for that is that the Mu'tazila claimed that Allah was, and nothing else besides Him, then there occurred...
Source pages p. 913

Commentary
This passage discusses the blame of the Qadariyya (fatalists/deniers of divine decree). It begins by stating scholarly consensus on condemning the name 'Qadariyya' and then cites the hadith: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah.' The author explains that this hadith blames them because they share in the Magian dualistic belief. He then elaborates the Magian doctrine: Allah was alone, then an evil thought occurred, leading to Iblis who created evil, while Allah created only good, with neither having power over the other's domain. This opposes monotheistic religions. The Mu'tazila, by denying that Allah creates evil and attributing evil to human free will entirely, share in this dualism, hence the name. The passage cuts off mid-sentence explaining the Mu'tazila claim, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
القدرية (Qadariyya)
مجوس (Magians)
فكرة ردية (evil thought)
شر العالم (evil of the world)
خير (good)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith condemns all Qadariyya as actual disbelievers or associates them with Magian religion wholesale.
Correction: The blame is specific: they share a dualistic concept about the creation of good and evil, not that they are Magians in faith.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the main similarity between the Qadariyya and the Magians that justifies the hadith's condemnation?
A) Both reject the prophethood of Muhammad.
B) Both believe in two separate creators for good and evil.
C) Both deny the existence of angels.
D) Both believe in reincarnation.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya are condemned because their view on evil creation parallels the Magian dualism, both denying Allah's exclusive sovereignty over all creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself blaming Allah for the evil in the world, or do you, like the Qadariyya, implicitly assume that some evil originates from sources other than Him? How would you refute that dualistic tendency in your own heart?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone creates everything, including evil, how does that affect your reaction to personal adversity? Will you submit or rebel, and what does that reveal about your tawheed?
?
The Magians and Qadariyya both tried to protect Allah's goodness by separating evil from His action. Are there areas in your life where you attribute your failures to anything other than your own choices and Allah's decree? How can you affirm His sole sovereignty over both good and evil in your daily trials?

Why the Qadariyya Are Called the Magians of This Ummah
Maturidi opens the chapter on blaming the Qadariyya and cites the hadith linking them to Magians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi equates the Mu'tazila (Qadariyya) with the Magians based on their shared dualistic view of good and evil.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya resemble the Magians in attributing evil to a source other than God.

Maturidi opens his chapter on the Qadariyya by noting a universal condemnation of the label. He then invokes a Prophetic hadith that calls them the Magians of this community, and deciphers what this means. By analyzing the Magian belief in two creators—one for good and one for evil—Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila share this dualistic tendency, thus earning the blameworthy name.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus
Start by reading the first sentence where Maturidi states that all scholars agree in condemning the label 'Qadariyya'. Notice how even those accused disavow it.
This sets the stage for defining who truly deserves the name.
2
Examine the hadith
Read the hadith: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.' Reflect on why the Prophet would compare them to Magians.
Maturidi says this hadith enables us to identify the real Qadariyya.
3
Understand Magian dualism
Maturidi describes the Magian belief: God created only good, an evil thought arose from Him (or from another origin), and Iblis created evil. Both are independent creators.
This is the key blameworthy attribute.
4
Connect to Mu'tazila
Maturidi asserts that the Mu'tazila have a share in each of those blameworthy attributes, particularly in denying God's power over evil.
Therefore they are rightly called Qadariyya.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how Maturidi uses the Magian benchmark to classify a theological group. What does this teach about the importance of attributing all actions to God?
This connects to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في ذم القدرية - قال الشيخ رحمه الله : أجمع أهل الكلام على ذم اسم القدرية، وتبرأ كل عنه . وقد روى في ذلك عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ما يمكن السبيل إلى معرفة من له حقيقة هذا الاسم وهو قوله : القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة . أنه أراد به ذم أهلها، بمعنى شاركوا فيه المجوس فيما خالف به المجوس أهل الأديان من القول، لا بد من تأمل ذلك؛ ليظهر حقيقة أهل هذا الاسم . وكان الأصل الذي ذم به المجوس مما خالفوا به أهل الأديان من أوجه : أحدها أنهم قالوا : كان الله يتحدا لا شريك له ثم حدثت منه فكرة ردية، إما لما أصابته عينه، أو لما ظن أن يكون له عدوا ينازعه، فإذا ابليس حدث من تلك الفكرة الردية، فخلق هو شر العالم، والله خيره، من غير أن كان لله قدرة على خلق شيء من الشر والفساد ونحو ذلك، أو لإبليس قدرة على خلق شيء من الخير والصلاح فقام العالم بهما، وبهذا كله خالفوا به أهل الأديان . ومعلوم أن هذا كله أوصاف ذم ونعوت شين . ثم للمعتزلة عن كل صفة من هذه الصفات نصيب؛ فلذلك لقبوا باسم القدرية ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
A question concerning the blame of the Qadariyya. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The people of kalām are agreed upon blaming the name Qadariyya, and each disavows it. It has been narrated on this matter from the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, that which enables the way to know who truly deserves this name, that is his saying: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.' He meant by it the blame of its people, meaning that they share with the Magians in what the Magians opposed the people of the [true] religions in terms of statement. It is necessary to reflect on this, so that the true nature of the people of this name becomes clear. The basis for which the Magians were blamed, in what they opposed the people of the [true] religions, is in aspects: one of them is that they said: God was unique with no partner, then an evil thought arose from Him — either because His eye afflicted him, or because He thought He might have an enemy to contend with Him. Then Iblīs arose from that evil thought, so he created the evil of the world, and God [created] its good, without God having any power to create anything of evil and corruption and the like, or Iblīs having power to create anything of good and righteousness. Thus the world arose from them both, and by all this they opposed the people of the [true] religions. It is known that all of these are attributes of blame and qualities of disgrace. Then the Muʿtazila have a share in every one of these attributes; therefore they were labelled with the name Qadariyya. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 913

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a new chapter in Kitāb al-Tawḥīd titled 'A Question Concerning the Blame of the Qadariyya.' The Shaykh (Imām al-Māturīdī) begins by noting a consensus among the people of kalām (dialectical theology) on condemning the label 'Qadariyya' (a group associated with free will), such that every group disassociates itself from it. He then cites the famous ḥadīth, 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community,' which he interprets as a means to identify who truly deserves this name. The key is to understand what aspect of the Magians is blameworthy so that those who share that aspect may be labelled similarly. He then explains the Magians' doctrine: they believe in two eternal principles — God (who creates good) and an evil thought that produces Iblīs, who creates evil. God is not powerful over evil, and Iblīs is not powerful over good. This dualism is opposed to the monotheistic religions. He then asserts that the Muʿtazila have a share in each of these blameworthy attributes, and thus they deservedly are called Qadariyya. The passage ends with the humble acknowledgment that all power is from God.

Key Terms
القدرية
المجوس
المعتزلة
فكرة ردية
ذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the label Qadariyya applies to all who discuss qadar or affirm human free will.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the label specifically applies to those who share the Magian dualistic view—attributing evil to a source other than God—like the Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the Mu'tazila deserve to be called Qadariyya?
A) Because they deny all forms of divine decree
B) Because they share in the Magians' blameworthy attributes, particularly in their dualistic view of good and evil
C) Because they reject the hadith about the Magians
D) Because they believe in two gods

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the Magian dualism as a benchmark to identify the Qadariyya, showing that the Mu'tazila are rightly called that because they too limit God's power over evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience suffering or witness evil, does your heart immediately recognize it as from Allah's decree, or do you find yourself subtly blaming people, circumstances, or shaytan as if they have independent power? How would your reaction change if you truly believed that Allah alone is the creator of both good and evil? This week, when something goes wrong, pause and say 'Allah created this' and see if it transforms your patience and trust.
?
The Magians believed that an evil thought arose from God, and from that came Iblis. Do you ever entertain thoughts about Allah that are beneath His majesty? For example, do you ever think that He is unjust, neglectful, or that He permits evil because He is weak? This lesson warns that even such subtle thoughts can align one with dualism. Examine your inner speech—are there any seeds of complaint against Allah's decree? Repent and affirm His perfect wisdom and power.
?
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila share in each blameworthy attribute of the Magians. Could it be that you, too, share in some of those attributes by the way you live? For instance, when you sin, do you blame your environment, your upbringing, or your nafs as if they created that sin apart from Allah's will? This lesson demands that you take full responsibility before Allah, while still affirming His absolute sovereignty. Will you, starting today, attribute every single event—good and bad—to Allah alone, and in doing so, cleanse your heart of every trace of dualism?

Mu'tazila's Originated Will vs Magians' Evil Thought
Maturidi compares the Mu'tazila's claim of an originated will to the Magians' evil thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazila by comparing their concept of an originated will to the Magians' evil thought, and why he considers the Mu'tazila worse.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila and Magians both posit a deficient originated entity to explain creation — only the name differs.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi exposes a striking parallel between the Mu'tazila and the Magians. He argues that both groups introduce a temporally originated entity into the divine plan, whether they call it 'will' or 'thought'. By showing that the difference is merely nominal, Maturidi undermines the Mu'tazila claim to a rational theology and associates them with dualistic tendencies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp Maturidi's argument.
Notice how the passage flows from the Mu'tazila claim to a comparison with the Magians.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Extract what the Mu'tazila claim about God, the will, and the origin of the world. How do they support their view with the Quran?
They say the will occurred (hadatha) without any prior attribute in God.
3
Identify the Magian position
What do the Magians believe? They call their originated entity 'thought' (fikra) and use it to explain evil.
The Magians limit this originated entity to part of the world.
4
Compare the two positions
According to Maturidi, what is the actual difference between the originated will and the originated thought?
He says the difference is in name only, not in reality.
5
Understand Maturidi's verdict
Why does Maturidi say both groups are under a blameworthy statement, and why are the Mu'tazila worse?
The Mu'tazila attribute the entire world to an originated entity, while the Magians limit it to evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه ذلك أن المعتزلة زعمت أن الله تعالى كان ولا شيء غيره، ثم حدثت الإرادة من غير أن كان من الله بحدوثها إرادة أو اختيار منه إليها معنى سوى أن كانت، فكان بها جميع العالم؛ إذ من قولهم : إن العالم فعل الله، وإنه كان باختيار، وإن الاختيار إرادة كقوله : فَعَالُ لِمَا يُرِيدُ، قسمت المعتزلة تلك الحادثة إرادة، والمجوس فكرة، وهى واحدة، بينهما اختلاف في الاسم لا الحقيقة. ثم جعلت المجوس بها يصف العالم، والمعتزلة كل العالم، فيكونان في الحاصل تحت قول ذميم، والمعتزلة زائدة.
The reason for that is that the Muʿtazila claimed that God, exalted, existed while nothing else existed, then will (irāda) occurred without there being from God, by its occurrence, any will or choice on His part toward it — a meaning other than that it was. So by it all the world came to be. For it is from their statement: indeed the world is the act of God, and it was by choice, and choice is will, as His saying: 'Doer of what He wills' (Quran 11:107, 85:16). The Muʿtazila designated that originated thing as will, and the Magians designated it as thought, but they are one; between them there is a difference in name, not in reality. Then the Magians by it described part of the world, while the Muʿtazila [described] the whole world, so both ultimately come under a blameworthy statement, and the Muʿtazila are [even] more excessive.
Source pages p. 914

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed parallel between the Muʿtazila and the Magians regarding the origin of the world. Maturidi explains that the Muʿtazila claim that God existed alone, and then will (irāda) occurred—a temporally originated entity—without any prior attribute of will or choice in God; it simply happened. Through this originated will, the entire world came into existence. The author points out that the Muʿtazila call this originated entity 'will' (irāda), while the Magians call it 'thought' (fikra), but in reality the two are identical, differing only in name. The Magians use this concept to explain only a part of the world (evil), whereas the Muʿtazila use it to explain the entire world. Therefore, both groups fall under a blameworthy position, but the Muʿtazila are worse because they attribute the entire creation to this deficient originated entity, whereas the Magians limited it to evil. The underlying critique is that positing an originated attribute in God that is not grounded in His eternal attributes resembles the Magian dualism, which undermines divine perfection.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الفكرة
حادثة
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'will' is necessarily an eternal attribute; Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for making it originated like the Magians' thought.
Correction: Maturidi shows that both groups posit a temporally originated entity, differing only in name, which undermines divine perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the main similarity Maturidi draws between the Mu'tazila and the Magians in this passage?
A) Both believe the world is eternal
B) Both posit a temporally originated entity in God that brings about the world, differing only in name (will vs. thought)
C) Both deny the existence of God
D) Both affirm that God has an eternal will

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazila by equating their originated will with the Magians' originated thought, highlighting that both positions are rationally deficient, with the Mu'tazila being more excessive because they apply it to the entire creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will, do you ever unconsciously imagine it as something that 'happens' or 'comes about' rather than being eternal and perfect? How does Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila make you reconsider the way you speak about God's attributes in your daily life?
?
The Magians blamed an originated thought for evil, while the Mu'tazila used an originated will for all creation. In your own struggles with understanding evil or suffering, have you ever subtly diminished God's sovereignty by attributing things to a force outside His eternal will? What would it take for you to fully trust in God's absolute perfection, even when you don't understand?
?
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila are 'more excessive' because they apply a deficient originated entity to the whole world. Is there an area of your life where you have relegated God's role to something limited or temporary — perhaps in your prayers, your trust, or your expectations? What step will you take today to affirm God's eternal and perfect involvement in every detail of your existence?
